What Is Kalam?
beginner
4 min
Definition of Tasawwur and Tasdiq
The author defines the two primary modes of knowledge: conception and assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent) as the two basic types of knowledge in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Knowledge is either bare conception or judgment.

This passage from the Talkhis al-Muhassal introduces the foundational distinction in Islamic epistemology. Before any argument can be made, a learner must understand the difference between simply conceiving an idea and affirming or denying it. This distinction underpins all subsequent discussions in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal English translation. Notice how the definition is framed as part of the first introduction on primary sciences.
2
Identify the two types
Identify what tasawwur and tasdiq have in common (both are perceptions) and how they differ (absence vs. presence of judgment).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أركان علم الكلام
الركن الأول في المقدمات
علم الكلام مرتب على أركان: الركن الأول في المقدمات وهي ثلاثة:
(المقدمة الأولى في العلوم الأولية)
إذا أدركنا حقيقة فإما أن نعتبرها من حيث هي هي من غير حكم عليها لا بالنفي ولا بالإثبات وهو التصور، أو نحكم عليها بنفي أو إثبات وهو التصديق.
القول في التصورات
وعندي أن شيئا منها غير مكتسب أو وجهين [؟] بباطل [؟] لا يحظى، بل يجعل طالب الحق بنظره فيه كظمآن يصل إلى السراب. ويصير المتحير في الطرق المختلفة آيسا عن الظفر بالصواب. رأيت أن أكشف القناع عن وجوه أبكار مخدراته، وأبين الخلل في مكامن من شبهاته، وأدل على غثه وسمينه، وأبين ما يجب أن يبحث عنه من شكه ويقينه. وإن كان قد اجتهد قوم من الأفاضل في إيضاحه وشرحه، وقوم في نقض قواعده وجرحه. ولم يجر أكثرهم على قاعدة الإنصاف. ولم تخل بياناتهم عن الميل والاعتساف. وأسميت الكتاب 'تلخيص المحصل'، وأتحف به بعد أن يتم ويتحصل إلى مجلس المولى المعظم، الصاحب الأعظم، العالم العادل، المنصف الكامل، علاء الحق والدين، بهاء الإسلام والمسلمين، ملك الوزراء في العالمين، صاحب ديوان الممالك، دستور الشرق والغرب، عطاء الملك [؟]، ابن صاحب السعيد [؟]، بهاء الدولة والدين محمد، أعز الله أنصاره وضاعف اقتداره، إذ هو في هذا العصر بحمد الله معتنى بالأمور الدينية لا غير، موفق في إحياء معالم كل خير، منفرد في اقتناء الكمالات الحقيقية، متخصص بإنشاء الخيرات الأخروية. فإن لاحظه بعين الرضا فذلك هو المبتغى، وإلى الله المرجع، والعاقبة لمن اهتدى.
والأشرع [؟]
ولا شرع [؟] فيهما أنا بصدده، وأورد عباراته أولا ثم اشتغل بحل عقده.
(1) أقول: خالف المصنف سائر الحكماء في التصديق، فإنه عنده إدراك مع الحكم كما أن التصور إدراك لا مع الحكم. وعندهم أن التصديق هو الحكم وحده من غير أن يدخل التصور في مفهومه دخول الجزء في الكل، والتصور هو الإدراك الساذج. وكأنهم قسموا المعاني إلى نفس الإدراك وإلى ما يلحقه، وقسموا ما يلحقه إلى ما يجعله معتدلا للتصديق والتكذيب وإلى ما لا يحتمله كذلك كالصيغ اللاحقة به في الأمر والنهي والاستفهام والتمني وغير ذلك. وسموا القسمين الأولين بالعلم. وضمير 'هو' في لفظ المصنف في قوله 'وهو التصديق' يرجع إلى مصدر 'أدركنا' كما هو في اللفظة وهو التصور، ولا يجوز أن يرجع إلى مصدر 'نحكم' في قوله 'أو نحكم عليها'، لأن ذلك يقتضي كون التصديق هو الحكم وحده.
(2) أقول: هذه الصفة توهم جزئية المحكوم [؟] ومراده كليته، مثل ما يقتضي دخول حرف السلب على النكرة.
The pillars of the science of kalam. The first pillar: on the preliminaries. The science of kalam is arranged on pillars: the first pillar on the preliminaries, and they are three: (The first introduction: on primary sciences). If we perceive a reality, then either we consider it as it is in itself without any judgment on it, neither negation nor affirmation, and that is conception (tasawwur), or we make a judgment on it by negation or affirmation, and that is assent (tasdiq). The discussion on conceptions. And in my view, none of them is non-acquired or two aspects [؟] with falsehood [؟] does not succeed, but rather makes the seeker of truth, by his looking into it, like a thirsty person who reaches a mirage. And the perplexed one in the various paths becomes despairing of attaining correctness. I saw that I should remove the veil from the faces of the virgins of its veiled ones, and show the defect in the hiding places of its doubts, and point out its bad and its good, and show what must be sought from its doubt and its certainty. And even though some virtuous people have striven to clarify it and explain it, and some to refute its principles and wound it, most of them did not follow the rule of fairness, and their statements were not free from bias and deviation. And I named the book 'Talkhis al-Muhassal' (The Summary of al-Muhassal), and I present it after it is completed and summarized to the council of the exalted master, the greatest companion, the learned just one, the perfect fair one, the glory of the truth and religion, the splendor of Islam and Muslims, the king of ministers in the worlds, the owner of the court of the kingdoms, the constitution of the East and West, 'Ata' al-Mulk [؟], son of Sahib al-Sa'id [؟], Baha' al-Dawla wa al-Din Muhammad, may God strengthen his supporters and double his power, for he in this age, praise be to God, is concerned with religious matters only, successful in reviving the landmarks of all good, unique in acquiring true perfections, specialized in founding eternal goods. If he looks upon it with the eye of approval, that is the goal, and to God is the return, and the end is for those who are guided. And al-Ashra' [؟]. And I do not delve [؟] into both of them now; I present his statements first and then engage in solving his difficulty. (1) I say: The author differed from the rest of the philosophers regarding assent (tasdiq). For him, it is perception with judgment, just as conception (tasawwur) is perception without judgment. According to them, assent is judgment alone, without conception entering its concept as a part of a whole, and conception is bare perception. It is as if they divided meanings into perception itself and what attaches to it, and divided what attaches to it into what makes it balanced for affirmation and denial and into what does not admit that, like the forms attached to it in command, prohibition, interrogation, wish, etc. And they called the first two divisions 'knowledge'. And the pronoun 'huwa' in the author's statement 'wa huwa al-tasdiq' refers back to the source 'adrakna' as it is in the word, which is 'al-tasawwur', and it is not allowed to refer to the source 'nahkum' in his statement 'aw nahkum 'alayha', because that would require that assent is judgment alone. (2) I say: This description suggests that the judged is partial [؟], but his intention is its universality, like what the entry of the negative particle over an indefinite noun implies.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a combination of the author's text and a commentator's explanation. It begins with the heading 'The pillars of kalam' and introduces the first pillar on preliminaries, which has three introductions. The first introduction defines primary sciences: when we perceive something, either we consider it without judgment (tasawwur) or we make a judgment (tasdiq). The author then comments that none of the tasawwurat are acquired (i.e., all are known intuitively?), and criticizes those who approach the subject without success. He resolves to clarify the topic and names his work 'Talkhis al-Muhassal'. He dedicates it to a patron. The commentator then explains that the author differed from other philosophers: for the author, tasdiq is perception with judgment, while for others tasdiq is judgment alone and tasawwur is bare perception. The commentator analyzes a pronoun reference issue and a problem about universal vs. particular in a subsequent note.

Key Terms
التصور
التصديق
المقدمة
العلوم الأولية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that tasawwur and tasdiq are completely separate types of knowledge with no relation.
Correction: In fact, tasdiq always builds on tasawwur: you must conceive of something before you can judge it.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is tasawwur?
A) A judgment with negation or affirmation.
B) Perception without judgment.
C) A type of proof.
D) The same as tasdiq.

Key Takeaway
The first step in knowledge is conception; judgment follows upon that conception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your belief in Allah, have you merely formed a concept (tasawwur) of Him, or have you made a decisive judgment (tasdiq) that affects your actions? What specific evidence in your daily life shows that your belief is more than just a concept?
?
The lesson states that tasawwur is without any judgment. How many Islamic teachings do you simply ‘know about’ without ever passing a personal judgment on their truth? Pick one teaching you have only conceptualized and ask yourself: what would it take for you to move from tasawwur to tasdiq?
?
You claim to believe in the Day of Judgment. Is that belief a mere mental image (tasawwur) or a profound assent (tasdiq) that transforms how you spend your time today? If it were truly tasdiq, what would you be doing differently right now?

Module 8 · Lesson 2

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
6 min
Al-Razi's Unique View on Tasdiq
The commentator clarifies al-Razi's unique definition of tasdiq compared to other philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author (al-Razi) differed from other philosophers in defining tasdiq as perception with judgment.

Core Idea
For al-Razi, tasdiq includes judgment and perception; for others, it is only judgment.

The commentator explains a crucial nuance in al-Razi's epistemology. While other philosophers define tasdiq as mere judgment, al-Razi insists it is perception accompanied by judgment. This has implications for how knowledge is structured.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentator's explanation
Read the paragraph starting with '(1) I say: The author differed...' and its translation. Note the structure: first the difference, then the classification of meanings, then the pronoun resolution.
2
Identify the two views
Clearly distinguish al-Razi's view (perception with judgment) from that of other philosophers (judgment alone).
3
Understand the pronoun reference issue
The commentator explains why the pronoun 'huwa' must refer to 'al-tasawwur' and not to judgment, to avoid implying that tasdiq is only judgment.
This shows careful attention to wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أركان علم الكلام
الركن الأول في المقدمات
علم الكلام مرتب على أركان: الركن الأول في المقدمات وهي ثلاثة:
(المقدمة الأولى في العلوم الأولية)
إذا أدركنا حقيقة فإما أن نعتبرها من حيث هي هي من غير حكم عليها لا بالنفي ولا بالإثبات وهو التصور، أو نحكم عليها بنفي أو إثبات وهو التصديق.
القول في التصورات
وعندي أن شيئا منها غير مكتسب أو وجهين [؟] بباطل [؟] لا يحظى، بل يجعل طالب الحق بنظره فيه كظمآن يصل إلى السراب. ويصير المتحير في الطرق المختلفة آيسا عن الظفر بالصواب. رأيت أن أكشف القناع عن وجوه أبكار مخدراته، وأبين الخلل في مكامن من شبهاته، وأدل على غثه وسمينه، وأبين ما يجب أن يبحث عنه من شكه ويقينه. وإن كان قد اجتهد قوم من الأفاضل في إيضاحه وشرحه، وقوم في نقض قواعده وجرحه. ولم يجر أكثرهم على قاعدة الإنصاف. ولم تخل بياناتهم عن الميل والاعتساف. وأسميت الكتاب 'تلخيص المحصل'، وأتحف به بعد أن يتم ويتحصل إلى مجلس المولى المعظم، الصاحب الأعظم، العالم العادل، المنصف الكامل، علاء الحق والدين، بهاء الإسلام والمسلمين، ملك الوزراء في العالمين، صاحب ديوان الممالك، دستور الشرق والغرب، عطاء الملك [؟]، ابن صاحب السعيد [؟]، بهاء الدولة والدين محمد، أعز الله أنصاره وضاعف اقتداره، إذ هو في هذا العصر بحمد الله معتنى بالأمور الدينية لا غير، موفق في إحياء معالم كل خير، منفرد في اقتناء الكمالات الحقيقية، متخصص بإنشاء الخيرات الأخروية. فإن لاحظه بعين الرضا فذلك هو المبتغى، وإلى الله المرجع، والعاقبة لمن اهتدى.
والأشرع [؟]
ولا شرع [؟] فيهما أنا بصدده، وأورد عباراته أولا ثم اشتغل بحل عقده.
(1) أقول: خالف المصنف سائر الحكماء في التصديق، فإنه عنده إدراك مع الحكم كما أن التصور إدراك لا مع الحكم. وعندهم أن التصديق هو الحكم وحده من غير أن يدخل التصور في مفهومه دخول الجزء في الكل، والتصور هو الإدراك الساذج. وكأنهم قسموا المعاني إلى نفس الإدراك وإلى ما يلحقه، وقسموا ما يلحقه إلى ما يجعله معتدلا للتصديق والتكذيب وإلى ما لا يحتمله كذلك كالصيغ اللاحقة به في الأمر والنهي والاستفهام والتمني وغير ذلك. وسموا القسمين الأولين بالعلم. وضمير 'هو' في لفظ المصنف في قوله 'وهو التصديق' يرجع إلى مصدر 'أدركنا' كما هو في اللفظة وهو التصور، ولا يجوز أن يرجع إلى مصدر 'نحكم' في قوله 'أو نحكم عليها'، لأن ذلك يقتضي كون التصديق هو الحكم وحده.
(2) أقول: هذه الصفة توهم جزئية المحكوم [؟] ومراده كليته، مثل ما يقتضي دخول حرف السلب على النكرة.
The pillars of the science of kalam. The first pillar: on the preliminaries. The science of kalam is arranged on pillars: the first pillar on the preliminaries, and they are three: (The first introduction: on primary sciences). If we perceive a reality, then either we consider it as it is in itself without any judgment on it, neither negation nor affirmation, and that is conception (tasawwur), or we make a judgment on it by negation or affirmation, and that is assent (tasdiq). The discussion on conceptions. And in my view, none of them is non-acquired or two aspects [؟] with falsehood [؟] does not succeed, but rather makes the seeker of truth, by his looking into it, like a thirsty person who reaches a mirage. And the perplexed one in the various paths becomes despairing of attaining correctness. I saw that I should remove the veil from the faces of the virgins of its veiled ones, and show the defect in the hiding places of its doubts, and point out its bad and its good, and show what must be sought from its doubt and its certainty. And even though some virtuous people have striven to clarify it and explain it, and some to refute its principles and wound it, most of them did not follow the rule of fairness, and their statements were not free from bias and deviation. And I named the book 'Talkhis al-Muhassal' (The Summary of al-Muhassal), and I present it after it is completed and summarized to the council of the exalted master, the greatest companion, the learned just one, the perfect fair one, the glory of the truth and religion, the splendor of Islam and Muslims, the king of ministers in the worlds, the owner of the court of the kingdoms, the constitution of the East and West, 'Ata' al-Mulk [؟], son of Sahib al-Sa'id [؟], Baha' al-Dawla wa al-Din Muhammad, may God strengthen his supporters and double his power, for he in this age, praise be to God, is concerned with religious matters only, successful in reviving the landmarks of all good, unique in acquiring true perfections, specialized in founding eternal goods. If he looks upon it with the eye of approval, that is the goal, and to God is the return, and the end is for those who are guided. And al-Ashra' [؟]. And I do not delve [؟] into both of them now; I present his statements first and then engage in solving his difficulty. (1) I say: The author differed from the rest of the philosophers regarding assent (tasdiq). For him, it is perception with judgment, just as conception (tasawwur) is perception without judgment. According to them, assent is judgment alone, without conception entering its concept as a part of a whole, and conception is bare perception. It is as if they divided meanings into perception itself and what attaches to it, and divided what attaches to it into what makes it balanced for affirmation and denial and into what does not admit that, like the forms attached to it in command, prohibition, interrogation, wish, etc. And they called the first two divisions 'knowledge'. And the pronoun 'huwa' in the author's statement 'wa huwa al-tasdiq' refers back to the source 'adrakna' as it is in the word, which is 'al-tasawwur', and it is not allowed to refer to the source 'nahkum' in his statement 'aw nahkum 'alayha', because that would require that assent is judgment alone. (2) I say: This description suggests that the judged is partial [؟], but his intention is its universality, like what the entry of the negative particle over an indefinite noun implies.

Commentary
This passage is a combination of the author's text and a commentator's explanation. It begins with the heading 'The pillars of kalam' and introduces the first pillar on preliminaries, which has three introductions. The first introduction defines primary sciences: when we perceive something, either we consider it without judgment (tasawwur) or we make a judgment (tasdiq). The author then comments that none of the tasawwurat are acquired (i.e., all are known intuitively?), and criticizes those who approach the subject without success. He resolves to clarify the topic and names his work 'Talkhis al-Muhassal'. He dedicates it to a patron. The commentator then explains that the author differed from other philosophers: for the author, tasdiq is perception with judgment, while for others tasdiq is judgment alone and tasawwur is bare perception. The commentator analyzes a pronoun reference issue and a problem about universal vs. particular in a subsequent note.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that tasdiq is purely a judgment without any accompanying perception.
Correction: Al-Razi insists that tasdiq includes both perception and judgment, contra other philosophers.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, how do other philosophers define tasdiq?
A) Perception with judgment.
B) Judgment alone.
C) Bare perception.
D) A type of interrogation.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's definition of tasdiq as perception-with-judgment sets his epistemology apart from other philosophers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make an assent (tasdiq) about a matter of faith, do you find that it involves both an intellectual perception and a judgment of the heart? How do you experience the difference between merely knowing a fact and assenting to it with conviction? Which of the two views in this lesson resonates with your own inner experience? This week, pay attention to a belief you hold—does it feel like a bare perception or a judgment accompanied by a deeper knowing?
?
The commentator emphasizes the precision of pronoun reference to avoid misunderstanding. How often do you read sacred texts or scholarly works with such attention to details like pronoun antecedents? Could a lack of precision in your own reading lead you to adopt a wrong understanding of a core doctrine? Commit to reading one paragraph of a theological text this week with careful grammatical analysis, noting how tiny words carry big meanings.

What Is Kalam?
beginner
4 min
The Necessity of Unacquired Conceptions
Razi argues for the necessity of unacquired conceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some conceptions must be unacquired and the rule determining when a conception is acquired or not.

Core Idea
Not all conceptions can be acquired — some must be innate to avoid infinite regress.

This passage begins by establishing that scholars who allow acquired conceptions agree that not all are acquired. Otherwise, we would face infinite regress or circularity, both impossible. Thus, some conceptions must be independent of acquisition. The author then formulates a rule: every conception on which an unacquired affirmation depends is itself unacquired; for acquired affirmations, it may be acquired or not.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem of infinite regress
Read the passage and note why not all conceptions can be acquired: otherwise we would have infinite regress or circularity.
These are logical impossibilities in this context.
2
Identify the rule about dependence
Find the rule: a conception needed for an unacquired affirmation is itself unacquired; for an acquired affirmation, it may be acquired or not.
3
Reflect on foundational concepts
Think about why this foundation is necessary for the theory of definition and knowledge.
This sets the stage for the subsequent discussion of definition types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون بالتصور تفريع : القائلون بأن التصور قد يكون كسبيا اتفقوا على أنه ليس كله كذلك وإلا لزم التسلسل أو الدور وهما محالان، بل لابد من تصورات غنية عن الاكتساب ، ثم الضابط أن كل تصور يتوقف عليه تصديق غير مكتسب فهو غير مكتسب، أما الذي يتوقف عليه تصديق مكتسب فقد يكون مكتسبا وقد لا يكون مكتسبا ، واتفقوا على أنه لا يمكن أن يكون المكتسب نفس المكتسب (1) بل إن كان مجموع أجزائه ، فهي الحد التام ، أو بعض أجزائه المساوية فهي الحد الناقص ، أو الأمر الخارج وحده وهو الرسم الناقص ، أو ما يتركب من الداخل والخارج وهو الرسم التام (2) . تذنيبات: البسيط الذي لا يتركب عنه غيره لا يعرف ولا يعرف به، والمركب الذي يتركب عنه خبره يعرف ويعرف به، والمركب الذي لا يتركب عنه خبره يعرف ولا يعرف به، والبسيط الذي يتركب عنه غيره لا يعرف ويعرف به، والمراد من هذه التعريفات الحدية (3) (ب) يجب الاحتراز عن تعريف الشيء بما هو مثله وبالأخفى ، وعن تعريف الشيء بنفسه وبما لا يعرف إلا به ، إما بمرتبة واحدة أو بمراتب (4) (ج) يجب تقديم الجزء الأعم على الأخص لأن الأعم أعرف وتقديم الأعرف أولى .
Those who say that conception may be acquired agreed that it is not entirely so, otherwise there would be infinite regress or circularity, both impossible. Rather, there must be conceptions that are independent of acquisition. Then the rule is: every conception upon which an unacquired affirmation depends is itself unacquired. As for that upon which an acquired affirmation depends, it may be acquired or not. They agreed that the acquired cannot be identical with the acquired (1); rather, if it is the sum of its parts, it is the complete definition; or some of its equal parts, it is the incomplete definition; or the external part alone, it is the incomplete description; or what is composed of internal and external, it is the complete description (2). Appendices: The simple which nothing else is composed from it is not known and does not make known. The composite which something is composed from it is known and makes known. The composite which something is not composed from it is known but does not make known. The simple which something else is composed from it is not known but makes known. The intended meaning of these definitions is essential definitions (3). (B) One must avoid defining a thing by what is like it, by what is more obscure, by itself, or by what is not known except through it, whether in one degree or in degrees (4). (C) The more general part must be presented before the more specific, because the more general is more known, and presenting what is more known is preferable.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of definitions in the context of acquired conceptions (tasawwur). It begins by stating that all scholars who allow acquired conceptions agree that not all conceptions are acquired, otherwise infinite regress or circularity would result. Hence, there must be some unacquired conceptions. The author then establishes a rule: any conception that is a prerequisite for an unacquired affirmation (tasdiq) is itself unacquired; if it is a prerequisite for an acquired affirmation, it may be acquired or not. He then clarifies that the acquired definition cannot be identical to what is being defined. Instead, four types of definition are distinguished: (1) complete definition (al-ḥadd al-tāmm) when it includes all parts of the thing, (2) incomplete definition (al-ḥadd al-nāqiṣ) when it includes some equal parts, (3) incomplete description (al-rasm al-nāqiṣ) when it includes only the external part, and (4) complete description (al-rasm al-tāmm) when it combines internal and external parts. Next, the passage includes appendices (tadhnībāt) that classify simple and composite concepts in terms of whether they are known (ma'rūf) and whether they make other things known (mu'arraf bih). A simple that is not a constituent of anything else is neither known nor makes known; a composite that is a constituent of something is both known and makes known; a composite that is not a constituent is known but does not make known; a simple that is a constituent of something else is not known but makes known. The author refers to these as 'definitions' in the essential sense. He then gives two rules for proper definition: (B) avoid defining a thing by what is like it (e.g., defining 'even' as 'not odd'), by what is more obscure, by itself, or by something that itself is only known through the thing being defined, whether immediately or through multiple steps; (C) present the more general term before the more specific because the general is more known and presenting the more known is preferable.

Key Terms
تصور (tasawwur)
تصديق (tasdiq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All conceptions can be acquired through definition or instruction.
Correction: The passage shows that some conceptions must be unacquired to avoid infinite regress and circularity.

Checkpoint

B) Perfect knowledge
C) Nothing

The Four Types of Definition
Razi enumerates four types of definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of definition: complete and incomplete definitions and descriptions.

Core Idea
Definitions are either complete/incomplete definitions or descriptions based on parts.

After establishing the necessity of unacquired conceptions, the author turns to the nature of acquired definitions. He first states that the acquired definition cannot be identical to the thing defined. Then he distinguishes four types based on whether the definition includes all or some of the thing's parts and whether those parts are internal (essential) or external (accidental).

Guided Path
1
Note the principle of non-identity
Understand that the acquired definition cannot be the very thing defined.
This prevents circular definitions.
2
Identify the four types
Learn the four types: complete definition, incomplete definition, incomplete description, complete description.
Pay attention to what each includes: parts, equal parts, external part, or combination.
3
Compare and contrast
Distinguish between definitions (containing essential parts) and descriptions (containing accidental parts).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون بالتصور تفريع : القائلون بأن التصور قد يكون كسبيا اتفقوا على أنه ليس كله كذلك وإلا لزم التسلسل أو الدور وهما محالان، بل لابد من تصورات غنية عن الاكتساب ، ثم الضابط أن كل تصور يتوقف عليه تصديق غير مكتسب فهو غير مكتسب، أما الذي يتوقف عليه تصديق مكتسب فقد يكون مكتسبا وقد لا يكون مكتسبا ، واتفقوا على أنه لا يمكن أن يكون المكتسب نفس المكتسب (1) بل إن كان مجموع أجزائه ، فهي الحد التام ، أو بعض أجزائه المساوية فهي الحد الناقص ، أو الأمر الخارج وحده وهو الرسم الناقص ، أو ما يتركب من الداخل والخارج وهو الرسم التام (2) . تذنيبات: البسيط الذي لا يتركب عنه غيره لا يعرف ولا يعرف به، والمركب الذي يتركب عنه خبره يعرف ويعرف به، والمركب الذي لا يتركب عنه خبره يعرف ولا يعرف به، والبسيط الذي يتركب عنه غيره لا يعرف ويعرف به، والمراد من هذه التعريفات الحدية (3) (ب) يجب الاحتراز عن تعريف الشيء بما هو مثله وبالأخفى ، وعن تعريف الشيء بنفسه وبما لا يعرف إلا به ، إما بمرتبة واحدة أو بمراتب (4) (ج) يجب تقديم الجزء الأعم على الأخص لأن الأعم أعرف وتقديم الأعرف أولى .
Those who say that conception may be acquired agreed that it is not entirely so, otherwise there would be infinite regress or circularity, both impossible. Rather, there must be conceptions that are independent of acquisition. Then the rule is: every conception upon which an unacquired affirmation depends is itself unacquired. As for that upon which an acquired affirmation depends, it may be acquired or not. They agreed that the acquired cannot be identical with the acquired (1); rather, if it is the sum of its parts, it is the complete definition; or some of its equal parts, it is the incomplete definition; or the external part alone, it is the incomplete description; or what is composed of internal and external, it is the complete description (2). Appendices: The simple which nothing else is composed from it is not known and does not make known. The composite which something is composed from it is known and makes known. The composite which something is not composed from it is known but does not make known. The simple which something else is composed from it is not known but makes known. The intended meaning of these definitions is essential definitions (3). (B) One must avoid defining a thing by what is like it, by what is more obscure, by itself, or by what is not known except through it, whether in one degree or in degrees (4). (C) The more general part must be presented before the more specific, because the more general is more known, and presenting what is more known is preferable.

Rules for Definition and the Simple/Composite Distinction
Razi discusses properties of simple/composite concepts and rules for definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of simple and composite concepts in terms of being known/making known, and the two rules for avoiding faulty definitions.

Core Idea
A good definition avoids circularity, obscurity, and prioritizes the general over the specific.

The final part of this passage contains two appendices. First, it classifies simple and composite concepts based on whether they are known and whether they make other things known. Second, it gives two rules for constructing a proper definition: avoid defining by what is like, more obscure, by itself, or by what is only known through it, and present the more general before the more specific.

Guided Path
1
Read the appendices on simple/composite
Understand the four cases: simple that is not a constituent, composite that is a constituent, composite not a constituent, simple that is a constituent.
Focus on the pattern of being known (ma'rūf) and making known (mu'arraf bih).
2
Learn the two rules for definition
Rule B: avoid circular, obscure, self-referential definitions. Rule C: present the more general before the more specific.
3
Apply the rules
Think of examples that violate these rules and how to correct them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Mirror Errors: Sense or Soul?
The commentator explains that mirror distortions are due to mental interpretation, not sense error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why perceived errors in mirror images are due to the soul's interpretation, not a failure of the sense of sight.

Core Idea
Mirror 'errors' are the soul’s interpretation, not sense errors.

Imam al-Razi illustrates a key epistemological point: the senses themselves do not err; error arises from the mind's interpretation. Through the example of cylindrical mirrors, he shows how reflection geometry leads to distorted images, yet the sense of sight receives accurate rays. This lesson unpacks that insight.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full English translation. Let the examples and conclusion sink in.
Pay attention to the different mirror configurations described.
2
Identify the mirror effects
List the different effects: face appears long, wide, distorted, multiple, inverted. Notice the pattern: each effect results from a specific geometry of reflection.
The reflection depends on whether the reflector is straight or curved.
3
Connect to the conclusion
Reflect on the final sentence: all these are errors of mental apprehension (badīhat al-idrāk al-nafsānī), not of the sense. Ask yourself: why does this distinction matter for theology?
This distinction defends the reliability of sense perception as a source of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : المرآة الطويلة المستقيمة فى الطول والمنحنية في العرض كقالب أسطوانة مستديرة إذا نظر إليها بحيث يكون طولها محاذيا لطول الوجه برى الوجه فيها طويلا طوله بقدر طول الوجه قليل المرض لانعكاس الشعاع المرضى بما هو أقل عرضا مما أو كان مستقيما، وذلك لأن الطول ينعكس من عاكس مستقيم، والعرض ينعكس من عاكس منحن، وإذا نظر إليها بحيث يكون طولها محاذيا لعرض الوجه كان الأمر بالعكس، فترى الوجه عريضا عرضه بقدر عرض الوجه وطوله أقل من طوله ، وإذا نظر إليها بحيث يكون موريا في محاذاة الوجه يرى الوجه معوجا ، وإذا كانت المرآة بحيث ينعكس منها الشعاع من موضعين أو أكثر إلى موضع واحد رأى الناظر فيها لنفسه وجهين أو أكثر ورأسين أو أكثر ، ومن بعضها يرى وجهه متنكسا ، وكذلك في الاختلافات المتنوعة التي تشتمل على أكثرها كتب المرايا ويحتال لها متخذو المرايا على وجه يقصدونه، فقد ظهر ما مر أن كل ذلك غلط بديمة الإدراك النفساني من المحسوسات المنادية إليها ، لا غلط الحس.
I say: The long mirror that is straight in length and curved in width, like the mold of a round cylinder, when one looks into it such that its length aligns with the length of the face, one sees the face long, its length being as much as the length of the face, with little width due to the reflection of the widthwise ray as less wide than it was straight. That is because the length reflects from a straight reflector, and the width reflects from a curved reflector. And when one looks into it such that its length aligns with the width of the face, the matter is reversed: one sees the face wide, its width equal to the width of the face, and its length less than its length. And when one looks into it such that it is placed in alignment with the face, one sees the face distorted. And when the mirror is such that the ray reflects from it from two or more positions to one position, the viewer sees for himself two or more faces and two or more heads; and from some [mirrors] one sees his face inverted, and similarly for the various differences that most books on mirrors contain, and mirror makers contrive them for the purpose they intend. So it has become apparent from what has passed that all that is a mistake of the primary mental apprehension from the sensible things attributed to it, not a mistake of the sense.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by the author (أقول) on the earlier argument about mirrors. It clarifies that the apparent errors in mirror perception—such as seeing the face elongated, widened, inverted, or multiple—are not errors of the sense itself but of the soul's judgment (الادراك النفساني). The commentator explains the geometry of reflection: in a cylindrical mirror, the length and width reflect differently because the curvature affects the rays. When the face aligns with the mirror's length, the length reflects straight (from a straight section) and the width reflects from a curved section, causing distortion. The same applies when alignment changes. Multiple reflections from different points to one point create multiple images. These are not errors of the sense of sight but errors in the soul's interpretation due to unawareness of the reflection process. The conclusion is that all such phenomena are mistakes of the immediate mental apprehension (بديهة الادراك النفساني) from the sensible objects, not mistakes of the sense itself.

Key Terms
المرآة الطويلة المستقيمة
المنحنية في العرض
انعكاس الشعاع
عاكس مستقيم
عاكس منحن
خطأ الادراك النفساني
غلط الحس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sense of sight is directly deceived by mirrors.
Correction: The sense receives the ray accurately; the error occurs in the soul's immediate judgment (badīhat al-idrāk al-nafsānī) that misinterprets the image without accounting for reflection geometry.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why does a cylindrical mirror sometimes make the face appear longer than normal?
A) The mirror is defective.
B) The length reflects from a straight reflector while the width reflects from a curved one.
C) The sense of sight is inherently incorrect.
D) The mirror is made of a special material.

Key Takeaway
Perceptual errors in mirrors are mistakes of the mind's immediate judgment, not errors of the sense of sight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see something that appears to contradict your faith or reason, do you immediately assume your senses or intellect have failed, or do you examine your own interpretation? How does this lesson challenge your tendency to blame your senses rather than your understanding?

Think of a time you were certain of something you perceived but later realized it was a misinterpretation. How does that experience align with Razi's distinction between sense and mental judgment? Are you quick to trust your initial reading of a situation without accounting for the 'mirror' of your own biases?
?
In your daily life, when you encounter something that seems to conflict with clear texts or logic, do you immediately conclude the texts or logic are wrong, or do you pause to consider that your own mental 'mirror' might be curved? How can you train yourself to examine your own interpretations before concluding error?

The Dream Argument: Sense Error or Soul Error?
The commentator distinguishes between sense error and soul error in dreams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the dream argument does not prove sense fallibility but rather the soul's error due to heedlessness, and why this distinction matters in Islamic epistemology.

Core Idea
The sleeper's error is in the soul, not the senses.

This passage addresses a common sceptical challenge based on dreams. The commentator clarifies that the sleeper's mistake is not a failure of the senses but a failure of the soul to distinguish reality from imagination due to heedlessness. Understanding this distinction protects the reliability of sensory knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the argument. Notice the comparison between the wakeful and sleeping states.
2
Identify the two states
Compare the wakeful person's awareness (واع) to the sleeper's heedlessness (غافل). How does each judge their perceptions?
3
Recognize the mistake type
The commentator explicitly says the error is not of sense (غلط حس) but of the soul (غلط النفس). Understand why this distinction is important.
The soul's error arises from failing to distinguish the real thing from its likeness during heedlessness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : النائم يرى فى خياله مثل ما يرى المستيقظ، إلا أن المستيقظ لما كان وانها على أحكام اليقظة حكم بأن أحد مراتب واقع حق والآخر غير واقع وغير حق، والنائم لما كان غافلا عن الإحساس حسب أن الواقع الذى يراه في خياله وهذا ليس بغلط حتى ، بل هو غلط للنفس من عدم بين الشيء وبين مثاله حال الذهول عن الشيء .
I say: The sleeper sees in his imagination the like of what the wakeful person sees, except that the wakeful person, since he is aware of the judgments of wakefulness, judges that one of the two levels is real and true and the other is unreal and false. And the sleeper, since he is heedless of sensation, supposes that the reality he sees in his imagination [is true] — but this is not a mistake of sense; rather, it is a mistake of the soul due to not distinguishing between the thing and its likeness during the state of heedlessness of the thing.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
In this commentary, the author clarifies the third argument's implication. He explains that both the sleeper and the wakeful person see mental images, but the wakeful person is aware (واع) of the judgments of wakefulness and can distinguish between real perception and imagination. The sleeper, being heedless of external sensation (غافل عن الإحساس), mistakenly takes his imaginative images as real. This error is not an error of the sense itself (غلط حس) but an error of the soul (غلط النفس) that fails to differentiate between the actual object and its mental likeness. Thus, the argument does not prove sense fallibility but rather the fallibility of the soul's judgment when it is distracted.

Key Terms
خيال النائم
المستيقظ
أحكام اليقظة
غلط النفس
الذهول عن الشيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That dreams prove the senses are unreliable.
Correction: The commentator shows the error is due to the soul's heedlessness, not sensory fallibility.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why does the sleeper mistake his imaginings for reality?
A) His senses are dormant and thus unreliable.
B) He is heedless of sensation and fails to distinguish between the real thing and its likeness.
C) Dreams are created by demons.
D) The sense of sight is inherently deceptive.

Key Takeaway
The sleeper's mistake is a soul's error, not a sense error, because it arises from heedlessness of sensation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are heedless of the truth, like the sleeper, what false 'realities' do you tend to mistake for genuine knowledge? What judgments of wakefulness have you abandoned that leave you confused between the real and the imaginary?
?
The sleeper's error is a failure of the soul, not the senses. In what areas of your life are you 'asleep'—judging falsely because you have neglected the signs of truth? Will you wake up now and actively distinguish between the thing and its likeness?
?
If the soul's heedlessness can lead you to mistake fantasy for reality, what practical steps can you take today to train your soul to stay present and discerning, so you do not live in a self-made illusion?

The Pleurisy Patient Analogy: Sense vs. Imagination
Commentator explains the pleurisy patient's error as an imaginative mistake.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator argues that the pleurisy patient's error is not a failure of the senses but a mistake of the soul due to imagination.

Core Idea
The senses never perceive non-existents; only the imagination misleads.

In this brief but crucial passage, the commentator responds to an argument that supposedly proves the senses can be deceived by pointing to cases like dreaming and pleurisy. He shows that in every such case, the senses themselves remain reliable; it is the soul's imaginative judgment that errs. This preserves the foundational role of sense perception in kalam epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice the analogy and the conclusion.
The Arabic is dense; take time to parse each phrase.
2
Identify the analogy
What does the commentator compare the pleurisy patient to? Why is this comparison made? Notice the terms 'استغراقه في الخيال' and 'غفلته عن الإحساس'.
The patient is like a dreamer absorbed in imagination.
3
Understand the defense of the senses
How does the commentator show that the sense itself is not deceived? What role does 'imagination' play? Trace the conclusion that 'الحس لم يدرك ماليس موجود'.
The sense never perceives non-existents; error arises from the soul's judgment.
4
Apply the reasoning
Consider whether this reasoning can be applied to other cases of purported sensory deception, such as dreams or mirages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : حكم صاحب البرسام حكم النائم، فإنه لاستغراقه في الخيال وغفلته عن الإحساس تحكم نفسه بمثل ما يحكم به الدائم، وفى جميع هذه الأحوال لم يعرض الإنسان حالة لأجلها يرى ما ليس بموجود موجوداً وإنه لم ير ذلك بل أدرك بخياله شيئاً ذهل در الإحساس، تظهر أن الحس لم يدرك ماليس وجود فى حال من الأحوال أصلا.
I say: The case of the person with pleurisy is the case of the sleeper. For due to his absorption in imagination and his heedlessness of sensation, his soul judges with something like what the perpetual [sleeper] judges. And in all these states, a state has not occurred to a person on account of which he sees what is not existent as existent; rather, he did not see that, but he perceived something in his imagination while heedless of sensation. It becomes apparent that the sense has never perceived something non-existent in any state at all.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This commentary responds to the fourth argument. The commentator argues that the pleurisy patient's situation is analogous to the dreamer: he is so absorbed in his imagination (الخيال) and heedless of external sensation (غافل عن الإحساس) that his soul (نفسه) makes judgments similar to those of a perpetual dreamer. In all these cases, the person does not actually see something non-existent; rather, he perceives an imaginary object while being unaware of sensory input. The conclusion is that the sense itself has never perceived a non-existent thing in any situation. Therefore, the argument does not prove that the senses are fallible—only that the soul's judgment can be faulty when distracted.

Key Terms
صاحب البرسام
الخيال
الغفلة عن الإحساس
الحس لم يدرك ماليس موجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The pleurisy patient's hallucination proves that the senses can be deceived.
Correction: The commentator insists that the sense itself never perceives non-existents; only the imagination misleads when the soul is heedless of sensation.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the true source of the pleurisy patient's hallucination?
A) The sense of sight is malfunctioning.
B) The patient is absorbed in imagination and heedless of sensation, so his soul mistakenly judges imaginative images as real.
C) Demons are showing him illusions.
D) The patient's eyes are damaged.

Key Takeaway
The senses are infallible in perceiving what exists; errors arise when the soul misinterprets or imagines things while distracted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you, like the pleurisy patient, been so absorbed in your own imagination or anxiety that you treated a false fear as if it were real? What would it mean to 'wake up' to sensory reality and test that fear against actual experience?
?
The commentator says the soul judges based on imagination when heedless of sensation. In your daily life, where are you most prone to this heedlessness—social media, memories, or future worries? What concrete practice could ground you in present sensory awareness?

The Definition of Speculative Reasoning (Nazar)
Defines nazar and discusses its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of nazar as a structured arrangement of assents and its role in generating knowledge.

Core Idea
Nazar is not random thought but a purposeful arrangement of known truths to discover new ones.

Scholars who accept self-evident and sensory knowledge differ on whether these can be combined to yield new knowledge. The majority say yes, and this lesson presents their definition of nazar as the key method. By examining this definition, you will grasp the foundational tool of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation to get a sense of the author's style and the key terms. Note the term 'النظر' and the definition given.
The text contains some unclear phrases due to OCR, but the main definition is clear.
2
Understand the definition of nazar
Focus on the sentence: 'النظر ترتيب تصديقات ليتوصل بها إلى تصديقات أخر'. This is the core definition. Break it down: nazar is an arrangement (ترتيب) of assents (تصديقات) to arrive at other assents.
Assents are judgments (e.g., 'the world is changing'). The arrangement must be purposeful.
3
Reflect on the controversy
The passage mentions that those who accept self-evident and sensory assents differ on whether these can be composed to yield new knowledge. Why might some disagree? Consider the role of logic.
The majority affirmed it; this definition is a response to that debate.
4
Analyze the rule of implication
The author notes that if the premises (the two impliers) are certain, the conclusion is certain; if doubtful, the conclusion follows suit. This connects nazar to epistemic certainty.
This shows that the quality of the conclusion depends on the quality of the premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية في أحكام النظر المعترفون بالتصديقات البديهية والمحسوسة اختلفوا فى أنه هل يمكن تركبها بحيث يتأدى ذلك التركيب إلى صيرورة ما ليس بمعلوم معلوما ، والجمهور من أهل العالم قالوا به ، والكلام فيه وفى تعاريفه يستدعى مسائل . ( مسألة : النظر ترتيب تصديقات ليتوصل بها إلى تصديقات أخر ) فإن من صدق بأن العالم متغير وكل متغير كن حتى لونه التصديق بأن العالم يمكن فلا معنى لفكرة إلا ما حضر في ذهنه من التصدية من المستار بين الصديق الثالث ، ثم المستلزمان إن كانا يقينين كان اللازم كذلك ، وإن كانا ظنين و أحدهم فااللازم كذلك ، ومنهم من جعل الفكر أمرا وراء هذه التصديقات المترتبة إما عدميا
The second introduction: On the judgments of speculative reasoning (النظر). Those who affirm self-evident and sensory assents differed on whether it is possible to combine them such that this combination leads to making what is unknown known. The majority of people affirmed it. Discussion of it and its definitions requires [several] issues. (Issue: Speculative reasoning is the arrangement of assents to arrive at other assents.) For if one assents that the world is changing and that everything changing is [؟] even its color, the assent that the world is [؟] then there is no meaning of thought except what is present in his mind of the assents from the implication between the third assent, then the two implications if they are certain, the consequent is likewise; if they are doubtful or one of them, then the consequent is likewise. And some have made thought something beyond these arranged assents, either non-existent...
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage introduces the second introduction (المقدمة الثانية) on the rulings of nazar (speculative reasoning). It states that those who accept self-evident and sensory assents differ on whether these can be composed to yield new knowledge. The majority affirm this. Then a definition is given: nazar is the arrangement of assents (تصديقات) to arrive at other assents. The author illustrates with an example: if one knows that the world is changing and that everything changing is... (the text is corrupted). Then he notes that if the premises are certain, the conclusion is certain; if they are doubtful, the conclusion is doubtful. Some consider thought (الفكر) to be something beyond these arranged assents, either non-existent or existent (the text cuts off). This lesson presents the definition of nazar and begins the discussion of its conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nazar is just any kind of thinking or pondering.
Correction: Nazar is specifically the arrangement of known assents in a logical order to derive new knowledge; it is not random contemplation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of 'nazar'?
A) Doubt about self-evident truths.
B) The arrangement of assents to arrive at other assents.
C) The suspension of judgment on theoretical matters.
D) The combination of sensory perceptions.

Key Takeaway
Nazar is the purposeful arrangement of known assents to derive new knowledge, and its result's certainty depends on the premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek knowledge of Allah, do you merely rely on what feels right, or do you deliberately arrange what you already know to reach sound conclusions? Which approach does the definition of nazar demand of you?
?
The author states that the certainty of your conclusion depends on the certainty of your premises. Think of a belief you hold about Allah or the unseen. Are your premises certain? If not, what should you do before claiming to know?
?
Many people say they 'think' about God, but nazar requires a purposeful arrangement. Does your reflection on tawheed have structure, order from sound, self-evident premises, or is it chaotic? How will you change that starting today?

The Definition and Function of al-Nazar
Author defines nazar and explains its logical condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam definition of nazar as the arrangement of affirmations to reach new knowledge, and the condition that certainty of premises yields certainty of conclusion.

Core Idea
Al-nazar is using known truths to discover unknown truths.

This lesson introduces the core definition of nazar (theoretical reasoning) in kalam. The author presents nazar as a deliberate arrangement of known affirmations to reach unknown ones, and explains how the certainty of the conclusion depends on the certainty of the premises. This definition sets the stage for the entire discussion of induction and inference in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic definition of nazar: 'arrangement of affirmations to reach other affirmations.' Notice how it emphasizes a purposeful ordering of known truths.
The term 'affirmations' refers to propositions that are accepted as true.
2
Understand the underlying controversy
The passage states that those who accept intuitive and sensory knowledge differ on whether combining them can yield new knowledge. The majority affirm it. This controversy motivates the need for a definition of nazar.
3
Examine the conditional rule
The author explains that if the two implicating premises are certain, the conclusion is certain; if they are probable, the conclusion is probable. This shows that nazar does not guarantee certainty—it depends on the quality of the premises.
This rule is fundamental in kalam logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية في أحكام النظر

المعترفون بالتصديقات البديهية والمحسوسة اختلفوا فى أنه هل يمكن تركبها بحيث يتأدى ذلك التركيب إلى صيرورة ما ليس بمعلوم معلوما ، والجمهور من أهل العالم قالوا به ، والكلام فيه وفى تعاريفه يستدعى مسائل . ( مسألة :
: النظر ترتيب تصديقات ليتوصل بها إلى تصديقات أخر )

فإن من صدق بأن العالم متغير وكل متغير كن حتى لونه التصديق بأن العالم يمكن فلا معنى لفكرة إلا ما حضر في ذهنه من التصدية من المستار بين الصديق الثالث ، ثم المستلزمان إن كانا يقينين كان اللازم كذلك ، وإن كانا ظنين و أحدهم فااللازم كذلك ، ومنهم من جعل الفكر أمرا وراء هذه التصديقات المترتبة إما عدميا

الذي يقال الفكر تجريد العقل عن الغفلات، أو وجوديا وهو الذى يقال الفكر هو تحديق العقل محـــــو وهو المعقولات ، وهذا كما أن الرؤية بالعين يتقدمها تحديق النظر إلى المرء وهو تقليب الحدقة نحوه التماسا لرؤيته بالبصر ، وكذا الرؤية بالمقل يتقدمها تحذيق العقل نحو المطلوب التماسا لرؤيته بالبصيرة (1) .
The Second Introduction: On the Judgments of al-Naẓar.
Those who affirm intuitive and sensory affirmations differ on whether they can be combined such that the combination leads to making what is not known known. The majority of the world's people affirm it. Discussion about it and its definitions requires issues.
(Question: al-Naẓar is the arrangement of affirmations to reach other affirmations.)
For one who affirms that the world is changing and everything changing is ... then the affirmation that the world is ... So no meaning to thought except what is present in his mind from the affirmations of the necessary between the third affirmation. Then if the two implicants are certain, then the implication is likewise; if they are probable and one of them ... the implication is likewise. And among them are those who make thought something beyond these arranged affirmations, either privative — which is said to be the abstraction of the intellect from heedlessness — or existential — which is said to be the concentration of the intellect, which is [the focus] on intelligibles. This is like how vision with the eye is preceded by focusing the gaze on the thing, which is turning the pupil towards it seeking to see it with sight. Similarly, vision with the intellect is preceded by concentration of the intellect towards the object sought, seeking to see it with insight.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage opens the second introduction on the rulings of naẓar (theoretical reasoning). The author first states the issue: those who accept intuitive and sensory knowledge differ on whether such knowledge can be combined in a way that yields new knowledge; the majority affirm it. Then he presents a definition: naẓar is the arrangement of affirmations to reach other affirmations. He gives an example involving the world's mutability. He then explains that if the premises are certain, the conclusion is certain; if probable, the conclusion is probable. Some thinkers consider thought (fikr) to be something beyond this arrangement: either privative (abstraction from heedlessness) or existential (concentration of the intellect on intelligibles), analogous to focusing one's gaze to see an object.

Key Terms
نظر
تصديقات
بديهية
يقين
ظن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think nazar always produces certain knowledge.
Correction: Nazar's conclusion is only as certain as its premises; if premises are probable, conclusion is probable.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the term 'nazar' mean?
A) Intuitive knowledge
B) Arrangement of affirmations to reach other affirmations
C) Abstraction from heedlessness
D) Concentration of the intellect

Key Takeaway
Nazar is the deliberate arrangement of known affirmations to derive unknown ones, and the certainty of the conclusion depends on the certainty of the premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a question about your faith, do you sincerely arrange what you already know to reach a conclusion, or do you rely on feelings or hearsay? Which approach does this lesson demand of you?
?
The passage teaches that nazar can produce certainty or probability depending on the premises. Are the premises of your own faith convictions certain (based on clear evidence) or only probable? How does that affect your confidence in your beliefs?
?
The definition of nazar involves an active arrangement of what you know. Have you ever deliberately taken the time to reason through a matter of aqeedah, or do you let it remain vague? What change can you make today to practice nazar in one area of your faith?

Two Conceptions of Thought Beyond Arrangement
Author presents two alternative views of thought beyond arrangement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two alternative interpretations of thought (fikr) beyond the arrangement of affirmations: as privative abstraction from heedlessness or existential concentration on intelligibles.

Core Idea
Thought can be seen as either removing heedlessness or focusing the intellect.

After defining nazar as arrangement of affirmations, the author notes that some scholars consider thought (fikr) to be something beyond that arrangement. They propose two interpretations: one privative (abstraction from heedlessness) and one existential (concentration of the intellect on intelligibles). An analogy with vision illustrates this.

Guided Path
1
Read the two interpretations
Read the passage that presents the two views: thought as privative (abstraction from heedlessness) and as existential (concentration of the intellect on intelligibles).
Note that both views go beyond the simple arrangement of affirmations.
2
Understand the analogy with vision
The author compares intellectual concentration to focusing the eye's gaze. Before seeing with the eye, one focuses the pupil; similarly, before intellectual insight, one concentrates the intellect on the object sought.
This analogy emphasizes that thought is an active, preparatory process.
3
Reflect on the significance
These interpretations show that early kalam thinkers debated whether thought is merely a logical process or includes a psychological aspect. The two views represent a deeper exploration of the nature of reasoning.

Definition of al-Fikr and al-Nazar
Author defines fikr and nazar with analogy; commentator critiques and offers broader definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of al-fikr and al-nazar, including the author's analogy with sight and the commentator's comprehensive definition.

Core Idea
Fikr and nazar are the mind's act of focusing on intelligibles to reach knowledge.

This lesson introduces two key terms in Islamic epistemology: al-fikr (thought) and al-nazar (reasoning). The author gives initial definitions and an analogy with sight, but the commentator offers a more comprehensive definition. Understanding these terms is essential for studying kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully without analyzing it. Then read the literal English translation. Let the author's and commentator's words speak for themselves.
Notice the two distinct parts: the author's definitions and the commentator's critique.
2
Identify the two definitions of fikr
The author gives a negative definition (abstraction from heedlessness) and a positive definition (focusing the mind towards intelligibles). Identify both in the text.
The positive definition is the main one.
3
Examine the analogy with sight
The author compares intellectual vision to physical vision. Just as focusing the eye precedes seeing, focusing the mind precedes understanding. Reflect on why this analogy is used.
This analogy shows the preparatory nature of nazar.
4
Understand the commentator's critique
The commentator argues that the author's definition is too specific because it only covers reasoning from premises to conclusions. He points out that analysis (from conclusions to premises) and arrangement of concepts are missing.
The commentator uses bold language like 'neglected'.
5
Learn the comprehensive definition
The commentator defines nazar as the transition from things in the mind to sought objectives, and notes that fikr is synonymous. This broader definition includes all forms of reasoning.
This is the key takeaway for the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الذي يقال الفكر تجريد العقل عن الغفلات، أو وجوديا وهو الذي يقال الفكر هو تحديق العقل نحو المعقولات، وهذا كما أن الرؤية بالعين يتقدمها تحديق النظر إلى المرء وهو تقليب الحدقة نحوه التماسا لرؤيته بالبصر، وكذا الرؤية بالعقل يتقدمها تحديق العقل نحو المطلوب التماسا لرؤيته بالبصيرة.
(1) أقول : إنه حد النظر بما هو أخص منه، لأن هذا الحد مختص بما لا يقال من المبادي التصديقية إلى المطالب، وقلما يتيقن مثل هذا النظر ابتداء وإلا كثر أن ينتقل من المطالب أولا إلى مباديها ثم من مباديها إليها، وهذا لا يدخل بتمامه في الحد المذكور، وأيضا ترتيب تصورات يتوصل بها إلى تصور آخر لم يدخل فيه، وهذا القسم هو الذي أغفل صاحب الكتاب، ويتقدم ذلك تحليل تصور إلى مبادي يتألف منها الحد أعني الانتقال من المحدود إلى الحد حتى ينتهي بعد ذلك الانتقال من الحد إلى المحدود، والحد الجامع للنظر أن يقال: النظر هو الانتقال من أمور حاصلة في الذهن إلى أمور مستحصلة هي المقاصد والفكر بحسب الاصطلاح كالمرادف للنظر.
That which is called 'fikr' (thought) is the abstraction of the mind from heedlessness, or existentially it is that which is called 'fikr' and is the focusing of the mind towards intelligibles. This is like how vision by the eye is preceded by focusing the gaze upon the object, which is turning the pupil towards it seeking to see it with sight; likewise, vision by the mind is preceded by focusing the mind towards the sought object, seeking to see it with insight.
(1) I say: This definition of 'nazar' (reasoning) is more specific than it should be, because this definition is restricted to what goes from assertoric premises to conclusions, and rarely is such reasoning certain from the start; otherwise, it would often be the case that one moves first from conclusions to their premises and then from premises to them, and this is not entirely included in the mentioned definition. Also, the arrangement of concepts by which one arrives at another concept is not included in it, and this is the section that the author neglected. Preceding that is the analysis of a concept into premises from which the definition is composed, i.e., moving from the defined to the definition, until after that one moves from the definition to the defined. The comprehensive definition of 'nazar' is to say: 'nazar' is the transition from things present in the mind to things sought that are the objectives; and 'fikr' according to convention is synonymous with 'nazar'.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a definitional discussion of the terms 'al-fikr' and 'al-nazar'. The author begins by giving two definitions of 'al-fikr': first, as the abstraction of the mind from heedlessness (a negative definition), and second, as the focusing of the mind towards intelligibles (a positive definition). He then illustrates this with an analogy: just as physical vision is preceded by focusing the eye and turning the pupil toward the object, so intellectual vision is preceded by focusing the mind on what is sought. After this, a commentator (likely a later scholar) interjects with a critique. He argues that the author's definition is too specific, as it only covers reasoning that proceeds from assertoric premises to conclusions and ignores other valid forms, such as moving from conclusions to premises (analysis) or arranging concepts to arrive at a new concept. The commentator then proposes a more comprehensive definition: 'nazar' is the transition from things already in the mind to things sought (the objectives). He also notes that 'fikr' and 'nazar' are used synonymously in this context.

Key Terms

النظر
تحديق العقل
المعقولات
الغفلات
الحد الجامع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: al-nazar only proceeds from premises to conclusions.
Correction: al-nazar also includes analysis from conclusions to premises and arrangement of concepts.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is missing from the author's definition of 'nazar'?
A) The transition from conclusions to premises
B) The role of sensory perception
C) The definition of 'fikr'
D) The analogy with sight

Key Takeaway
The comprehensive definition of nazar is the transition from things in the mind to sought objectives, and it includes both analysis and synthesis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own pursuit of knowledge, how often do you truly focus your mind (tahdeeq al-'aql) on what you seek, rather than drifting in heedlessness? When was the last time you moved from conclusions to premises, questioning your assumptions?

The commentator warns against a narrow view of reasoning. Are you willing to examine both the forward and backward movements of your thinking when it comes to matters of faith? What would that look like today?
?
If nazar is the transition from present thoughts to sought objectives, what are you actively seeking in your spiritual journey right now? Will you commit to making that transition deliberately, not passively?

Razi's Proof that Knowledge is Not Within Power
Razi argues that knowledge ends with primary principles which are not acquired.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that acquired knowledge cannot regress infinitely and must terminate in primary principles that are not within human power, and the implications for divine command.

Core Idea
Some knowledge is self-evident and not acquired; commanding it is commanding the impossible.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents a foundational argument in Islamic theology: the chain of acquired knowledge cannot go on forever, but must end with self-evident truths. These primary principles are not acquired through effort, and the knowledge that follows from them is necessary. This leads to the conclusion that not all knowledge is within human power, and therefore God cannot command us to acquire it. Let's trace the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi constructs his proof from the impossibility of infinite regress to the conclusion about divine command.
This passage is dense with technical terms like al-awwaliyyāt and amr bi-mā lā yuṭāq.
2
Understand the concept of primary principles
Identify what Razi means by al-awwaliyyāt (primary principles). They are self-evident truths whose terms and implications are not acquired.
For example, 'the whole is greater than the part' is a primary principle.
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the argument: infinite regress is impossible → knowledge ends with primary principles → these are not within power → therefore commanding them is impossible.
Notice how Razi uses the principle of necessity in the third and fourth consequences.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider the doctrine of 'amr bi-mā lā yuṭāq' (commanding what cannot be done). Why does Razi say such a command is not permissible?
This issue was debated among theologians; Razi takes a strong position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى غير النهاية بل ينتهى إلى الأوليات وهى غير مكتسبة لاتصور طرفها ولا استلزام ذينك التصورين للتصديق لإثبات أحدهما الآخر أو سلبه عنه ، ثم إن لزوم ما يلزم منها ضرورى، وكذا القول في اللازم الثالث والرابع، فظهر أن العلوم غير مقدورة البتة ، فكان الأمر بها أمرا بما لا يطاق وأنه غير جائز ولو صح لبطل أصل الدليل
To infinity, but it ends with primary principles (al-awwaliyyāt), which are not acquired (ghayr muktasaba), because neither are their terms conceived nor is the implication of those two conceptions for assent in affirming one of them of the other or negating it from it. Then the necessity of what follows from them is necessary, and the same holds for the third and fourth necessary [consequence]. So it appears that sciences (al-ʿulūm) are absolutely not within power (ghayr maqdūra al-batta), so commanding them is commanding what cannot be accomplished (amr bi-mā lā yuṭāq), and that is not permissible (ghayr jā'iz), and if it were valid the foundation of the proof would be invalid.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a proof that some knowledge is not within human power, concluding that commanding such knowledge is impossible. The argument proceeds: If the chain of acquired knowledge went on infinitely, it would regress ad infinitum. Therefore it must end with primary principles (al-awwaliyyāt) that are not acquired. These principles are not acquired because their terms are not conceived (i.e., they are self-evident) and the implication of those two conceptions for assent in affirming or negating one of the other is not acquired. The necessity of what follows from them is necessary, and the same holds for the third and fourth consequences. From this it follows that all sciences (including these primary principles) are absolutely not within human power. Thus, commanding them is commanding what cannot be accomplished (amr bi-mā lā yuṭāq), which is not permissible (ghayr jā'iz). If it were permissible, the foundation of the proof would be invalid. The argument establishes that the obligation to know God cannot be a command to acquire knowledge that is not within one's power.

Key Terms
al-awwaliyyāt
ghayr muktasaba
amr bi-mā lā yuṭāq

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All knowledge is acquired through effort and learning.
Correction: Razi argues that some knowledge, specifically primary principles (al-awwaliyyāt), is self-evident and not acquired. The chain of acquired knowledge must end with these principles, which are not within human power.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that knowledge cannot be commanded?
A) Because all knowledge is acquired.
B) Because the chain of acquired knowledge ends with primary principles that are not within power.
C) Because primary principles are unattainable.
D) Because God does not command impossible things.

Key Takeaway
Razi proves that knowledge cannot be commanded because the chain of acquired knowledge ends with self-evident primary principles that are not within human power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a truth you cannot fully explain or prove, do you dismiss it as irrelevant, or do you accept it as a primary principle from which other knowledge follows? How does this lesson challenge your reliance on intellectual acquisition?
?
Is there a command you place on yourself (or on others) that requires knowledge beyond your power? Will you now release that burden, recognizing that some knowledge is given and not earned?
?
Razi argues that commanding the impossible is not permissible. Do you ever demand from yourself absolute certainty in matters of faith where only self-evident principles apply? How can you rest in the knowledge that God does not command what is beyond your capacity?

Definition of Correct and Incorrect Thinking (Fikr)
Al-Razi defines fikr as ordered tasdiqat and distinguishes correct from incorrect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's definition of fikr as the arrangement of affirmations and the criterion for correct thinking based on the correspondence of premises to reality.

Core Idea
Correct thinking requires both sound arrangement and truthful premises.

Al-Razi begins by defining fikr (thinking) as an ordered process of affirmations that lead to conclusions. But he immediately adds a crucial condition: the premises must correspond to reality. This lesson unpacks that definition and its implications for evaluating reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the concise definition of fikr and the condition for correctness.
Notice the concise style and key terms.
2
Identify the two components
Identify the definition of fikr (arrangement of affirmations) and the subsequent condition that separates correct from incorrect thinking.
The condition hinges on correspondence (mutabaqa) of premises to reality.
3
Reflect on the importance
Consider why correspondence of premises is necessary for correct thinking, not just logical structure. How does this differ from modern notions of validity?
This passage grounds Islamic epistemology in truth correspondence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الفكر هو ترتيب تصديقات يتوسل بها إلى تصديقات أخر ثم التصديقات المستلزمة إن كانت مطابقة المتعلقاتها فهو الفكر الصحيح وإلا فهو الفكر الفاسد
Issue: Thinking (al-fikr) is the arrangement of affirmations (tasdiqat) by means of which one arrives at other affirmations. Then the necessitating affirmations, if they correspond to their objects, then it is correct thinking; otherwise it is incorrect thinking.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage gives a concise definition of fikr (thinking or reflection) and distinguishes its correct and incorrect forms. Fikr is defined as an ordered arrangement of tasdiqat (affirmations or judgments) that serve as means to obtain other tasdiqat (the conclusions). The author then adds: if these necessitating tasdiqat (the premises) correspond to their objects (i.e., are true in reality), then the thinking is correct; if they do not correspond, it is incorrect. This emphasizes that truth of premises is essential for correct thinking, in addition to the formal arrangement.

Misconception: Some think that any thinking process that results in a conclusion is correct thinking.
Correction: Al-Razi clarifies that correct thinking additionally requires that the premises (necessitating affirmations) actually correspond to reality. Without that correspondence, even a well-arranged argument yields incorrect thinking.

Checkpoint

B) The correspondence of the affirming premises to reality.
C) The number of premises.
D) The intention of the thinker.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi defines fikr as an ordered arrangement of affirmations that is correct only when its premises correspond to reality.

Defining Thought and Its Types
Imam Razi defines thought and distinguishes correct from faulty thought.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's definition of thought as an ordered arrangement of assertions and the criterion for correct versus faulty thought.

Core Idea
Thought is a purposeful arrangement of assertions aiming at truth.

Imam Razi lays the foundation for logical reasoning by defining what thought is and how we can judge its correctness. This passage presents a concise yet powerful definition that distinguishes valid reasoning from faulty reasoning based on the truth of premises.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the author's direct style come through.
Notice the phrase 'ترتيب تصديقات' – arrangement of assertions.
2
Understand the definition of thought
Thought is defined as an arrangement of assertions (tasdiqat) that serves as a means to arrive at other assertions. This highlights its instrumental and ordered nature.
3
Identify the criterion for correctness
The passage states that if the entailing assertions conform to their objects (reality), the thought is correct; otherwise faulty. This makes truth of premises the standard.
The word 'مطابقة' means conformity or correspondence.
4
Reflect on the implication
Thought is not just any mental process but a structured one aimed at truth. The validity depends on the truth of the premises, not just logical form.
5
Consider the common misunderstanding
Some think thought is any mental activity, but Razi specifies it as an ordered arrangement for a conclusion. This distinction is crucial for kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الفكر هو ترتيب تصديقات يتوسل بها إلى تصديقات أخر. ثم التصديقات المستلزمة إن كانت مطابقة لمتعلقاتها فهو الفكر الصحيح وإلا فهو الفكر الفاسد.
Issue: Thought is the arrangement of assertions by which one seeks to arrive at other assertions. Then the entailing assertions, if they conform to their objects, then it is correct thought; otherwise it is faulty thought.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage defines thought (al-fikr) and distinguishes its types. Imam Razi states that thought is the ordering of assertions (tasdiqat) in a way that serves as a means to arrive at other assertions. This is a concise definition highlighting the instrumental nature of thought. He then explains the criterion for correct and faulty thought: if the assertions that are entailed (i.e., the premises) match (muttabiqa) their objects (i.e., reality), then the thought is correct; otherwise it is faulty. Thus, the truth of the premises determines the validity of the thought process, not the logical form alone.

Correction: Razi defines thought specifically as an ordered arrangement of assertions used to arrive at other assertions, emphasizing its purposeful and structured nature.

Checkpoint

A) The number of premises.
B) The logical form of the argument.
C) Whether the entailing assertions conform to their objects.
D) The novelty of the conclusion.

Key Takeaway
Thought is correct when the assertions it uses match reality; otherwise it is faulty.
