Definition of Proof and Indicator
Razi defines proof and indicator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definitions of proof (dalil) and indicator (amara) in Islamic epistemology, and the key difference between certainty and conjecture.

Core Idea
Proof yields certainty; indicator yields only probability.

Imam Razi begins the third introduction of his work by clarifying two fundamental terms: proof and indicator. These concepts are essential for distinguishing between arguments that produce certain knowledge and those that only produce probable belief. Let's explore his definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the clear structure: first the heading, then the two definitions.
The passage is short but precise.
2
Identify the two definitions
There are exactly two definitions: one for proof (dalil) and one for indicator (amara). Underline the key verbs: 'يَلْزَمُ' (necessarily follows) in both.
Note the difference in what follows: 'العلم' (knowledge) vs. 'الظن' (conjecture).
3
Analyze the implications
Ask yourself: what does it mean for something to 'necessitate knowledge'? And what does it mean to 'necessitate conjecture'? The former is certain, the latter is probable.
4
Reflect on importance
Consider why this distinction is crucial for theology and law. Without it, we might mistake a weak indicator for a certain proof.
This distinction forms the basis of argument classification in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة في الدليل وأقسامه. مسألة تعريف الدليل والأمارة وبيان أقسامهما. الدليل: هو الذي يلزم من العلم به العلم بوجود المدلول، والأمارة: هو الذي يلزم من العلم بها ظن وجود المدلول.
The third introduction on the proof and its divisions. Issue: Definition of the proof and the indicator and explanation of their divisions. The proof: that from knowledge of which knowledge of the existence of the conclusion necessarily follows. The indicator: that from knowledge of which conjecture of the existence of the conclusion necessarily follows.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage introduces the third preliminary section of the work, focusing on proof (dalil) and its divisions. Imam Razi defines two key terms: proof (dalil) is something from knowledge of which knowledge of the existence of the conclusion (madlul) necessarily follows. That is, a proof entails certainty. The indicator (amara) is something from knowledge of which only conjecture (zann) of the conclusion follows, i.e., probability. This distinction is fundamental in Islamic epistemology and legal theory, as it separates demonstrative arguments from merely probabilistic indications.

Key Terms
الدليل
الأمارة
المدلول
العلم
الظن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Proof and indicator both lead to certain knowledge.
Correction: Imam Razi defines proof as leading to certain knowledge (ilm) and indicator as leading only to conjecture (zann). They are epistemologically different.

Checkpoint
What is the difference between a proof (dalil) and an indicator (amara) according to Imam Razi?
A) A proof is always true; an indicator is always false.
B) A proof leads to knowledge; an indicator leads to conjecture.
C) A proof is used in theology; an indicator is used in law.
D) A proof is a single statement; an indicator is a group of statements.

Key Takeaway
The fundamental difference between proof and indicator is that proof produces certain knowledge while indicator produces only conjecture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you evaluate your own religious convictions, how many are based on proofs (certainty) versus indicators (conjecture)? Are you honest with yourself about which is which?
?
In your da'wah, do you clearly distinguish between what is certain and what is probable? How might failing to do so affect the integrity of your call?
?
Consider a belief you once thought was certain. After this lesson, can you honestly say it rests on a proof (dalil) or merely an indicator (amara)? What would it take to ground it in certainty?

Knowledge of Indication vs. Knowledge of the Indicated
Razi distinguishes three types of knowledge and argues against circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between three types of knowledge in reasoning from evidence and why relational knowledge cannot be the source of knowledge of the indicated.

Core Idea
Knowing the proof is not the same as knowing the proven.

In theological reasoning, it's crucial to distinguish between knowing the evidence, knowing the conclusion, and knowing that the evidence supports the conclusion. This lesson examines a classical dispute about these distinctions, as presented by Imam Razi, and explains why confusing them leads to circular reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Notice the structure: question, then threefold distinction, then argument.
The passage uses technical terms: وجه دلالة, ذات الدليل, ذات المدلول.
2
Identify the three kinds of knowledge
From the passage, list the three types of knowledge mentioned. Understand what each one refers to.
The first two are straightforward; the third is relational.
3
Understand the argument against circularity
Focus on the last part: why cannot the relational knowledge entail knowledge of the indicated? Trace the dependency.
The key point is that knowledge of a relation depends on knowledge of both relata.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this distinction matters for sound reasoning. How does it prevent circularity?
This guards against a common fallacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة اختلفوا في أن العلم بوجه دلالة الدليل على المدلول هل هو عين العلم بالمدلول أم لا ) والحق أن هنا أمورا ثلاثة : العلم بذات الدليل كالعلم بإمكان العالم ، والعلم بذات المدلول كالعلم بأنه لا بد له من مؤثر ، والعلم يكون الدليل دليلا على المدلول، أما العلم بذات الدليل فهو مغاير للعلم بذات المدلول ومستلزم له، وأما العلم يكون الدليل دليلا على المدلول فهو مغاير أيضا للعلم بذات الدليل والمدلول لأنه علم بإضافة أمر إلى أمر والإضافة بين الشيئين مغايرة لهما فالعلم بها مغاير للعلم بهما . ولا يجوز أن يكون المستلزم للعلم بالمدلول هو العلم يكون الدليل دليلا عليه لأن العلم بإضافة أمر إلى أمر يتوقف على العلم بالمتضايفين فالعلم يكون الدليل دليلا على المدلول يتوقف على العلم بوجود المدلول ، فلو كان العلم بوجود المدلول مستفادا من العلم يكون الدليل دليلا عليه لزم الدور وأنه محال وبالله التوفيق .
A question: They differed on whether knowledge of the indication (وجه دلالة الدليل) of the evidence upon the indicated is identical to knowledge of the indicated or not. The truth is that there are three things: knowledge of the essence of the evidence, like knowledge of the possibility of the world; knowledge of the essence of the indicated, like knowledge that it must have an agent; and knowledge that the evidence is evidence for the indicated. As for knowledge of the essence of the evidence, it is different from knowledge of the essence of the indicated and entails it. And as for knowledge that the evidence is evidence for the indicated, it is also different from knowledge of the essence of the evidence and the indicated, because it is knowledge of the relation of one thing to another, and the relation between two things is different from them, so knowledge of it is different from knowledge of them. And it is not permissible for that which entails knowledge of the indicated to be the knowledge that the evidence is evidence for it, because knowledge of the relation of one thing to another depends on knowledge of the two relatives; so knowledge that the evidence is evidence for the indicated depends on knowledge of the existence of the indicated. Thus, if knowledge of the existence of the indicated were derived from knowledge that the evidence is evidence for it, circularity would arise, and that is impossible. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage presents a disputational question (مسألة) in theological epistemology: whether the knowledge of the indication (وجه دلالة الدليل) of an evidence for its indicated is identical to the knowledge of the indicated itself. The author (Imam Razi) gives the correct answer by distinguishing three distinct types of knowledge. First, knowledge of the essence of the evidence (e.g., knowing that the world is possible). Second, knowledge of the essence of the indicated (e.g., knowing that the world must have an agent). Third, knowledge that the evidence is evidence for the indicated—a relational knowledge (علم إضافي). The first two are different from each other, and the first entails the second. The third is also different because it is a relation between two things. This relational knowledge cannot be what entails knowledge of the indicated, because it depends on knowledge of the two relatives (المتضايفين), i.e., both the evidence and the indicated. Therefore, if knowledge of the indicated were derived from this relational knowledge, circularity would occur. The passage ends with 'وبالله التوفيق' (and with God is success). The argument is carefully structured to avoid circularity in reasoning.

Key Terms
علم بوجه دلالة الدليل
علم بذات الدليل
علم بذات المدلول
الإضافة بين الشيئين
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that knowing that a proof is valid automatically gives knowledge of the conclusion.
Correction: Razi shows that knowing the proof's validity (relational knowledge) depends on already knowing both the proof and the conclusion, so it cannot be the source of the conclusion's knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, how many kinds of knowledge are involved in the relation between evidence and indicated?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of the indication (relational) is distinct from and dependent on knowledge of both the evidence and the indicated, preventing circular reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Where in your own life have you assumed that having the right arguments or proofs means you truly know God? What is missing in that assumption, and what would it take to move from intellectual assent to real knowledge of the One indicated?
?
Razi warns against circular reasoning—proving something by presupposing it. Is there a belief you hold about your faith that you have never examined for circularity? Ask yourself: Does my confidence in this matter rest on evidence that points beyond itself, or have I unknowingly locked myself in a loop? What would honest self-examination reveal?
?
This lesson distinguishes three types of knowledge: knowledge of the evidence, knowledge of the indicated, and knowledge of the relation between them. Honestly, which of these three is weakest in your relationship with your Creator? Will you commit this week to strengthening that specific kind of knowledge, and how?

Definition of Evidence and Indicator and Their Divisions
Razi defines dalil and amara, and classifies them into rational, transmitted, and composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of dalil and amara, their threefold division into rational, transmitted, and composite, and the logical relationships (luzum) that ground rational inference.

Core Idea
Evidence yields certainty; indicator yields probability; both require rational or transmitted foundations.

This lesson introduces two fundamental tools of theological reasoning: evidence (dalil) and indicator (amara). Razi defines them based on the certainty or probability they produce, then classifies them into rational, transmitted, and composite. You will see how rational inference depends on necessary relationships (luzum) between concepts, and why purely transmitted reports are not self-sufficient.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of dalil and amara
Begin by reading the Arabic and translation to grasp the precise wording: dalil yields certain knowledge, amara yields probability (zann).
2
Identify the threefold classification
Notice that each of dalil and amara is divided into purely rational, purely transmitted (sam‘i), and composite. This is the basis for all theological arguments.
3
Examine the rational subtypes
The rational type is explained in detail: the consequence (luzum) must hold. There are several patterns: conditioned to condition, cause to effect, effect to cause (if equality proven), one effect to another, or one necessary consequent to the other.
These patterns illustrate the logical relations that make an argument valid.
4
Consider the status of transmitted and composite
The purely transmitted is not self-sufficient because it requires rational confirmation of the reporter's truth. The composite is obvious.
5
Engage with the commentary
Read the author's correction: the definition should be based on contemplation (nazar) not mere knowledge, and note the critique of redundancy in the classification of mutual necessitants.
This commentary refines the earlier definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة في الدليل وأقسامه ) مسألة تعريف الدليل والأمارة وبيان أقسامهما ) الدليل : هو الذي يلزم من العلم به العلم بوجود المدلول ، والأمارة هو الذي يلزم من العلم بها ظن وجود المدلول ، وكل واحد منهما إما أن يكون عقليا محضا أو سمعيا محضا أو مركبا منهما ، أما العقل فلابد وأن يكون بحيث يلزم من وجوده وجود المدلول، فاللزوم حاصل لا محالة من هذا الطرف ، فإن لم يحصل من الطرف الآخر فهو الاستدلال بالمشروط على الشرط كالاستدلال بالعلم على الحياة ، وإن حصل من الآخر فهو الاستدلال بالعلة المعينة على المعلول المعين والمعلول المعين على العلة المطلقة أو المعينة إن ثبت التساوي بدليل منفصل أو أحد المعلولين على الثاني وهو مركب من الأولين أو بأحد المتلازمين على الآخر كالمتضايفين ، أما السمعي المحض فلا حال لأن خبر الغير ما لم يعرف بالعقل صدقه لم يفد ، وأما المركب فظاهر . أقول: الصواب أن يقال : الدليل هو الذي يلزم من النظر فيه العلم بالمدلول ، فإن من المدلول ما لا وجود له ويستدل عليه كنفي العلم الذي يستدل عليه بنفي الحياة ، وكذلك الدليل والأمارة هي التي يلزم من النظر فيها الظن بالمدلول. وأما قوله « فاللزوم حاصل لا محالة من هذا الطرف وإن حصل من الطرف الآخر فهو كذا وكذا ، فلا شك في أن اللزوم إذا حصل من الطرفين كان الطرفان متلازمين ثم قوله بعد ذلك, أو بأحد الملازمين على الآخر ، هو عين ما قاله أولا لأنه قسيمه، والاختلاف بينهما ليس إلا بالأمثلة والتلازم بين المضافين في الحقيقة ليس مغايرا لما ذكر في العلة والمعلول، لأن ذات كل واحد من المضافين علة للإضافة المتعلقة بالآخر فهو نوع من دلالة العلة على المعلول ، إلا أنه واقع مرتين في الجانبين والباقي ظاهر
The third introduction: On the evidence and its divisions. A question: Definition of the evidence (dalil) and the indicator (amara) and clarification of their divisions. The evidence: it is that from knowledge of which knowledge of the existence of the indicated follows; the indicator: it is that from knowledge of which an opinion (ẓann) of the existence of the indicated follows. Each of them is either purely rational (ʿaqliyyan maḥḍan), or purely transmitted (samʿiyyan maḥḍan), or composite (murakkaban min humā). As for the rational, it must be such that from its existence the existence of the indicated follows. So the consequence (luzūm) necessarily occurs from this side. If it does not occur from the other side, then it is inference from the conditioned to the condition, like inferring knowledge from life. If it occurs from the other side, then it is inference from a specific cause to a specific effect, or from a specific effect to an absolute or specific cause if equality is established by a separate proof, or from one of two effects to the other—which is composite from the first two—or from one of two necessary consequents (mutalāzimayn) to the other, like the two relatives (mutaḍāifayn). As for the purely transmitted, it has no status because the report of another, unless its truth is known rationally, does not yield anything. As for the composite, it is obvious. I say: The correct thing is to say: The evidence is that from contemplation (naẓar) of which knowledge of the indicated follows, for among the indicated is that which has no existence and is inferred, like the negation of knowledge which is inferred from negation of life. Similarly, the evidence and indicator are that from contemplation of which opinion (ẓann) of the indicated follows. As for his statement: 'so the consequence necessarily occurs from this side' and if it occurs from the other side it is such and such, there is no doubt that if the consequence occurs from both sides, then the two sides are mutual necessitants. Then his subsequent statement 'or from one of the two necessary consequents to the other' is exactly what he said earlier because it is its counterpart, and the difference between them is only in examples. And the mutual necessitation between the two relatives (mutaḍāifayn) in reality is not different from what was mentioned regarding cause and effect, because the essence of each of the two relatives is the cause of the relation pertaining to the other, so it is a type of inference from cause to effect, except that it occurs twice on both sides. And the rest is obvious.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification (تعريف وأقسام) of two key terms in kalam methodology: الدليل (evidence) and الأمارة (indicator). The author defines dalil as that from knowledge of which certain knowledge of the indicated follows, while amara yields only probability (ظن). He then divides each into three types: purely rational, purely transmitted (sam‘i), and composite. The author explains the rational type in detail: the logical relationship (luzūm) must hold. If the consequence is one-way, it is inference from conditioned to condition (e.g., knowledge is conditioned on life). If two-way, it is inference from cause to effect, or effect to cause (if equality is proven), or from one effect to another (composite), or from one correlative to the other (like the two relatives). As for the purely transmitted, it is not self-sufficient because it requires rational verification of the reporter’s truthfulness. The composite type is obvious. Then a commentary (أقول) follows, offering a correction: the definition should be from 'contemplation' (النظر) rather than mere 'knowledge', and points out that the author’s classification of one of the correlatives to the other is redundant with the earlier case. The commentary also clarifies that the relation between relatives is a type of cause-effect relation twice over. This lesson is crucial for understanding how theologians classify arguments.

أمارة

عقلي محض
سمعي محض
مركب
علة
معلول
متلازمان
متضايفان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any report (khabar) can be evidence on its own.
Correction: The author clarifies that purely transmitted evidence requires rational verification of the reporter's truthfulness; without that, it has no status.

Checkpoint
What is the difference between dalil and amara according to the definition in this lesson?
A) Dalil yields certainty, amara yields probability
B) Dalil is rational, amara is transmitted
C) Dalil is from God, amara is from humans
D) Dalil requires no conditions, amara requires many

Key Takeaway
In theological reasoning, evidence (dalil) produces certainty, while indicator (amara) produces only probability; arguments are classified as rational, transmitted, or composite, with rational arguments relying on necessary logical connections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your personal study of Islam, are you content with 'probability' (ẓann) on matters where Allah demands certainty? Name one belief you hold that you have only from an indicator (amara) rather than a proof (dalil). Will you now seek the evidence until certainty is reached?
?
This passage teaches that even a transmitted report requires rational verification. Have you ever accepted a religious claim purely because you heard it from a respected speaker, without checking its rational basis? How will you guard against this blind acceptance?
?
The author distinguishes between two types of knowledge: certain knowledge from dalil and probable opinion from amara. Examine your heart: Are you more attached to comfortable opinions that reassure you, or do you genuinely pursue the hard path of evidence? What step will you take this week to replace an opinion with certainty?

The First Disagreement: Do Non-Existent Substances Occupy Space?
Imam Razi reports three views on non-existent substance and space.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three positions on whether non-existent substances are described by occupying space.

Core Idea
Substance is described by both substantiality and occupying space, but disagreement arises before existence.

Imam Razi begins by stating a shared premise: a substance is described by substantiality and by occupying space. Then he reports three distinct views on what this means for the substance before it exists. This passage sets the stage for deeper debates about the nature of non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the premise
Notice that Imam Razi starts with an agreed point: substance is described by both substantiality (jawhariyya) and occupying space (tahayyuz).
This is the baseline before disagreements.
2
Identify the three opinions
Find the views of al-Shahham, Abu Abdullah al-Basri, and Ibn Ayyash. Understand how each relates substance, space, and existence.
Al-Shahham says non-existent is present in space; Abu Abdullah says described but not present; Ibn Ayyash says no attributes at all.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider what these positions imply about the nature of non-existence and the relationship between attributes and existence.
This impacts later arguments about God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذات الجوهر كما أنها موصوفة بالجوهرية فهى موصوفة بالتحيز ثم اختلفا . فذهب الشحام إلى أن الجوهر حال عدمه حاصل فى الحيز قبل الوجود ، وذهب أبو عبد الله إلى أن الشرط فى كون المتحيز حاصلا في الحيز هو الوجود، فإن الجوهر قبل وجوده موصوف بالتحيز ولكنه غير حاصل فى الحيز . وزعم ابن عياش أن الجوهر حال العدم كما يمتنع اتصافه بالتحيز يمتنع اتصافه بالجوهرية فلهذا ثبت الذوات خالية عن الصفات . وثانيها : اختلفوا في أن المعدوم هل له بكونه معدوما صفة ؟ فالكل أنكروه إلا أبا عبد الله البصرى فإنه قال به . وثالثها : اتفقوا على أن الجواهر المعدومة لا توصف بأنها أجسام حال العدم إلا أبا الحسين الخياط فإنه قال به . ورابعها : انوا على أن بعد العلم بأن للعالم صانعا عالما قادرا حيا حكيما مرسلا للرسل يمكننا الشك في أنه هل هو موجود أولا إلى أن يعرف ذلك بالدليل ، لأنهم لما جوزوا اتصاف المعدوم بالصفة لم يلزم من اتصاف ذات الله تعالى بصفة العالمية والقادرية كونه موجودا فلا بد من دلالة منفصلة واتفق الباقون من العقلاء على أن ذلك جهالة وإلا لزم أن لا يعرف وجود الأجسام المتحركة والساكنة إلا بالدليل وبالله التوفيق (1) أقول هذا نقل المذاهب وليس فيه موضع بحث ، والفائلون بأن الماهيات غير مجمولة لم يقولوا بأنها غير مبدعة ، بل قالوا إذا فرضت ماهية فكونها تلك الماهية لا يكون مجمل جاعل وهذه ضرورة تلحقها بعد فرضها تلك الماهية . وقول المعتزلة : إن تأثير الفاعل ليس فى جعل الذوات ذواتا ليس هكذا لأنهم يجعلون الذوات المعدومة ثابتة فى الأزل من غير تأثير فاعل، ولما جعلوا الذوات متساوية في الذاتية احتاجوا إلى إثبات صفات الأجناس وإلا فكان الكل نوعا واحدا، والأعراض المشروطة بالحياة هى الاعتقادات والظنون والأنظار والقدر والشهوات والنفارات والآلام والإرادات والكراهات وهى مع الحياة عشرة ، والموت عند أبى على أيضا منها . والنحيز هي الصفة المختصة بالجواهر التى لأجلها يحتاج إلى حيز وتقتضيها الجوهرية وهى مشروطة بالوجود أما الكائنية المعللة بالحصول فى الحيز ككون الجوهر متحركا أو ساكنا أو مجتمعا أو متفرقا وهى معللة بالأكوان التى هى الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق بشرط الوجود والاتصاف بالوجود يكون بالفاعل والأعراض بدل التحيز ، والحصول في الحيز صفة واحدة لأجلها يحتاج إلى محل : وأدلة كل قوم منهم والكلام فيها وعليها كثيرة لكنها قليلة الفائدة فلنعرض عنها
The essence of the substance, just as it is described by substantiality (al-jawhariyya), is described by occupying space (al-tahayyuz). Then they disagreed. Al-Shahham held that the substance in the state of its non-existence is present in space (al-hayyiz) before existence. Abu Abdullah held that the condition for the occupier of space to be present in space is existence; for the substance before its existence is described by occupying space but it is not present in space. Ibn Ayyash claimed that the substance in the state of non-existence, just as it is impossible to describe it by occupying space, it is impossible to describe it by substantiality; thus the atoms are established as devoid of attributes. The second: they disagreed about whether the non-existent (al-ma'dum) has, by virtue of its being non-existent, an attribute. All denied it except Abu Abdullah al-Basri, for he affirmed it. The third: they agreed that non-existent substances are not described as bodies in the state of non-existence except Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat, for he affirmed it. The fourth: they agreed that after knowledge that the world has a Maker, Knowing, Powerful, Living, Wise, Sender of messengers, we can doubt whether He is existent or not until that is known by proof, because since they allowed the description of the non-existent with an attribute, it does not follow from the description of God's Essence with the attribute of knowledge and power that He is existent; thus a separate indication is necessary. The rest of the rational people agreed that such is ignorance; otherwise it would be necessary that the existence of moving and stationary bodies be known only by proof. And with God is success. (1) I say: This is a transmission of the schools, and there is no place for investigation in it. Those who say that essences (al-mahiyyat) are not made do not say they are not created; rather they said: if a quiddity is assumed, its being that quiddity is not a specific maker, and this necessity attaches to it after its assumption as that quiddity. The statement of the Mu'tazila that the agent's effect is not in making the atoms atoms is not so, because they make the non-existent atoms established in eternity without the influence of an agent. When they made the atoms equal in atomity, they needed to affirm the attributes of genera (sifat al-ajnas), otherwise everything would be one type. The accidents conditional on life are beliefs, opinions, reflections, power, desires, aversions, pains, wills, and hatreds; they are with life ten; death according to Abu Ali is also among them. Al-tahayyuz is the attribute specific to substances on account of which they need space, and substantiality necessitates it, and it is conditional on existence. As for the contingent (al-ka'iniyya) explained by occurrence in space, like the substance being moving or stationary or united or separated, they are explained by the contingencies (al-akwan) which are motion, rest, union, and separation, conditional on existence. Attribution of existence is by the agent, and accidents are instead of occupying space. Occurrence in space is a single attribute on account of which it needs a locus. The proofs of each group among them and the discussion about them and against them are many but of little benefit, so we turn away from them.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a survey of theological opinions regarding whether non-existent substances can be described with attributes. It begins by stating that a substance is described both by substantiality (jawhariyya) and by occupying space (tahayyuz). Then it records three disagreements: First, al-Shahham says a non-existent substance is already present in space (hayyiz) before creation, Abu Abdullah says it is described by tahayyuz but not actually present until existence, and Ibn Ayyash says that if a non-existent cannot occupy space, it also cannot be described as a substance at all, implying atoms are devoid of attributes. Second, the question whether 'non-existence' itself is an attribute: most deny it except Abu Abdullah al-Basri. Third, agreement that non-existent substances are not called bodies except Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat. Fourth, a practical consequence: if the non-existent can have attributes, then God's attributes (knowledge, power) do not entail His existence; thus a separate proof for God's existence is needed. The rest of the rationalists call this ignorance. The author's comment ('Aqul') clarifies that this is just a transmission of views, not an investigation. He then critiques the Mu'tazila position: they say essences are not made, but in fact they treat non-existent atoms as eternally established without an agent. Needing to differentiate types, they postulate 'attributes of genera' (sifat al-ajnas). He lists the ten life-conditioned accidents (beliefs, opinions, etc.) including death according to Abu Ali. 'Tahayyuz' is the attribute requiring space and is conditional on existence. The 'contingent' (ka'iniyya) properties like motion, rest, union, separation are explanations of occurrence in space. Existence is due to the agent, and accidents substitute for tahayyuz. Occurrence in space is a single need, and there is much discussion but little benefit, so he will omit it.

الجوهر
التحيز
الجوهرية
الحال
المعدوم

Key Takeaway

Three More Disputes: Attribute of Non-Existence, Bodies, and God's Existence
Imam Razi reports three more disputes on non-existent substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the debate over non-existent substances led to disagreements on whether non-existence is an attribute, whether non-existent substances are bodies, and the implication for proving God's existence.

Core Idea
If non-existence can be an attribute, then God's attributes do not entail His existence.

After the first disagreement on space, Imam Razi lists three further disputes. They concern whether non-existence itself is an attribute, whether non-existent substances can be called bodies, and a far-reaching consequence: if non-existent things can have attributes, then God's attributes of knowledge and power do not prove He exists. This shows how theological positions on minor points can affect fundamental beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Read the three disputes
Read the passage identifying the second (attribute of non-existence), third (bodies), and fourth (consequence for God's existence) opinions.
2
Understand the fourth dispute
Focus on the argument: if the non-existent can have attributes, then God's attributes don't entail His existence, requiring a separate proof.
This was considered ignorance by the majority.
3
Reflect on the chain
Consider how these disputes are interconnected and why they matter for theology.

Source Text

اختلفوا في أن المعدوم هل له بكونه معدوما صفة ؟ فالكل أنكروه إلا أبا عبد الله البصرى فإنه قال به . وثالثها : اتفقوا على أن الجواهر المعدومة لا توصف بأنها أجسام حال العدم إلا أبا الحسين الخياط فإنه قال به . ورابعها : انوا على أن بعد العلم بأن للعالم صانعا عالما قادرا حيا حكيما مرسلا للرسل يمكننا الشك في أنه هل هو موجود أولا إلى أن يعرف ذلك بالدليل ، لأنهم لما جوزوا اتصاف المعدوم بالصفة لم يلزم من اتصاف ذات الله تعالى بصفة العالمية والقادرية كونه موجودا فلا بد من دلالة منفصلة واتفق الباقون من العقلاء على أن ذلك جهالة وإلا لزم أن لا يعرف وجود الأجسام المتحركة والساكنة إلا بالدليل وبالله التوفيق (1) أقول هذا نقل المذاهب وليس فيه موضع بحث ، والفائلون بأن الماهيات غير مجمولة لم يقولوا بأنها غير مبدعة ، بل قالوا إذا فرضت ماهية فكونها تلك الماهية لا يكون مجمل جاعل وهذه ضرورة تلحقها بعد فرضها تلك الماهية . وقول المعتزلة : إن تأثير الفاعل ليس فى جعل الذوات ذواتا ليس هكذا لأنهم يجعلون الذوات المعدومة ثابتة فى الأزل من غير تأثير فاعل، ولما جعلوا الذوات متساوية في الذاتية احتاجوا إلى إثبات صفات الأجناس وإلا فكان الكل نوعا واحدا، والأعراض المشروطة بالحياة هى الاعتقادات والظنون والأنظار والقدر والشهوات والنفارات والآلام والإرادات والكراهات وهى مع الحياة عشرة ، والموت عند أبى على أيضا منها . والنحيز هي الصفة المختصة بالجواهر التى لأجلها يحتاج إلى حيز وتقتضيها الجوهرية وهى مشروطة بالوجود أما الكائنية المعللة بالحصول فى الحيز ككون الجوهر متحركا أو ساكنا أو مجتمعا أو متفرقا وهى معللة بالأكوان التى هى الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق بشرط الوجود والاتصاف بالوجود يكون بالفاعل والأعراض بدل التحيز ، والحصول في الحيز صفة واحدة لأجلها يحتاج إلى محل : وأدلة كل قوم منهم والكلام فيها وعليها كثيرة لكنها قليلة الفائدة فلنعرض عنها
The essence of the substance, just as it is described by substantiality (al-jawhariyya), is described by occupying space (al-tahayyuz). Then they disagreed. Al-Shahham held that the substance in the state of its non-existence is present in space (al-hayyiz) before existence. Abu Abdullah held that the condition for the occupier of space to be present in space is existence; for the substance before its existence is described by occupying space but it is not present in space. Ibn Ayyash claimed that the substance in the state of non-existence, just as it is impossible to describe it by occupying space, it is impossible to describe it by substantiality; thus the atoms are established as devoid of attributes. The second: they disagreed about whether the non-existent (al-ma'dum) has, by virtue of its being non-existent, an attribute. All denied it except Abu Abdullah al-Basri, for he affirmed it. The third: they agreed that non-existent substances are not described as bodies in the state of non-existence except Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat, for he affirmed it. The fourth: they agreed that after knowledge that the world has a Maker, Knowing, Powerful, Living, Wise, Sender of messengers, we can doubt whether He is existent or not until that is known by proof, because since they allowed the description of the non-existent with an attribute, it does not follow from the description of God's Essence with the attribute of knowledge and power that He is existent; thus a separate indication is necessary. The rest of the rational people agreed that such is ignorance; otherwise it would be necessary that the existence of moving and stationary bodies be known only by proof. And with God is success. (1) I say: This is a transmission of the schools, and there is no place for investigation in it. Those who say that essences (al-mahiyyat) are not made do not say they are not created; rather they said: if a quiddity is assumed, its being that quiddity is not a specific maker, and this necessity attaches to it after its assumption as that quiddity. The statement of the Mu'tazila that the agent's effect is not in making the atoms atoms is not so, because they make the non-existent atoms established in eternity without the influence of an agent. When they made the atoms equal in atomity, they needed to affirm the attributes of genera (sifat al-ajnas), otherwise everything would be one type. The accidents conditional on life are beliefs, opinions, reflections, power, desires, aversions, pains, wills, and hatreds; they are with life ten; death according to Abu Ali is also among them. Al-tahayyuz is the attribute specific to substances on account of which they need space, and substantiality necessitates it, and it is conditional on existence. As for the contingent (al-ka'iniyya) explained by occurrence in space, like the substance being moving or stationary or united or separated, they are explained by the contingencies (al-akwan) which are motion, rest, union, and separation, conditional on existence. Attribution of existence is by the agent, and accidents are instead of occupying space. Occurrence in space is a single attribute on account of which it needs a locus. The proofs of each group among them and the discussion about them and against them are many but of little benefit, so we turn away from them.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a survey of theological opinions regarding whether non-existent substances can be described with attributes. It begins by stating that a substance is described both by substantiality (jawhariyya) and by occupying space (tahayyuz). Then it records three disagreements: First, al-Shahham says a non-existent substance is already present in space (hayyiz) before creation, Abu Abdullah says it is described by tahayyuz but not actually present until existence, and Ibn Ayyash says that if a non-existent cannot occupy space, it also cannot be described as a substance at all, implying atoms are devoid of attributes. Second, the question whether 'non-existence' itself is an attribute: most deny it except Abu Abdullah al-Basri. Third, agreement that non-existent substances are not called bodies except Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat. Fourth, a practical consequence: if the non-existent can have attributes, then God's attributes (knowledge, power) do not entail His existence; thus a separate proof for God's existence is needed. The rest of the rationalists call this ignorance. The author's comment ('Aqul') clarifies that this is just a transmission of views, not an investigation. He then critiques the Mu'tazila position: they say essences are not made, but in fact they treat non-existent atoms as eternally established without an agent. Needing to differentiate types, they postulate 'attributes of genera' (sifat al-ajnas). He lists the ten life-conditioned accidents (beliefs, opinions, etc.) including death according to Abu Ali. 'Tahayyuz' is the attribute requiring space and is conditional on existence. The 'contingent' (ka'iniyya) properties like motion, rest, union, separation are explanations of occurrence in space. Existence is due to the agent, and accidents substitute for tahayyuz. Occurrence in space is a single need, and there is much discussion but little benefit, so he will omit it.

Key Terms
المعدوم
الصفة
الجوهر
الجسم
الدليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fourth dispute, why is a separate proof for God's existence needed?
A) Because God's attributes are different from His essence
B) Because if non-existent can have attributes, then God's attributes do not entail existence
C) Because the world does not prove God
D) Because the Mu'tazila deny God's attributes

Key Takeaway
The fourth dispute shows how the theory of attributes for non-existence impacts the fundamental Islamic belief in God's existence being self-evident from His attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you rely solely on logical proofs for God's existence, or does your heart sense His presence beyond arguments? How does this dispute challenge you to examine the basis of your own certainty?
?
If you were asked by someone who denies God to prove His existence, would you feel equipped to respond? This passage shows that even among Muslims, the proof of God's existence was debated. How can you strengthen your conviction so that it is not shaken by intellectual objections?
?
The fourth dispute warns that wrong assumptions about non-existence can lead to doubt about God. What hidden assumptions in your own thinking might unintentionally weaken your faith, and how will you guard against them?

What Is Kalam?
advanced
6 min
Author's Critique of Mu'tazila on Essences and Attributes of Genera
Imam Razi critiques Mu'tazila ontology and attributes of genera.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's critique of the Mu'tazila position on essences and why they introduced 'attributes of genera'.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila wrongly claim essences are not made, but they treat them as eternally established without an agent.

After reporting the various schools, Imam Razi adds his own analysis. He criticizes the Mu'tazila for saying that essences (mahiyyat) are not made by an agent, yet they posit non-existent atoms as eternally fixed. To avoid all atoms being identical, they invent 'attributes of genera' (sifat al-ajnas). This passage reveals the philosophical commitments behind the theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila position
Imam Razi says they claim the agent's effect is not making the atoms atoms, but the essence is eternal. Identify what he rejects.
He points out a contradiction.
2
The concept of 'attributes of genera'
Notice why the Mu'tazila needed these attributes: to differentiate types when all atoms are equal in essence.
3
Imam Razi's criticism
He points out that they still treat essences as caused in a way, but deny it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila deny creation entirely.
Correction: Imam Razi explains they only deny that the agent makes the essence as that essence; creation is still affirmed.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, why did the Mu'tazila need to posit 'attributes of genera'?
A) To prove God's existence
B) To differentiate between types of atoms that are otherwise identical
C) To explain motion
D) To deny creation

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi exposes a flaw in Mu'tazili thought: their ontology of non-existent atoms forces them to invent unnecessary attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the Mu'tazila posited atoms eternally established without an agent, do you realize that any philosophy that denies that everything other than God is created makes God less than the Sovereign? Examine your own heart: have you ever considered something—your talents, your relationships—as independently existing without constant creation by Allah? Repent from that thought and affirm that every moment of your existence is a new creation.
?
Imam Razi points out that the Mu'tazila needed to invent attributes of genera to avoid uniformity. Are there areas in your life where you invent justifications to avoid admitting that all goodness comes from Allah alone? Commit to tracing every good thing back to its true Source.
?
The lesson shows that even those who affirm creation (like the first group) still limit the agent's role in making quiddities. Do you ever act as if your own nature—your personality, your defaults—is 'just there' and not something that Allah continuously shapes? What would change if you saw your very quiddity as made by Him each moment?

What Is Kalam?

Accidents, Tahayyuz, and Ka'iniyya: Definitions and Closing Remarks
Imam Razi defines life-conditioned accidents, tahayyuz, and ka'iniyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the list of life-conditioned accidents, the definition of tahayyuz, and why Imam Razi omits detailed discussion.

Core Idea
Accidents conditioned by life include ten, and tahayyuz is a conditional attribute of substance.

This final passage from the section provides detailed definitions: the ten accidents that depend on life (including death according to Abu Ali), the nature of tahayyuz as a conditional attribute of substance, and the concept of ka'iniyya (contingent states). Imam Razi ends by dismissing further arguments as 'little benefit.' This lesson captures the terminological precision required in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Learn the ten accidents
Memorize the list: beliefs, opinions, reflections, power, desires, aversions, pains, wills, hatreds, and death (per Abu Ali). Note they are conditional on life.
2
Understand tahayyuz
Tahayyuz is the attribute requiring space, specific to substances, conditional on existence.
3
Understand ka'iniyya
Ka'iniyya refers to being in a state such as motion, rest, union, separation; explained by akwan (contingencies).
4
Note the author's closing
Imam Razi says proofs are many but of little benefit, so he omits them.

بالأكوان التى هى الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق بشرط الوجود والاتصاف بالوجود يكون بالفاعل والأعراض بدل التحيز ، والحصول في الحيز صفة واحدة لأجلها يحتاج إلى محل : وأدلة كل قوم منهم والكلام فيها وعليها كثيرة لكنها قليلة الفائدة فلنعرض عنها
The essence of the substance, just as it is described by substantiality (al-jawhariyya), is described by occupying space (al-tahayyuz). Then they disagreed. Al-Shahham held that the substance in the state of its non-existence is present in space (al-hayyiz) before existence. Abu Abdullah held that the condition for the occupier of space to be present in space is existence; for the substance before its existence is described by occupying space but it is not present in space. Ibn Ayyash claimed that the substance in the state of non-existence, just as it is impossible to describe it by occupying space, it is impossible to describe it by substantiality; thus the atoms are established as devoid of attributes. The second: they disagreed about whether the non-existent (al-ma'dum) has, by virtue of its being non-existent, an attribute. All denied it except Abu Abdullah al-Basri, for he affirmed it. The third: they agreed that non-existent substances are not described as bodies in the state of non-existence except Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat, for he affirmed it. The fourth: they agreed that after knowledge that the world has a Maker, Knowing, Powerful, Living, Wise, Sender of messengers, we can doubt whether He is existent or not until that is known by proof, because since they allowed the description of the non-existent with an attribute, it does not follow from the description of God's Essence with the attribute of knowledge and power that He is existent; thus a separate indication is necessary. The rest of the rational people agreed that such is ignorance; otherwise it would be necessary that the existence of moving and stationary bodies be known only by proof. And with God is success. (1) I say: This is a transmission of the schools, and there is no place for investigation in it. Those who say that essences (al-mahiyyat) are not made do not say they are not created; rather they said: if a quiddity is assumed, its being that quiddity is not a specific maker, and this necessity attaches to it after its assumption as that quiddity. The statement of the Mu'tazila that the agent's effect is not in making the atoms atoms is not so, because they make the non-existent atoms established in eternity without the influence of an agent. When they made the atoms equal in atomity, they needed to affirm the attributes of genera (sifat al-ajnas), otherwise everything would be one type. The accidents conditional on life are beliefs, opinions, reflections, power, desires, aversions, pains, wills, and hatreds; they are with life ten; death according to Abu Ali is also among them. Al-tahayyuz is the attribute specific to substances on account of which they need space, and substantiality necessitates it, and it is conditional on existence. As for the contingent (al-ka'iniyya) explained by occurrence in space, like the substance being moving or stationary or united or separated, they are explained by the contingencies (al-akwan) which are motion, rest, union, and separation, conditional on existence. Attribution of existence is by the agent, and accidents are instead of occupying space. Occurrence in space is a single attribute on account of which it needs a locus. The proofs of each group among them and the discussion about them and against them are many but of little benefit, so we turn away from them.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a survey of theological opinions regarding whether non-existent substances can be described with attributes. It begins by stating that a substance is described both by substantiality (jawhariyya) and by occupying space (tahayyuz). Then it records three disagreements: First, al-Shahham says a non-existent substance is already present in space (hayyiz) before creation, Abu Abdullah says it is described by tahayyuz but not actually present until existence, and Ibn Ayyash says that if a non-existent cannot occupy space, it also cannot be described as a substance at all, implying atoms are devoid of attributes. Second, the question whether 'non-existence' itself is an attribute: most deny it except Abu Abdullah al-Basri. Third, agreement that non-existent substances are not called bodies except Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat. Fourth, a practical consequence: if the non-existent can have attributes, then God's attributes (knowledge, power) do not entail His existence; thus a separate proof for God's existence is needed. The rest of the rationalists call this ignorance. The author's comment ('Aqul') clarifies that this is just a transmission of views, not an investigation. He then critiques the Mu'tazila position: they say essences are not made, but in fact they treat non-existent atoms as eternally established without an agent. Needing to differentiate types, they postulate 'attributes of genera' (sifat al-ajnas). He lists the ten life-conditioned accidents (beliefs, opinions, etc.) including death according to Abu Ali. 'Tahayyuz' is the attribute requiring space and is conditional on existence. The 'contingent' (ka'iniyya) properties like motion, rest, union, separation are explanations of occurrence in space. Existence is due to the agent, and accidents substitute for tahayyuz. Occurrence in space is a single need, and there is much discussion but little benefit, so he will omit it.

الحياة
التحيز
الكائنية
الأكوان
المتحيز

Checkpoint

A) Beliefs
B) Power
C) Colors
D) Desires

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi provides precise definitions of key terms before dismissing further debate as unproductive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Which of these—belief, opinion, reflection, power, desire, aversion, pain, will, or hatred—do you most consistently direct toward your own ego rather than toward Allah? What would it look like to transform that attribute into a life truly lived for Him?
?
The author dismisses many theological proofs as 'of little benefit.' Is there a religious debate or intellectual curiosity you cling to that actually numbs your heart and distracts you from acting on what you already know? Will you set it aside this week to focus on one concrete act of obedience?
?
You learned that even death is considered among the life-conditioned accidents by some scholars. Does your awareness of death condition your daily choices, or do you live as if it is irrelevant? What is one thing you would change if you truly believed that your next moment could be your last?

The Second Argument for the State (Hal): Generic Commonality
Imam Razi records the second argument for the state (حال) based on shared generic properties and provides a terminological comment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument for the existence of the state (hal) based on shared generic properties and why this commonality is not merely verbal.

Core Idea
Shared generic properties are real and not mere words, necessitating an intermediate state.

Imam Razi presents the second argument for the state (hal), which arises from the observation that different specific quiddities, such as blackness and whiteness, share common generic properties like color-ness. This commonality cannot be dismissed as mere verbal agreement because it is grounded in a real, perceived homogeneity. By examining this argument, we see how Ash'ari theologians use the concept of generic commonality to argue for a state that is neither existent nor non-existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: a premise, an explanatory test, and a concluding remark.
2
Identify the premise
The argument begins with the claim that specific quiddities share in genera. This is the foundation of the proof for the state.
3
Understand the counterfactual test
The author uses a mental experiment: if we used the same name for blackness and motion, we would still perceive a real similarity between blackness and whiteness that is absent between blackness and motion. This shows the commonality is real, not just verbal.
This test is crucial to the argument.
4
Consider the author's terminological note
Imam Razi clarifies that theologians use 'genus' and 'species' opposite to logicians. For them, 'species' (naw') refers to the more general and 'genus' (jins) to the more specific, based on linguistic roots.
This note helps avoid confusion when reading other texts.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The real commonality between blackness and whiteness (color-ness) is an attribute that is neither existent nor non-existent, thus it is a state. This argument supports the Ash'ari doctrine of the state.
Consider how this differs from the argument from the attribute of existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : الماهيات النوعية مشتركة فى الأجناس وذلك يوجب القول بالحال (۳) بيان الأول من وجوه أحدها : أن السواد والبياض اشتركا فى اللونية ، وليس الاشتراك في مجرد الاسم لانا لو سمينا السواد والحركة باسم واحد ولم نضع للسواد والبياض اسما واحدا لكنا نعلم بالضرورة أن بين السواد والبياض من المجانسة ما ليس بين السواد والحركة، ولذلك فإن الاشتراك اللفظى لا يكون مفردا في اللغات بأسرها، وهذا النوع من الاشتراك معلوم لكل العقلاء. (۳) أقول : اصطلاحهم في الجنس و النوع على عكس اصطلاح المنطقيين، فإنهم يسمون الأعم نوعا والأخص جنسا ، فإن التنوع فى اللغة الاختلاف ، والتجانس التماثل
The second proof: The specific quiddities (al-mahiyyat al-naw'iyya) share in the genera (al-ajnas). This necessitates affirming the state (al-hal). (3) Explanation of the first from several aspects: one is that blackness and whiteness share in color-ness (al-lawniyya). The sharing is not merely verbal, because if we were to name blackness and motion with one name and did not give blackness and whiteness a single name, we would know by necessity that there is between blackness and whiteness a kind of homogeneity (mujanasa) that is not between blackness and motion. Therefore verbal commonality is not prevalent in all languages, and this type of commonality is known to all rational people. (3) I say: Their convention regarding genus (jins) and species (naw') is opposite to the convention of the logicians; for they call the more general a species and the more specific a genus, for 'diversity' (tanawwu') in language means difference, and 'homogeneity' (tajanus) means similarity.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for the existence of a 'state' (hal) intermediate between existence and non-existence. The argument is that specific quiddities (e.g., blackness, whiteness) share in common genera (e.g., color). This commonality is not simply a matter of verbal agreement, because if we were to arbitrarily name blackness and motion with the same term, we would still perceive a genuine similarity between blackness and whiteness that is absent between blackness and motion. Thus, the common property (color-ness) is a real attribute that is 'neither existent nor non-existent'—a 'state'—since it is neither a separate existent nor a mere name. The author's comment ('Aqul') clarifies a terminological point: the theologians use 'genus' and 'species' in the opposite sense to logicians. For theologians, the more general is called 'species' (naw') and the more specific 'genus' (jins), deriving from language: 'diversity' (tanawwu') means difference, and 'homogeneity' (tajanus) means similarity.

Key Terms
الماهيات النوعية
الأجناس
الحال
اللونية
المجانسة
الجنس
النوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument for the state is based on mere verbal agreement.
Correction: The argument contends that the commonality is real, not just verbal, as shown by the perceived homogeneity between blackness and whiteness that cannot be replicated by arbitrary naming.

Checkpoint
In the second argument, why does the commonality between blackness and whiteness not reduce to a verbal agreement?
A) Because they share the same name in all languages
B) Because we perceive a real homogeneity between them that is not present between blackness and motion
C) Because they are both colors
D) Because the theologians define genus and species differently

Key Takeaway
The second argument for the state shows that the real commonality between specific quiddities in a genus cannot be explained by existence or non-existence alone, thus necessitating an intermediate state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the world, do you ever stop to consider that the categories you use (like color, shape, size) are not merely labels but reflect real, underlying attributes? How does acknowledging the reality of these shared properties affect your sense of the order and purpose in creation?
?
If you accept that there is a real commonality between blackness and whiteness in 'color-ness', what does that tell you about the source of that attribute? Could this reasoning lead you to affirm a single Creator who bestows such commonalities? Ponder how this argument from generic commonality points beyond itself to the unity of the divine attributes.
?
The theologians distinguished between verbal and real commonality. In your own understanding of faith, are there areas where you treat shared Islamic concepts as mere words rather than realities that demand a response? Commit to one way you will move from verbal acknowledgment to heartfelt conviction today.

Definitions of Body and Atom
Razi defines body and atom and notes a linguistic dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how early theologians defined 'body' and 'atom' and why their definitions varied.

Core Idea
Body is divisible; atom is not; but what counts as 'body' was debated.

This lesson introduces two foundational terms in Islamic theology: body and atom. Razi presents the standard definition and the Mu'tazilite alternative, showing that even basic vocabulary involved discussions. Understanding this debate is essential for grasping later arguments about the nature of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the structure: first the division, then the two definitions.
The word 'al-munajjaz' is unclear but context suggests 'the originated'.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find where Razi defines body as divisible and atom as indivisible, and where he reports the Mu'tazilite definition. Then note his own definition using composition.
3
Reflect on the linguistic discussion
Razi calls this a linguistic discussion. Ask yourself: do different definitions change the reality, or just the words used?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المنجز فقد قال المتكامون إنه إما أن يكون قابلا للانقسام أولا يكون والأول هو الجسم والثاني هو الجوهر الفرد. وعند المعتزلة اسم الجسم لا يقع إلا على الطويل العريض العميق وعلى ما قلناه الجسم ما فيه التأليف وأقله جوهران فهذا بحث لغوى
As for the [؟] (al-munajjaz), the theologians said that it is either divisible or not divisible; the first is the body (jism) and the second is the atom (jawhar fard). According to the Mu'tazila, the name 'body' does not apply except to what is long, wide, and deep; but according to what we said, the body is that which has composition (ta'līf), and its minimum is two atoms. This is a linguistic discussion.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage defines the terms 'body' and 'atom' (indivisible particle). Razi reports that theologians say an originated thing is either divisible (the body) or indivisible (the atom). The Mu'tazila restrict the name 'body' to something with three dimensions (length, width, depth). Razi then states his own view (or the Ash'ari view): the body is that which has composition (ta'līf), and its minimum is two atoms. He notes that this is a linguistic dispute, not a substantive one.

Key Terms
جسم
جوهر فرد
تأليف
انقسام
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All theologians agree on what a body is.
Correction: Different schools (like Mu'tazila vs Ash'aris) defined body differently; Razi notes it's a linguistic discussion.

Checkpoint

A) One atom
B) Two atoms
C) Three atoms
D) Eight atoms

Key Takeaway
The definitions of 'body' and 'atom' were debated among theologians, but the core distinction is between divisible and indivisible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held onto a specific definition of a religious term so tightly that it caused division with others, even though the disagreement was merely linguistic? How can you learn from Razi's attitude that this is a 'linguistic discussion' and not a fundamental issue of faith?
?
When you study theology, are you more concerned with the substance of the argument or with the labels and categories you use? What would it look like to prioritize understanding over winning a terminological debate?

Knowledge is Self-Evident
Razi argues that knowledge's conception is self-evident via two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi considers the conception of knowledge to be self-evident and the reasoning behind it.

Core Idea
Knowledge cannot be defined because everything else depends on it.

Imam Razi begins by addressing the definition of knowledge. He argues that any attempt to define knowledge is circular because knowledge is the tool by which we understand everything else. He then presents a personal introspective proof: we are necessarily aware of our own knowledge. This lesson presents his view that the conception of knowledge is self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the translation. Let Razi's own argumentative flow come through.
Notice the two distinct proofs introduced by 'because' and 'and because'.
2
Identify the two proofs
The first proof is based on the impossibility of defining knowledge by something else. The second proof is based on self-awareness of our own existence and knowledge.
The second proof uses the idea that the part of a self-evident whole is self-evident.
3
Understand the circularity argument
If knowledge were defined by something else, that something else would have to be known first, leading to circularity. Since all knowledge depends on knowledge, knowledge must be self-evident.
4
Reflect on self-awareness
Consider that you are directly aware of your own knowledge of your existence. This direct awareness is self-evident, and since knowledge is a part of it, knowledge too is self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : اختلفوا في حد العلم وعندى أن تصوره بديهى ، لأن ما عدا العلم لا ينكشف إلا به فيستحيل أن يكون كاشفا له ، ولأني أعلم بالضرورة كونى عالما بوجودى وتدور العلم جزء منه وجزء البديهى بدیهی فتصور العلم بدیهی . مسألة : قيل العلم سلبي وهو باطل لأنه لو كان كذلك لكان سلب ما ينافيه والمنافى إن كان عدميا كان هو عدم العلم فيكون ثبوتيا وإن كان وجوديا فعدمه يصدق على العلم فيكون العدم موصوفا بالعالمية هذا خلف وقيل إنه انطباع صورة مساوية المعلوم في العالم وهو باطل واللزم أن يكون العالم بالحرارة والبرودة حارا باردا . لا يقال : المنطبع صورته ومثاله . لانا نقول : الصورة والمثال إن كان مساويا فى تمام الماهية للمعلوم لزم المحذور والابطال قولهم نكتة أخرى : يلزم أن يكون الجدار الموصوف بالحرارة والبرودة عالما . لا يقال : حصول الماهية للشيء إما أن يكون إدراكا كما إذا كان الشيء عما من شأنه أن يكون مدركا . لانا نقول: إن كان الإدراك هو نفس الحصول فالمدرك هو الذى له الحصول، فكان الجدار من شأنه أن يدرك لا من شأنه أن يكون له الحصول . احتجوا : بأنا نميز بعض المعلومات عن بعض فوجب أن يكون ثابتا ، لأن العدم الصرف لا تميز فيه وإذ قد لا يكون المعلوم ثابتا في الخارج فهو في الذهن . جوابه : هذا يقتضى أن يكون المعلوم بتمام ماهيته حاضرا في الذهن فمن تخيل البحر فقد حضر في خياله
Issue: They differed concerning the definition of knowledge (ʿilm). In my view, its conception (taṣawwur) is self-evident (badīhī), because nothing besides knowledge is disclosed except by it, so it is impossible that it be a discloser of itself. And because I know necessarily that I am a knower of my existence, and knowledge is part of it, and the self-evident part is self-evident, so the conception of knowledge is self-evident. Issue: It was said that knowledge is negative (salbī), and it is false. Because if it were so, it would be the negation of what contradicts it, and the contradictory if it is non-existent (ʿadamī) would be the absence of knowledge, so it would be positive (thubūtī); and if it is existent (wujūdī), then its non-existence applies to knowledge, so non-existence would be described as 'knowing' — this is absurd (khalf). And it was said that it is the impression (inṭibāʿ) of a form equal to the known thing in the knower, and it is false, for it would necessitate that the knower of heat and cold be hot and cold. It cannot be said: what is impressed is its form and likeness. For we say: the form and likeness, if it is equal in complete essence (māhiyya) to the known, the problem follows. And there is another point invalidating their statement: it would necessitate that the wall described by heat and cold be a knower. It cannot be said: the obtaining (ḥuṣūl) of the essence for a thing is only an apprehension (idrāk) when the thing is of that which is apt to be apprehensive (mudrik). For we say: if apprehension is the very obtaining, then the apprehending thing is that which has the obtaining; so the wall would be of that which is apt to apprehend, not of that which is apt to have the obtaining. They argued: we distinguish some known things from others, so it must be that they are fixed (thābit), because pure non-existence has no distinction; and since the known thing may not be fixed externally, it is in the mind. Its answer: this necessitates that the known thing by its complete essence be present in the mind; so one who imagines the sea, the sea would be present in his imagination.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument on the definition of knowledge (ʿilm). First, Razi states his view that the conception of knowledge is self-evident (badīhī), because everything else is disclosed by knowledge, so knowledge cannot be disclosed by something else. He adds that we necessarily know that we know ourselves, and knowledge is part of that self-evident awareness. Second, he refutes the view that knowledge is a negative (salbī) attribute: if it were a negation of its opposite, then either the opposite is non-existent (making knowledge positive) or existent (making non-existence described as knowledge — absurd). Third, he refutes the theory that knowledge is the impression (inṭibāʿ) of an equal form of the known in the knower: this would imply that the knower of heat becomes hot, which is false. He then anticipates an objection that it is the form, not the essence, and responds that if the form is equal in essence, the problem remains. Another refutation: a wall described as hot or cold would then be a knower. Further objection: the presence of an essence in something is only apprehension when that thing is capable of apprehension. Razi responds that if apprehension is the presence itself, then the thing that has the presence would be the apprehender, so the wall would be apt to apprehend. Finally, he presents an argument from the fact that we distinguish known things, so they must be fixed in the mind, and answers that this would require the whole essence to be present in the mind, leading to absurdity (e.g., imagining the sea would make the sea present).

Key Terms
العلم
بديهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does Razi consider the conception of knowledge self-evident?
A) Because it is the most general concept.
B) Because nothing besides knowledge is disclosed except by it, so it cannot be disclosed by something else.
C) Because knowledge is a divine attribute.
D) Because it is difficult to define.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is self-evident because it is the means by which everything else is known and because we are directly aware of it within our own self-knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that your very awareness of self is built on knowledge that cannot be defined, does it humble you? Have you ever thanked Allah for the immediate certainty of your own existence as a starting point for knowing Him?
?
If knowledge is self-evident, then your doubts about its validity are not from knowledge itself but from something else. Do you have an area in your faith where you overcomplicate what is actually clear? What would change if you trusted the self-evidence of the truth?
?
You are constantly using knowledge to judge everything else, yet you cannot define it. Does this drive you to depend on Allah, the source of all knowledge, rather than on your own intellect? How can you live today with greater reliance on Him, knowing that even your mind is a gift that points back to its Giver?

Refutation of Negative and Impression Definitions
Razi refutes the negative definition and the impression theory of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi rejects both the negative definition of knowledge and the theory that knowledge is an impression of forms.

Core Idea
Knowledge is neither a negation nor an impression; both lead to absurdities.

After declaring knowledge self-evident, Razi attacks two alternative definitions: the view that knowledge is a negative attribute and the view that it is an impression of a form equal to the known. Through logical analysis, he shows both lead to contradictions. This lesson explores these refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of negative definition
Read the first part about 'قيل العلم سلبي' and understand the dilemma Razi presents: either the contradictory is non-existent or existent, both problematic.
Pay attention to the terms 'عدمي' and 'وجودي'.
2
Analyze the dilemma
If the contradictory is non-existent, negating it makes knowledge positive. If it is existent, then knowledge would negate existence, making non-existence the knower – absurd.
This shows the negative definition is self-contradictory.
3
Read the refutation of impression theory
Read from 'وقيل إنه انطباع صورة' to the end of the passage. Note the main point: the knower would become like the known.
Razi gives multiple arguments against this theory.
4
Consider Razi's additional points
Razi anticipates objections (e.g., it is the form not the essence) and refutes them. He also gives the example of the wall to show absurdity.
The wall argument and the response about 'حصول الماهية' are subtle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : اختلفوا في حد العلم وعندى أن تصوره بديهى ، لأن ما عدا العلم لا ينكشف إلا به فيستحيل أن يكون كاشفا له ، ولأني أعلم بالضرورة كونى عالما بوجودى وتدور العلم جزء منه وجزء البديهى بدیهی فتصور العلم بدیهی . مسألة : قيل العلم سلبي وهو باطل لأنه لو كان كذلك لكان سلب ما ينافيه والمنافى إن كان عدميا كان هو عدم العلم فيكون ثبوتيا وإن كان وجوديا فعدمه يصدق على العلم فيكون العدم موصوفا بالعالمية هذا خلف وقيل إنه انطباع صورة مساوية المعلوم في العالم وهو باطل واللزم أن يكون العالم بالحرارة والبرودة حارا باردا . لا يقال : المنطبع صورته ومثاله . لانا نقول : الصورة والمثال إن كان مساويا فى تمام الماهية للمعلوم لزم المحذور والابطال قولهم نكتة أخرى : يلزم أن يكون الجدار الموصوف بالحرارة والبرودة عالما . لا يقال : حصول الماهية للشيء إما أن يكون إدراكا كما إذا كان الشيء عما من شأنه أن يكون مدركا . لانا نقول: إن كان الإدراك هو نفس الحصول فالمدرك هو الذى له الحصول، فكان الجدار من شأنه أن يدرك لا من شأنه أن يكون له الحصول . احتجوا : بأنا نميز بعض المعلومات عن بعض فوجب أن يكون ثابتا ، لأن العدم الصرف لا تميز فيه وإذ قد لا يكون المعلوم ثابتا في الخارج فهو في الذهن . جوابه : هذا يقتضى أن يكون المعلوم بتمام ماهيته حاضرا في الذهن فمن تخيل البحر فقد حضر في خياله
Issue: They differed concerning the definition of knowledge (ʿilm). In my view, its conception (taṣawwur) is self-evident (badīhī), because nothing besides knowledge is disclosed except by it, so it is impossible that it be a discloser of itself. And because I know necessarily that I am a knower of my existence, and knowledge is part of it, and the self-evident part is self-evident, so the conception of knowledge is self-evident. Issue: It was said that knowledge is negative (salbī), and it is false. Because if it were so, it would be the negation of what contradicts it, and the contradictory if it is non-existent (ʿadamī) would be the absence of knowledge, so it would be positive (thubūtī); and if it is existent (wujūdī), then its non-existence applies to knowledge, so non-existence would be described as 'knowing' — this is absurd (khalf). And it was said that it is the impression (inṭibāʿ) of a form equal to the known thing in the knower, and it is false, for it would necessitate that the knower of heat and cold be hot and cold. It cannot be said: what is impressed is its form and likeness. For we say: the form and likeness, if it is equal in complete essence (māhiyya) to the known, the problem follows. And there is another point invalidating their statement: it would necessitate that the wall described by heat and cold be a knower. It cannot be said: the obtaining (ḥuṣūl) of the essence for a thing is only an apprehension (idrāk) when the thing is of that which is apt to be apprehensive (mudrik). For we say: if apprehension is the very obtaining, then the apprehending thing is that which has the obtaining; so the wall would be of that which is apt to apprehend, not of that which is apt to have the obtaining. They argued: we distinguish some known things from others, so it must be that they are fixed (thābit), because pure non-existence has no distinction; and since the known thing may not be fixed externally, it is in the mind. Its answer: this necessitates that the known thing by its complete essence be present in the mind; so one who imagines the sea, the sea would be present in his imagination.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument on the definition of knowledge (ʿilm). First, Razi states his view that the conception of knowledge is self-evident (badīhī), because everything else is disclosed by knowledge, so knowledge cannot be disclosed by something else. He adds that we necessarily know that we know ourselves, and knowledge is part of that self-evident awareness. Second, he refutes the view that knowledge is a negative (salbī) attribute: if it were a negation of its opposite, then either the opposite is non-existent (making knowledge positive) or existent (making non-existence described as knowledge — absurd). Third, he refutes the theory that knowledge is the impression (inṭibāʿ) of an equal form of the known in the knower: this would imply that the knower of heat becomes hot, which is false. He then anticipates an objection that it is the form, not the essence, and responds that if the form is equal in essence, the problem remains. Another refutation: a wall described as hot or cold would then be a knower. Further objection: the presence of an essence in something is only apprehension when that thing is capable of apprehension. Razi responds that if apprehension is the presence itself, then the thing that has the presence would be the apprehender, so the wall would be apt to apprehend. Finally, he presents an argument from the fact that we distinguish known things, so they must be fixed in the mind, and answers that this would require the whole essence to be present in the mind, leading to absurdity (e.g., imagining the sea would make the sea present).

Key Terms
العلم
سلب
انطباع صورة
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think knowledge is simply the negation of ignorance or that it is an impression of forms in the mind.
Correction: Razi shows these lead to logical contradictions; knowledge is self-evident and cannot be defined in such ways.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what problem arises from defining knowledge as the impression of a form equal to the known?
A) It would make knowledge eternal.
B) It would require the knower to become the known (e.g., the knower of heat must be hot).
C) It would make knowledge a substance.
D) It would conflict with divine knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the negative definition and impression theory by showing they lead to logical absurdities, reinforcing that knowledge must be self-evident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to 'know' something, have you ever considered that your concept of knowledge might be flawed? Razi shows that two common definitions lead to absurdities. How do you personally define knowledge? After reading this, can you still hold that definition without falling into absurdity? Today, examine one belief you hold and ask yourself: is my 'knowledge' of this based on a solid definition, or am I assuming something that might be internally contradictory?
?
Razi argues that knowledge cannot be merely a negation or an impression. Think of a time you thought you knew something but later realized you were mistaken. Was your 'knowledge' actually a negation of ignorance, or an impression of the truth? How does this lesson challenge you to examine the basis of your own beliefs? Commit to testing one of your deeply held assumptions this week using the kind of logical scrutiny Razi applies.
?
The impression theory suggests that to know something is to become like it. In your spiritual life, do you seek to become like what you know about Allah? Does your knowledge of His attributes transform your character, or is it merely intellectual assent? If you are not being changed by what you claim to know, what does that say about the reality of your knowledge? Today, ask Allah to make your knowledge of Him a true impression that molds you to reflect His names.

The Argument from Distinction and Razi's Answer
Razi presents and refutes an argument from distinction for mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that knowledge requires mental existence and Razi's refutation that it would imply the whole essence is present in the mind.

Core Idea
Distinguishing known things does not prove they exist in the mind by their complete essence.

The opponents present an argument: we distinguish known things, so they must be fixed (thabit) in the mind. Razi counters that this would require the entire essence of the known to be present in the mind, leading to absurd consequences like the sea being present in the imagination. This lesson examines this exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponents' argument
Read the part 'احتجوا' and understand their reasoning: distinction implies existence, and since not external, it must be mental.
The key concept is 'العدم الصرف لا تميز فيه'.
2
Understand Razi's answer
Read the 'جوابه' section. Razi argues that this would mean the full essence of the known is present in the mind, which is absurd (e.g., the sea in imagination).
This refutes the idea that mental existence is exactly like external existence.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why requiring the entire essence is problematic: imagining the sea would require the actual sea to be present in the mind, which is impossible.
This shows that mental representation is not the same as full essence presence.
