The Self-Evidence of Knowledge and Its Definition
The editor explains the true object of defining knowledge and denies circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of knowledge is 'knowledge of knowledge' and how this avoids circularity.

Core Idea
Defining knowledge is knowing knowledge itself, not a circular trap.

This editorial note addresses a possible objection: if knowledge is self-evident, can it be defined? The editor clarifies that the real goal is 'knowledge of knowledge,' and this does not require circularity. Read on to see how knowledge can disclose other things while other things disclose knowledge of it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the editor's claim.
Notice the key phrase 'al-ʿilm bi-al-ʿilm'.
2
Identify the key concept: al-ʿilm bi-al-ʿilm
Focus on what is sought from the definition: it is not knowledge itself but knowledge of knowledge. This is the target.
3
Ask: Is this circular?
The editor denies impossibility. Reflect on how knowledge can disclose other things and be disclosed by them.
This resolves the circularity objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : المطلوب من حد العلم هو العلم بالعلم وما عدا العلم ينكشف بالعلم لا بالعلم بالعلم ، وليس من المحال أن يكون هو كاشفا عن غيره وغيره كاشفا عن العلم به .
I say: What is sought from the definition of knowledge is knowledge of knowledge, and what is other than knowledge is disclosed by knowledge, not by knowledge of knowledge; and it is not impossible that it (knowledge) be a discloser of other things and other things be disclosers of knowledge of it.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This editorial note addresses a possible objection to Razi's claim that knowledge is self-evident. The editor clarifies that defining knowledge means knowing knowledge itself (al-ʿilm bi-al-ʿilm). It is not impossible for knowledge to disclose other things while other things disclose knowledge of it. This allows for a non-circular understanding: we know knowledge through other means, not through itself. So the self-evidence is about the conception, not the definition.

Key Terms
العلم بالعلم
كاشف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Defining knowledge leads to an infinite regress or circularity because you need knowledge to define knowledge.
Correction: The editor shows that the desired definition is 'knowledge of knowledge,' and this is not impossible because knowledge can be disclosed by other things, breaking the circularity.

Checkpoint
According to the editor, what is the desired object of defining knowledge?
A) Knowledge of knowledge.
B) Knowledge of the known.
C) The impression of forms.
D) A negative attribute.

Key Takeaway
The definition of knowledge is knowledge of knowledge, and this does not require circularity because knowledge and its objects can mutually disclose each other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The editor explains that knowledge is self-evident yet can be defined through second-order knowledge. Are you content to rely on the first-order knowledge you have about your faith, or have you ever honestly examined whether that knowledge is genuine? What would it take for you to move from 'knowledge of things' to 'knowledge of knowledge' in your most fundamental beliefs?
?
This passage argues that mutual disclosure avoids circularity. In your own life, do you ever find yourself trapped in circular reasoning about your beliefs—justifying one belief with another without ever grounding them? What is one belief you hold that you think is self-evident, and how might you test its foundation today?
?
Consider the claim that 'other things are disclosed by knowledge, not by knowledge of knowledge.' This means your direct knowledge of Allah's signs (the world) reveals Allah, not your abstract knowledge of what knowledge is. Are you using your direct knowledge of creation to deepen your knowledge of the Creator, or are you distracted by second-order reflections? What is one specific sign in your life you can contemplate right now?

The Self-Evidence of Knowledge's Conception
Razi presents his view that the conception of knowledge is self-evident, with two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that the conception of knowledge is self-evident, based on two rational proofs.

Core Idea
The conception of knowledge is self-evident because nothing else can reveal it and it is part of our necessary self-knowledge.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi addresses a foundational question in kalam: can knowledge be defined? He argues that the very concept of knowledge is self-evident (badihi), meaning it does not require a definition. He gives two compelling arguments to support this claim, which we will examine step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two arguments
Razi provides two proofs. Find where each begins and understand their structure.
3
Analyze the first argument
Understand the logic: knowledge unveils everything else, so it cannot be unveiled by something else. This shows it is self-evident.
4
Analyze the second argument
Our self-knowledge includes the conception of knowledge. Since self-knowledge is self-evident, its part (the conception) is also self-evident.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why this matters: if knowledge's conception is self-evident, then defining it is not strictly necessary, though possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: اختلفوا في حد العلم. وعندي أن تصوره بديهي، لأن ما عدا العلم لا ينكشف إلا به فيستحيل أن يكون كاشفا له، ولأني أعلم بالضرورة كوني عالما بوجودي، وتصور العلم جزء منه، وجزء البديهي بديهي، فتصور العلم بديهي.
(2) أقول: المطلوب من حد العلم هو العلم بالعلم، وما عدا العلم ينكشف بالعلم لا بالعلم بالعلم، وليس من المحال أن يكون هو كاشفا عن غيره وغيره كاشفا عن العلم به.
Issue: They differed concerning the definition of knowledge. In my view, its conception is self-evident, because nothing other than knowledge is unveiled except by it, so it is impossible for it to be an unveiler of it. And because I know necessarily that I am knowing of my existence, and the conception of knowledge is part of it, and part of the self-evident is self-evident, so the conception of knowledge is self-evident. (2) I say: The aim of the definition of knowledge is knowledge of knowledge, and what is other than knowledge is unveiled by knowledge, not by knowledge of knowledge, and it is not impossible for it to be an unveiler of something else and for something else to be an unveiler of knowledge of it.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It addresses the question of whether knowledge can be defined. Razi argues that the conception (tasawwur) of knowledge is self-evident (badihi), meaning it does not require a definition. He gives two reasons: First, everything other than knowledge is known through knowledge, so knowledge cannot be known through something else. Second, I necessarily know that I am knowing of my existence, and the conception of knowledge is part of that self-knowledge, which is self-evident. The footnote (2) clarifies that the definition of knowledge is actually knowledge of knowledge, and it is possible for knowledge to reveal other things while other things reveal knowledge itself. This guards against the objection that if knowledge reveals everything, it cannot be self-revealing.

Key Terms
علم
بديهي
تصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that because knowledge is known through itself, it cannot be defined at all.
Correction: Razi does not say it cannot be defined; he says its conception is self-evident, meaning it does not need a definition, but a definition may still be given for clarification.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first proof, why can't knowledge be unveiled by something else?
A) Because knowledge is hidden.
B) Because everything else is unveiled by knowledge.
C) Because knowledge is a simple concept.
D) Because the definition is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The conception of knowledge is self-evident because it is both the unveiler of everything else and a part of our necessary self-knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the conception of knowledge is self-evident, why do you still find yourself doubting what you truly know? What in your life is blocking this self-evident certainty?
?
You know necessarily that you are knowing of your own existence—but do you live as if that knowledge is certain? When did you last act on that certainty, and when did you ignore it?
?
This lesson says the conception of knowledge cannot be made known by anything else. Are you looking for proofs for things that are already self-evident? What would change if you stopped and simply accepted what you already know?

The Aim of Defining Knowledge
Razi clarifies the purpose of defining knowledge and addresses an objection about self-unveiling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's clarification that the definition of knowledge is actually 'knowledge of knowledge' and why it is not contradictory.

Core Idea
Defining knowledge is knowing knowledge itself, and it is possible for knowledge to reveal others while being revealed by them.

In this short but important passage, Razi clarifies a potential confusion. Critics might argue that if knowledge unveils everything, it cannot unveil itself. Razi explains that the aim of a definition is 'knowledge of knowledge' (al-ilm bil-ilm), and it is perfectly possible for knowledge to reveal other things while other things reveal knowledge of itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage carefully. Notice the technical term 'knowledge of knowledge'.
2
Understand the aim of defining knowledge
Razi says the aim is 'knowledge of knowledge'. This means we seek to know knowledge itself, not to replace it with something else.
3
Analyze the logical possibility
Razi states it is not impossible for knowledge to unveil others while others unveil knowledge of it. This resolves the objection that knowledge cannot be self-revealing.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this clarification defends the possibility of defining knowledge without contradicting its self-evident nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: اختلفوا في حد العلم. وعندي أن تصوره بديهي، لأن ما عدا العلم لا ينكشف إلا به فيستحيل أن يكون كاشفا له، ولأني أعلم بالضرورة كوني عالما بوجودي، وتصور العلم جزء منه، وجزء البديهي بديهي، فتصور العلم بديهي.
(2) أقول: المطلوب من حد العلم هو العلم بالعلم، وما عدا العلم ينكشف بالعلم لا بالعلم بالعلم، وليس من المحال أن يكون هو كاشفا عن غيره وغيره كاشفا عن العلم به.
Issue: They differed concerning the definition of knowledge. In my view, its conception is self-evident, because nothing other than knowledge is unveiled except by it, so it is impossible for it to be an unveiler of it. And because I know necessarily that I am knowing of my existence, and the conception of knowledge is part of it, and part of the self-evident is self-evident, so the conception of knowledge is self-evident. (2) I say: The aim of the definition of knowledge is knowledge of knowledge, and what is other than knowledge is unveiled by knowledge, not by knowledge of knowledge, and it is not impossible for it to be an unveiler of something else and for something else to be an unveiler of knowledge of it.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It addresses the question of whether knowledge can be defined. Razi argues that the conception (tasawwur) of knowledge is self-evident (badihi), meaning it does not require a definition. He gives two reasons: First, everything other than knowledge is known through knowledge, so knowledge cannot be known through something else. Second, I necessarily know that I am knowing of my existence, and the conception of knowledge is part of that self-knowledge, which is self-evident. The footnote (2) clarifies that the definition of knowledge is actually knowledge of knowledge, and it is possible for knowledge to reveal other things while other things reveal knowledge itself. This guards against the objection that if knowledge reveals everything, it cannot be self-revealing.

Key Terms
علم بالعلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if knowledge unveils everything, it cannot be defined because that would require something else to unveil it.
Correction: Razi clarifies that the definition aims at 'knowledge of knowledge', and it is possible for knowledge to be unveiled by something else (like a definition) while it unveils everything else.

Checkpoint
What does Razi say is the aim of defining knowledge?
A) To deny that knowledge exists.
B) To achieve knowledge of knowledge (al-ilm bil-ilm).
C) To prove knowledge is self-evident.
D) To reject all definitions.

Key Takeaway
Defining knowledge is seeking knowledge of knowledge, and it is logically possible for knowledge to be both unveiler and unveiled.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider your own knowledge of the deen. Does it unveil the realities of your life, or is it itself in need of being unveiled by something else? What would it take for your knowledge to become a true unveiler that changes how you live?
?
Razi distinguishes between knowledge and knowledge of knowledge. Do you truly know where your beliefs come from? Are you aware of the sources that define your understanding of knowledge? If not, what does that mean for the strength of your faith?
?
The passage says it is not impossible for knowledge to both unveil and be unveiled. Examine your own heart: what defines your knowledge of Allah? Is it merely information you've memorized, or does it actively unveil His presence in your daily life? Commit to seeking knowledge that unveils you.

Refuting the Negative Theory of Knowledge
Razi refutes the negative view and replies to a proof for positivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi rejects the negative theory of knowledge and how he responds to the argument that knowledge must be a positive attribute.

Core Idea
Knowledge cannot be a negation because that would make non-existence knowing.

Imam Razi begins by addressing the claim that knowledge is a negative attribute. He shows that this leads to absurd consequences. He then considers an argument that knowledge must be something positive because we distinguish between known things, and he refutes that as well.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two separate arguments: the initial refutation of the negative theory, and the response to the argument from distinction.
The passage combines two arguments under one theme.
2
Identify the reductio ad absurdum
In the first part, Razi assumes knowledge is negative and derives two contradictory possibilities. Trace how each leads to an absurdity.
This is a classic kalam proof technique.
3
Understand the distinction argument
The opponents argue that we distinguish between known things, so knowledge must be something positive. Razi answers that this would require the entire essence of the known to be in the mind, which is false.
Razi uses an example of imagining the sea.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why Razi's refutation of the negative theory is important for a positive definition of knowledge.
This connects to the broader project of defining knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: قيل العلم سلبي وهو باطل. لأنه لو كان كذلك لكان سلب ما ينافيه، والمنافي إن كان عدميا كان هو عدم العلم فيكون ثبوتيا، وإن كان وجوديا فعدمه يصدق على العلم فيكون العدم موصوفا بالعالمية، هذا خلف. وقيل إنه انطباع صورة مساوية للمعلوم في العالم وهو باطل، واللازم أن يكون العالم بالحرارة والبرودة حارا بارد. لا يقال: المنطبع صورته ومثاله. لأنا نقول: الصورة والمثال إن كان مساويا في تمام الماهية للمعلوم لزم المحذور، وإبطاله قولهم نكتة أخرى: يلزم أن يكون الجدار الموصوف بالحرارة والبرودة عالما. لا يقال: حصول الماهية للشيء إنما يكون إدراكا كما إذا كان الشيء مما من شأنه أن يكون مدركا. لأنا نقول: إن كان الإدراك هو نفس الحصول فالمدرك هو الذي له الحصول، فكان الجدار من شأنه أن يدرك لا من شأنه أن يكون له الحصول. احتجوا: بأنا نميز بعض المعلومات عن بعض فوجب أن يكون ثابتا، لأن العدم الصرف لا تميز فيه، وإذ قد لا يكون المعلوم ثابتا في الخارج فهو في الذهن. جوابه: هذا يقتضي أن يكون المعلوم بتمام ماهيته حاضرا في الذهن، فمن تخيل البحر فقد حضر في خياله تمام ماهية البحر، وذلك باطل بالبديهة.
(1) أقول ل: الحكم بأن القول بكون العلم سلبيا باطل صحيح ولكن في دليله نظر لأن المناف إن كان مطلق العدم كان العلم مطلق الوجود، وإن كان عدميا لا يكون العلم عدم العلم حتى يكون ثبوتيا إنما هو عدم العدمي، ولا يجب أن يكون عدم العدمي ثبوتيا، فإن عدم العمى كما في الحجر وبل فيمن نزل في عينه ماء بل في الجدار لا يكون إبصارا. وأيضا يلزم من قوله «ولو كان وجودا فعدمه يصدق على العدم فيكون العدم موصوفا بالعلم» ثبوت ما ادعى إبطاله، لأن وصف العدم لا يكون وجوديا، فإذا العلم سلبي. وأما إبطال القول بالانطباع لوجوب أن يكون العالم بالحرارة حارا فليس بصحيح، لأنهم قالوا بانطباع صورة مساوية للحرارة، وفرق بين صورة الشيء وبينه فإن الإنسان ناطق وصورته ليس يناطق. وقوله «وإن كان مساويا في تمام الماهية لزم المحذور» فالصحيح أنها ليست مساوية في تمام الماهية، بل هي صورة لا نفس الماهية. وإذا كانت الماهية وصورتها اثنينية في النوع لكانت الصورة غير الماهية، وجاز أن يكون المقتضي لكون المحل حارا هو مجموع ما به الاشتراك وما به الامتياز. وأيضا في النكتة جعل العلم هو حصول الماهية، فالذي قاله هنا ليس ما ذهبوا إليه. وقوله في الجواب: «إن كان الإدراك هو نفس الحصول فالجدار من شأنه أن يدرك إذ له الحصول» ليس بصحيح لأنهم قالوا الإدراك نفس الحصول قابل مشروط بشرط مخصوص، فإنا لو قلنا الغنى حصول مال عند من شأنه أن يحصل له مال لا يلزم منه أن يكون الحمار الذي يحصل عنده مال غنيا.
Issue: It was said that knowledge is negative, and it is false. Because if it were so, it would be the negation of its opposite, and the opposite, if it were non-existent, would be non-existence of knowledge, so it would be positive; and if it were existent, its non-existence would apply to knowledge, so non-existence would be described as knowing, which is absurd. And it was said that it is an impression of a form equal to the known in the knower, and it is false, necessitating that one who knows heat and cold be hot and cold. It may not be said: The impressed is its form and likeness. For we say: If the form and likeness are equal to the known in the entirety of essence, the absurdity follows; and another point refutes their statement: It would necessitate that the wall described with heat and cold be knowing. It may not be said: The obtaining of the essence for a thing is only perception, as when the thing is such that it can perceive. For we say: If perception is the obtaining itself, then the perceiver is that which has the obtaining, so the wall would be such that it can perceive, not such that it has the obtaining. They argued: We distinguish some known things from others, so it must be something established, because pure non-existence has no distinction; and since the known may not be established externally, it is in the mind. Answer: This necessitates that the known in its entire essence be present in the mind, so one who imagines the sea has the entire essence of the sea present in his imagination, which is self-evidently false. (1) I say: The judgment that the statement that knowledge is negative is false is correct, but his proof is questionable, because if the opposite is absolute non-existence, then knowledge is absolute existence; and if it is non-existent, then knowledge is not negation of knowledge so as to become positive; rather it is negation of the non-existent, and negation of the non-existent does not have to be positive, for the negation of blindness, as in a stone, or even in one who has water in his eye, or in a wall, is not sight. Also from his statement 'And if it were existent, its non-existence would apply to non-existence so non-existence would be described with knowledge', follows the very thing he claimed to refute, because the description of non-existence is not existent, so knowledge is negative. As for refuting the statement that knowledge is impression by the necessity that the knower of heat be hot, it is not correct, because they said it is an impression of a form equal to heat, and there is a difference between the form of a thing and the thing itself: man is rational, but his form is not rational. And his statement 'And if it were equal in the entirety of essence, the absurdity would follow' – the correct view is that it is not equal in the entirety of essence, but rather a form, not the essence itself. And if essence and its form are two in type, then the form is other than the essence, and it is possible that the necessitator of the locus being hot is the sum of what is common and what is distinctive. Also in the other point he made knowledge the obtaining of the essence, but what he said here is not what they intended. And his statement in the answer: 'If perception is the obtaining itself, then the wall is such that it perceives because it has obtaining' is not correct, because they said perception is the obtaining itself that is susceptible, conditioned by a specific condition; for if we said wealth is the obtaining of money for one who is such that money can obtain for him, it does not necessitate that the donkey that obtains money is wealthy.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two incorrect theories of knowledge. First, Razi refutes the view that knowledge is a negative attribute (salbi). He argues that if knowledge were a negation of its opposite, then either the opposite is non-existent, making knowledge positive by affirming the negation of non-existence, or the opposite is existent, whose negation would apply to non-existence, absurdly describing non-existence as knowing. This is a reductio. Second, he refutes the impression theory (intiba'), that knowledge is an imprint of the form of the known object in the knower. If so, knowing heat would make the knower hot, which is false. He then addresses objections: one might say that the impressed is only a likeness, not the thing itself. Razi responds that if the likeness is identical in essence, the absurdity remains; if not, then the theory fails. Another objection says that for a wall to know would require that it have perception, but perception is a special quality. Razi argues that if perception is merely the presence of a form, then anything that has a form would perceive, which is false. Then he addresses an argument from distinction: we distinguish between known things, so knowledge must be a positive attribute in the mind. Razi answers that this would require the entire essence of the known to be present in the mind, which is self-evidently false (e.g., imagining the sea does not bring the entire sea into the mind). The long footnote (1) further critiques Razi's own proofs, pointing out that his refutation of the negative theory has weaknesses, and that the impression theory is not refuted by his argument because of the distinction between a thing and its form. This shows the author's own critical engagement with his arguments.

Key Terms
علم سلبي
تمييز
ثبوتي
عدمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge is merely the absence of ignorance (negative).
Correction: If knowledge were a negation, then describing non-existence as knowing would follow, which is absurd.

Checkpoint
What does Razi claim would follow if knowledge were a negation of an existent opposite?
A) Knowledge would be positive.
B) Non-existence would be described as knowing.
C) The opposite would be non-existent.
D) Knowledge would be an impression.

Key Takeaway
Razi's refutation shows that knowledge cannot be a mere negation; it must be a positive attribute, but not a full mental replica.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own knowledge of Allah, do you treat it as merely 'not being ignorant' or as a positive reality that actively shapes your heart and actions? Has this lesson exposed a passive attitude in your learning?
?
If knowledge cannot be a mere absence, then what does it mean for you to truly 'know' a truth from the Quran and Sunnah? Are you content with vague awareness, or are you seeking the kind of knowledge that leaves no room for indifference?
?
Razi shows that even imagining the sea does not bring its reality into the mind. How much of what you 'know' about the afterlife is just a mental image that hasn't penetrated your soul? Will you let this lesson push you to seek knowledge that transforms you?

intermediate

Refuting the Impression Theory of Knowledge
Razi refutes the impression theory and answers two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's arguments against the impression theory of knowledge and how he responds to anticipated objections.

Core Idea
If knowledge were an impression, knowing heat would make you hot.

The impression theory of knowledge claims that knowledge is the imprint of a form of the known object in the knower. Razi argues that this would lead to absurd physical consequences, such as the knower of heat becoming hot. He then addresses two objections to his refutation, defending his position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the structure: main refutation, then two objections (لا يقال) and Razi's responses.
This is a classic dialectical style.
2
Identify the core argument
Razi's main point: if knowledge is an impression of a form equal to the known, then the knower would acquire the properties of the known. E.g., knowing heat would make the knower hot.
This is a physical absurdity.
3
Analyze the first objection
Objection: the impressed is only a likeness, not the thing itself. Razi responds that if the likeness is identical in essence, the absurdity remains; if not, the theory fails.
Razi uses the concept of 'equality in essence'.
4
Analyze the second objection
Objection: the obtaining of essence is perception only when the thing is a perceiver. Razi counters that if perception is the obtaining itself, then a wall would perceive because it has obtaining (forms).
This shows the theory wrongly extends perception to all objects.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the impression theory is appealing and how Razi's refutation protects a coherent epistemology.
This lesson connects to the definition of knowledge as something more than passive reception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: قيل العلم سلبي وهو باطل. لأنه لو كان كذلك لكان سلب ما ينافيه، والمنافي إن كان عدميا كان هو عدم العلم فيكون ثبوتيا، وإن كان وجوديا فعدمه يصدق على العلم فيكون العدم موصوفا بالعالمية، هذا خلف. وقيل إنه انطباع صورة مساوية للمعلوم في العالم وهو باطل، واللازم أن يكون العالم بالحرارة والبرودة حارا بارد. لا يقال: المنطبع صورته ومثاله. لأنا نقول: الصورة والمثال إن كان مساويا في تمام الماهية للمعلوم لزم المحذور، وإبطاله قولهم نكتة أخرى: يلزم أن يكون الجدار الموصوف بالحرارة والبرودة عالما. لا يقال: حصول الماهية للشيء إنما يكون إدراكا كما إذا كان الشيء مما من شأنه أن يكون مدركا. لأنا نقول: إن كان الإدراك هو نفس الحصول فالمدرك هو الذي له الحصول، فكان الجدار من شأنه أن يدرك لا من شأنه أن يكون له الحصول. احتجوا: بأنا نميز بعض المعلومات عن بعض فوجب أن يكون ثابتا، لأن العدم الصرف لا تميز فيه، وإذ قد لا يكون المعلوم ثابتا في الخارج فهو في الذهن. جوابه: هذا يقتضي أن يكون المعلوم بتمام ماهيته حاضرا في الذهن، فمن تخيل البحر فقد حضر في خياله تمام ماهية البحر، وذلك باطل بالبديهة.
(1) أقول ل: الحكم بأن القول بكون العلم سلبيا باطل صحيح ولكن في دليله نظر لأن المناف إن كان مطلق العدم كان العلم مطلق الوجود، وإن كان عدميا لا يكون العلم عدم العلم حتى يكون ثبوتيا إنما هو عدم العدمي، ولا يجب أن يكون عدم العدمي ثبوتيا، فإن عدم العمى كما في الحجر وبل فيمن نزل في عينه ماء بل في الجدار لا يكون إبصارا. وأيضا يلزم من قوله «ولو كان وجودا فعدمه يصدق على العدم فيكون العدم موصوفا بالعلم» ثبوت ما ادعى إبطاله، لأن وصف العدم لا يكون وجوديا، فإذا العلم سلبي. وأما إبطال القول بالانطباع لوجوب أن يكون العالم بالحرارة حارا فليس بصحيح، لأنهم قالوا بانطباع صورة مساوية للحرارة، وفرق بين صورة الشيء وبينه فإن الإنسان ناطق وصورته ليس يناطق. وقوله «وإن كان مساويا في تمام الماهية لزم المحذور» فالصحيح أنها ليست مساوية في تمام الماهية، بل هي صورة لا نفس الماهية. وإذا كانت الماهية وصورتها اثنينية في النوع لكانت الصورة غير الماهية، وجاز أن يكون المقتضي لكون المحل حارا هو مجموع ما به الاشتراك وما به الامتياز. وأيضا في النكتة جعل العلم هو حصول الماهية، فالذي قاله هنا ليس ما ذهبوا إليه. وقوله في الجواب: «إن كان الإدراك هو نفس الحصول فالجدار من شأنه أن يدرك إذ له الحصول» ليس بصحيح لأنهم قالوا الإدراك نفس الحصول قابل مشروط بشرط مخصوص، فإنا لو قلنا الغنى حصول مال عند من شأنه أن يحصل له مال لا يلزم منه أن يكون الحمار الذي يحصل عنده مال غنيا.
Issue: It was said that knowledge is negative, and it is false. Because if it were so, it would be the negation of its opposite, and the opposite, if it were non-existent, would be non-existence of knowledge, so it would be positive; and if it were existent, its non-existence would apply to knowledge, so non-existence would be described as knowing, which is absurd. And it was said that it is an impression of a form equal to the known in the knower, and it is false, necessitating that one who knows heat and cold be hot and cold. For we say: If the form and likeness are equal to the known in the entirety of essence, the absurdity follows; and another point refutes their statement: It would necessitate that the wall described with heat and cold be knowing. Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two incorrect theories of knowledge. First, Razi refutes the view that knowledge is a negative attribute (salbi). He argues that if knowledge were a negation of its opposite, then either the opposite is non-existent, making knowledge positive by affirming the negation of non-existence, or the opposite is existent, whose negation would apply to non-existence, absurdly describing non-existence as knowing. This is a reductio. Second, he refutes the impression theory (intiba'), that knowledge is an imprint of the form of the known object in the knower. If so, knowing heat would make the knower hot, which is false. He then addresses objections: one might say that the impressed is only a likeness, not the thing itself. Razi responds that if the likeness is identical in essence, the absurdity remains; if not, then the theory fails. Another objection says that for a wall to know would require that it have perception, but perception is a special quality. Razi argues that if perception is merely the presence of a form, then anything that has a form would perceive, which is false. Then he addresses an argument from distinction: we distinguish between known things, so knowledge must be a positive attribute in the mind. Razi answers that this would require the entire essence of the known to be present in the mind, which is self-evidently false (e.g., imagining the sea does not bring the entire sea into the mind). The long footnote (1) further critiques Razi's own proofs, pointing out that his refutation of the negative theory has weaknesses, and that the impression theory is not refuted by his argument because of the distinction between a thing and its form. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge is the presence of a mental image of the object, like a photograph.
Correction: Razi shows that such a view leads to the knower acquiring physical properties of the known, which is absurd.

Checkpoint
What does Razi say would follow if knowledge were an impression of a form equal to the known?
A) The knower would become identical to the known.
B) The knower would acquire the physical properties of the known.
C) The knower would remain unchanged.
D) The known would become a knower.

Key Takeaway
Razi's refutation disproves the impression theory by showing it leads to physical absurdities and fails to distinguish perception from mere presence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you know something, do you ever assume that your mind simply 'copies' the object like a photograph? How does Razi's refutation challenge that assumption, and what does it reveal about the nature of your own knowledge?
?
If knowledge were just information stored in your mind, like a file on a hard drive, would you be any different from a book that contains that information? What does Razi's second argument about the wall and heat say about the kind of being you must be in order to truly know?
?
Razi shows that a flawed theory leads to absurd consequences. Are there beliefs you hold about knowledge, yourself, or Allah that, if followed logically, would lead to something absurd? Are you willing to reexamine them with the same rigor Razi applies here?

Critique of Razi's Arguments
The commentator critiques Razi's arguments in the two refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's critique of Razi's reasoning in both refutations and the nuances of the arguments.

Core Idea
Even Razi's arguments are not beyond criticism, as this footnote shows.

In this critical footnote, the commentator examines Razi's proofs. He agrees with the conclusions but points out flaws in the reasoning: the refutation of the negative theory has logical gaps, and the refutation of the impression theory fails to address the distinction between a thing and its form. This lesson encourages critical engagement with even authoritative texts.

Guided Path
1
Read the footnote and translation
Read the Arabic footnote and its translation. This is a dense critical commentary on Razi's earlier arguments.
The footnote is long; read slowly.
2
Identify the critique of the negative theory refutation
The commentator argues that Razi's proof for the negative theory is flawed: if the opposite is non-existent, then knowledge is negation of the non-existent, which need not be positive. Also, Razi's own argument would imply knowledge is negative.
This is a sophisticated logical point.
3
Identify the critique of the impression theory refutation
The commentator notes that Razi ignores the distinction between a thing and its form. The impression is of a form, not the thing itself, so knowing heat does not make the knower hot.
The commentator provides the example of man vs. image of man.
4
Understand the point about perception and conditions
The commentator clarifies that perception is the obtaining of a form under a specific condition, not mere presence. Razi's counterexample about the wall is invalid.
This shows the need for a precise definition of perception.
5
Reflect on the methodology
Consider how this critique illustrates the dialectical nature of kalam: even masters' arguments are subject to scrutiny.
This lesson emphasizes critical thinking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: قيل العلم سلبي وهو باطل. لأنه لو كان كذلك لكان سلب ما ينافيه، والمنافي إن كان عدميا كان هو عدم العلم فيكون ثبوتيا، وإن كان وجوديا فعدمه يصدق على العلم فيكون العدم موصوفا بالعالمية، هذا خلف. وقيل إنه انطباع صورة مساوية للمعلوم في العالم وهو باطل، واللازم أن يكون العالم بالحرارة والبرودة حارا بارد. لا يقال: المنطبع صورته ومثاله. لأنا نقول: الصورة والمثال إن كان مساويا في تمام الماهية للمعلوم لزم المحذور، وإبطاله قولهم نكتة أخرى: يلزم أن يكون الجدار الموصوف بالحرارة والبرودة عالما. لا يقال: حصول الماهية للشيء إنما يكون إدراكا كما إذا كان الشيء مما من شأنه أن يكون مدركا. لأنا نقول: إن كان الإدراك هو نفس الحصول فالمدرك هو الذي له الحصول، فكان الجدار من شأنه أن يدرك لا من شأنه أن يكون له الحصول. احتجوا: بأنا نميز بعض المعلومات عن بعض فوجب أن يكون ثابتا، لأن العدم الصرف لا تميز فيه، وإذ قد لا يكون المعلوم ثابتا في الخارج فهو في الذهن. جوابه: هذا يقتضي أن يكون المعلوم بتمام ماهيته حاضرا في الذهن، فمن تخيل البحر فقد حضر في خياله تمام ماهية البحر، وذلك باطل بالبديهة.
(1) أقول ل: الحكم بأن القول بكون العلم سلبيا باطل صحيح ولكن في دليله نظر لأن المناف إن كان مطلق العدم كان العلم مطلق الوجود، وإن كان عدميا لا يكون العلم عدم العلم حتى يكون ثبوتيا إنما هو عدم العدمي، ولا يجب أن يكون عدم العدمي ثبوتيا، فإن عدم العمى كما في الحجر وبل فيمن نزل في عينه ماء بل في الجدار لا يكون إبصارا. وأيضا يلزم من قوله «ولو كان وجودا فعدمه يصدق على العدم فيكون العدم موصوفا بالعلم» ثبوت ما ادعى إبطاله، لأن وصف العدم لا يكون وجوديا، فإذا العلم سلبي. وأما إبطال القول بالانطباع لوجوب أن يكون العالم بالحرارة حارا فليس بصحيح، لأنهم قالوا بانطباع صورة مساوية للحرارة، وفرق بين صورة الشيء وبينه فإن الإنسان ناطق وصورته ليس يناطق. وقوله «وإن كان مساويا في تمام الماهية لزم المحذور» فالصحيح أنها ليست مساوية في تمام الماهية، بل هي صورة لا نفس الماهية. وإذا كانت الماهية وصورتها اثنينية في النوع لكانت الصورة غير الماهية، وجاز أن يكون المقتضي لكون المحل حارا هو مجموع ما به الاشتراك وما به الامتياز. وأيضا في النكتة جعل العلم هو حصول الماهية، فالذي قاله هنا ليس ما ذهبوا إليه. وقوله في الجواب: «إن كان الإدراك هو نفس الحصول فالجدار من شأنه أن يدرك إذ له الحصول» ليس بصحيح لأنهم قالوا الإدراك نفس الحصول قابل مشروط بشرط مخصوص، فإنا لو قلنا الغنى حصول مال عند من شأنه أن يحصل له مال لا يلزم منه أن يكون الحمار الذي يحصل عنده مال غنيا.
Issue: It was said that knowledge is negative, and it is false. Because if it were so, it would be the negation of its opposite, and the opposite, if it were non-existent, would be non-existence of knowledge, so it would be positive; and if it were existent, its non-existence would apply to knowledge, so non-existence would be described as knowing, which is absurd. And it was said that it is an impression of a form equal to the known in the knower, and it is false, necessitating that one who knows heat and cold be hot and cold. It may not be said: The impressed is its form and likeness. For we say: If the form and likeness are equal to the known in the entirety of essence, the absurdity follows; and another point refutes their statement: It would necessitate that the wall described with heat and cold be knowing. It may not be said: The obtaining of the essence for a thing is only perception, as when the thing is such that it can perceive. For we say: If perception is the obtaining itself, then the perceiver is that which has the obtaining, so the wall would be such that it can perceive, not such that it has the obtaining. They argued: We distinguish some known things from others, so it must be something established, because pure non-existence has no distinction; and since the known may not be established externally, it is in the mind. Answer: This necessitates that the known in its entire essence be present in the mind, so one who imagines the sea has the entire essence of the sea present in his imagination, which is self-evidently false. (1) I say: The judgment that the statement that knowledge is negative is false is correct, but his proof is questionable, because if the opposite is absolute non-existence, then knowledge is absolute existence; and if it is non-existent, then knowledge is not negation of knowledge so as to become positive; rather it is negation of the non-existent, and negation of the non-existent does not have to be positive, for the negation of blindness, as in a stone, or even in one who has water in his eye, or in a wall, is not sight. Also from his statement 'And if it were existent, its non-existence would apply to non-existence so non-existence would be described with knowledge', follows the very thing he claimed to refute, because the description of non-existence is not existent, so knowledge is negative. As for refuting the statement that knowledge is impression by the necessity that the knower of heat be hot, it is not correct, because they said it is an impression of a form equal to heat, and there is a difference between the form of a thing and the thing itself: man is rational, but his form is not rational. And his statement 'And if it were equal in the entirety of essence, the absurdity would follow' – the correct view is that it is not equal in the entirety of essence, but rather a form, not the essence itself. And if essence and its form are two in type, then the form is other than the essence, and it is possible that the necessitator of the locus being hot is the sum of what is common and what is distinctive. Also in the other point he made knowledge the obtaining of the essence, but what he said here is not what they intended. And his statement in the answer: 'If perception is the obtaining itself, then the wall is such that it perceives because it has obtaining' is not correct, because they said perception is the obtaining itself that is susceptible, conditioned by a specific condition; for if we said wealth is the obtaining of money for one who is such that money can obtain for him, it does not necessitate that the donkey that obtains money is wealthy.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two incorrect theories of knowledge. First, Razi refutes the view that knowledge is a negative attribute (salbi). He argues that if knowledge were a negation of its opposite, then either the opposite is non-existent, making knowledge positive by affirming the negation of non-existence, or the opposite is existent, whose negation would apply to non-existence, absurdly describing non-existence as knowing. This is a reductio. Second, he refutes the impression theory (intiba'), that knowledge is an imprint of the form of the known object in the knower. If so, knowing heat would make the knower hot, which is false. He then addresses objections: one might say that the impressed is only a likeness, not the thing itself. Razi responds that if the likeness is identical in essence, the absurdity remains; if not, then the theory fails. Another objection says that for a wall to know would require that it have perception, but perception is a special quality. Razi argues that if perception is merely the presence of a form, then anything that has a form would perceive, which is false. Then he addresses an argument from distinction: we distinguish between known things, so knowledge must be a positive attribute in the mind. Razi answers that this would require the entire essence of the known to be present in the mind, which is self-evidently false (e.g., imagining the sea does not bring the entire sea into the mind). The long footnote (1) further critiques Razi's own proofs, pointing out that his refutation of the negative theory has weaknesses, and that the impression theory is not refuted by his argument because of the distinction between a thing and its form. This shows the author's own critical engagement with his arguments.

انطباع
صورة
ماهية
إدراك
حصول

Misconception: Razi's arguments in the Mukhtasar are infallible and without flaw.
Correction: The commentator shows that Razi's proofs contain logical gaps and misrepresentations of opposing views.

Checkpoint

C) The sea
D) A wall with heat

Key Takeaway
Even authoritative arguments in kalam can be scrutinized, and careful analysis often reveals deeper complexities.

What Is Kalam?

Can One Knowledge Know Two Objects?
Razi examines whether one knowledge can have two objects via two interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's distinction between knowledge as the relation itself versus knowledge as that which necessitates the relation, and how this distinction determines whether one knowledge can have multiple objects.

Core Idea
Whether one knowledge can know two objects depends on how you define knowledge.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi tackles a nuanced question in epistemology: can a single instance of knowledge have more than one object? He shows that the answer hinges on what we mean by 'knowledge' itself. By distinguishing between knowledge as the mere relation to an object and knowledge as the attribute that produces that relation, Razi opens the door for a single knowledge to encompass multiple objects in certain cases.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that Razi starts with 'Issue: They differed...' and then gives his own view.
The passage is dense; read twice.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Razi considers two definitions of knowledge: (a) as the relation (ta'alluq) itself, (b) as that which necessitates the relation (muwjib al-ta'alluq). List them.
3
Understand the argument for the first interpretation
Why is it impossible for one relation to be related to two distinct objects? The key point is the possibility of heedlessness (dhuhul) of one object while knowing the other.
This shows the relation is specific to each object.
4
Understand the argument for the second interpretation
How can one attribute cause relations to multiple objects? The example of the knowledge of opposition between blackness and whiteness shows that the single knowledge relates to both colors and the relation between them.
If the attribute were not related to both, it could not relate to the opposition.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi does not definitively choose one interpretation but shows the implications of each. The lesson is that theological concepts often depend on definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: اختلفوا في أن العلم الواحد هل يكون علما بمعلومين. وعندي أنا إن فسرنا العلم بنفس التعلق لم يصح ذلك، لأنه يصح أن يعقل كون الشيء عالما بأحد المعلومين مع الذهول عن كونه عالما بالآخر، ولولا التغاير لا يصح ذلك. وإن فسرناه بما يوجب التعلق لم يمتنع، لأن العالم المتعلق بكون السواد مضادا للبياض إن لم يكن هو بعينه متعلقا بهما لم يكن متعلقا بالمضادة التي بينهما مطلق المضادة.
Issue: They differed concerning whether one knowledge can be knowledge of two known things. In my view, if we interpret knowledge as the relation itself, that is not valid, because it is conceivable that a thing be knowing of one known while being heedless of knowing the other, and were it not for the distinction, that would not be valid. And if we interpret it as that which necessitates the relation, it is not impossible, because the knower that is related to the state of blackness being opposite to whiteness, if it is not itself related to both, it would not be related to the opposition between them in absolute opposition.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a classification and argument. It addresses the question of whether a single instance of knowledge can have two objects. Razi distinguishes two interpretations of knowledge: (1) as the mere relation (ta'alluq) to the known, and (2) as the attribute that necessitates that relation. If knowledge is the relation itself, then one relation cannot simultaneously relate to two distinct objects, because one can be aware of one object while being heedless (dhuhul) of another, showing that the relation is specific. If knowledge is the attribute that causes relation, then it is not impossible for one attribute to cause relations to multiple objects. For example, the knowledge that blackness is opposite to whiteness involves a relation to both blackness and whiteness and to the opposition between them; if the attribute were not related to both, it could not relate to the opposition. So Razi concludes that the second interpretation allows one knowledge to have multiple objects, while the first does not.

Key Terms
علم
تعلق
معلوم
ذهول
تغاير
مضادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since knowledge is a single attribute, it can only have one object.
Correction: Razi shows that this depends on how knowledge is defined. If knowledge is defined as the necessitator of relation, it can have multiple objects.

Checkpoint
Under which interpretation does Razi say one knowledge can know two objects?
A) If knowledge is the relation itself.
B) If knowledge is that which necessitates the relation.
C) If knowledge is a substance.
D) If knowledge is an accident.

Key Takeaway
Razi's analysis shows that the possibility of a single knowledge having multiple objects depends on whether knowledge is defined as the relation itself or as the cause of relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own knowledge—like your understanding of a complex idea—do you ever find that a single mental state encompasses multiple objects? How does Razi's distinction between knowledge as relation and knowledge as cause of relation help you make sense of your own experience, and what does that reveal about the limits of your attention?
?
Are you ever 'heedless' (dhuhul) of one aspect of a belief while focusing on another? For example, do you sometimes focus on Allah's mercy while forgetting His justice? Does that prove your knowledge is fragmented? How can Razi's analysis push you to seek a more unified understanding of divine attributes?
?
Razi uses the example of knowing opposition: to know that blackness is opposite to whiteness, your knowledge must relate to both. In your spiritual life, what 'opposites' do you need to hold together in a single act of knowledge (e.g., hope and fear, love and awe)? Are you willing to let your knowledge be stretched to encompass both, rather than escaping into one-sided comfort?

The Issue and the Permitter's Division
Razi states the issue and refutes a permitter's division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core question of whether a single science can attach to two known things, and the permitter's proposed division based on the possibility of knowing one while heedless of the other.

Core Idea
A single science can attach to two things even if each can be known separately.

Imam Razi begins by narrowing the discussion to the science of a specific opposition, then introduces a division proposed by those who permit a single science to attach to two things. This division relies on whether one of the two known things can be known while heedless of the other. Razi then declares this division invalid using a clear example.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the first part of the passage from 'وليس كلامنا' to 'مع الجهل لآخر'. Try to grasp the flow of the argument before reading the translation.
Notice the distinction between 'that science' and 'the science of specific opposition'.
2
Understand the permitter's division
Identify the two conditions: if one of two known things can be known while heedless of the other, then a single science cannot attach to both; if not, then they must be known by a single science.
3
Analyze Razi's invalidation
See how Razi uses the example of blackness and whiteness: the science of their opposition attaches to both, yet one can know blackness alone without knowing whiteness. This shows the division is invalid.
This example directly challenges the permitter's first condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس كلامنا في ذلك العلم بل فى العلم المتعلق بالمضادة المخصوصة، وإن كان متعلقا بهما فهو المطلوب. ثم المجوزون منهم من فصل فقال: كل معلومين يصح أن يعلم أحدهما مع الذهول عن الآخر امتنع تعلق العلم الواحد بهما، وكل معلومين لا يصح العلم بأحدهما مع الذهول عن الآخر يجب أن يعلما بعلم واحد. وهذا الفصل باطل عندي، لأن العلم بمضادة السواد والبياض لما ثبت أنه متعلق بالسواد والبياض مع أنه يصح أن يعلم السواد وحده مع الجهل بالبياض فقد تعلق ذلك العلم بأمرين يصح العلم بأحدهما مع الجهل لآخر.

(1) أقول: العلم القديم عند أهل السنة يتعلق بمعلومات الله تعالى التي لا نهاية لها مع أنه واحد، وهذا البحث يتعلق بالعلم المحدث. فقال أبو الحسن الباهلي أن العلم الواحد يجوز أن يتعلق بمعلومات كثيرة، وحكى عن أبي الحسن الأشعري ذلك وأنكره الأستاذ أبو إسحاق وقال إنه ذكره في الإلزام على من يقول العلم الواحد يتعلق بمعلومين. وذهب الجبائي إلى جواز تعلق العلم الواحد بمعلومين، وأوجب ذلك من أهل السنة أبو منصور البغدادي. قال القاضي أبو بكر: كل معلومين لا ينفك أحدهما عن الآخر في العقل يجوز أن يتعلق بهما علم واحد، وكل ما يصح أن يعلم مع الذهول عن شيء آخر فلا يمكن أن يتعلق بهما علم واحد. ويقال للمصنف: إذا فسرت العلم بالتعلق جاز تعلق العلم بالمجموع ويكون الأجزاء داخلا فيه، وحينئذ قد تعلق بأمرين وأنت حكمت بامتناع ذلك، وأنت استدللت على الامتناع بصحة تعلق العلم بأحد المعلومين مع الذهول عن كونه عالما بالآخر، وههنا لا يصح هذا الاستدلال. وأيضا كان يجب أن يقول مع الذهول عن الآخر وأنت قلت مع الذهول عن كونه عالما بالآخر، وذلك لأن المطلوب ههنا التعلق بمعلومين لا معلوم وبالعلم أيضا. وأيضا على تقدير تفسير العلم بما يوجب التعلق جعل العلم بمطلق المضادة غير متعلق بشيئين وذلك غير معقول، فإن المضادة لا تعقل إلا بين شيئين بل يكون الشيئان شاملين ككل ما يقع عليه اسم الشيئية. ولا فرق بين المضادة المطلقة والمضادة المخصوصة إلا بعدم التعيين ووجود التعيين فيما تتعلق المضادة بهما، ولا يختلفان من حيث تعلقهما معلومين.

وإبطال قول المجوزين بقوله: العلم بالسواد والبياض يتعلق بأمرين يصح العلم بأحدهما مع الجهل بالآخر غير صحيح، لأن كلامهم في المضادة المتعلقة بهما، وتصور السواد وحده غير تصور السواد المضاد للبياض. فليس ما يصح العلم مع الجهل بالآخر هو أحد الشيئين اللذين يتعلق بهما معا.
Our discourse is not about that science, but about the science pertaining to the specific opposition, and if it pertains to both, then that is the goal. Then among those who permit [a single science to pertain to two things], some distinguished and said: Every two known things such that it is possible to know one of them while heedless of the other, the attachment of a single science to both is impossible; and every two known things such that it is not possible to know one of them while heedless of the other, they must be known by a single science. This division is invalid in my view, because the science of the opposition of blackness and whiteness, since it is established that it pertains to blackness and whiteness while it is possible to know blackness alone with ignorance of whiteness, then that science pertains to two things such that it is possible to know one of them with ignorance of the other.

(1) I say: The eternal science according to Ahl al-Sunnah pertains to the infinite knowable things of God while it is one, and this discussion pertains to created science. Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili said that a single science can pertain to many knowable things, and he narrated that from Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, but al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq denied that and said he mentioned it in al-Ilzam against those who say a single science pertains to two knowable things. Al-Jubba'i held the permissibility of a single science pertaining to two knowable things, and Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi from Ahl al-Sunnah deemed it necessary. Al-Qadi Abu Bakr said: Every two knowable things that are not separable from each other in the intellect, it is permissible for a single science to pertain to both; and everything that can be known while heedless of another thing, it is impossible for a single science to pertain to both. And it is said to the author: If you interpret science as attachment, then it is permissible for the science to attach to the aggregate and the parts are included in it, and then it has attached to two things, but you ruled its impossibility. And you argued for the impossibility by the soundness of the attachment of science to one of the two knowable things while heedless of being a knower of the other, but here this argument is not sound. Also, he should have said 'while heedless of the other,' but you said 'while heedless of being a knower of the other,' and that is because the required here is the attachment to two knowable things, not to a knowable and to science. Also, assuming the interpretation of science as necessitating attachment, making the science of absolute opposition not attached to two things is unintelligible, for opposition is only understood between two things; rather, the two things are inclusive like everything that falls under the name of thing-ness. And there is no difference between absolute opposition and specific opposition except by non-determination and the presence of determination in that to which opposition pertains, and they do not differ in terms of their being two knowable things.

And the refutation of the permitter's statement: 'The science of blackness and whiteness pertains to two things such that it is possible to know one of them with ignorance of the other' is not correct, because their discourse is about the opposition pertaining to both, and the conception of blackness alone is not the conception of blackness opposed to whiteness. So what is possible to know with ignorance of the other is not one of the two things that both are attached to.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of whether a single science (ilm) can be attached to two distinct known things (ma'lumat). The author (Imam Razi) begins by clarifying that the debate is about science pertaining to a specific opposition (al-mudadda al-makhsusa) rather than absolute opposition. He then mentions a division made by some who permit it: if it is possible to know one of two things while heedless of the other, then one science cannot attach to both; if it is impossible to know one without the other, then they must be known by one science. Razi declares this division invalid, using the example of the science of the opposition between blackness and whiteness: since one can know blackness alone while ignorant of whiteness, but the science of their opposition pertains to both, a single science can attach to two things even though one can be known without the other.

Next, there is an extended comment (aqul) that surveys scholarly positions on this question. It notes that the eternal science of God is one but pertains to infinite things, and the discussion is about created science. Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari (narrated) allow one science to pertain to many things; al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq denies it. Al-Jubba'i permits one science for two things; Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi deems it necessary. Al-Qadi Abu Bakr distinguishes: if two things are inseparable in the intellect, one science can attach to both; otherwise, not. The comment then objects to the author's (Razi's) own argument: if science is defined as attachment, then the science of the aggregate includes the parts, so it attaches to two things, contradicting his claim of impossibility. Also, the argument that one can know one thing while heedless of being a knower of the other is misplaced; the issue is the object of knowledge, not the knower. Furthermore, the science of absolute opposition is not unintelligible—opposition requires two things. The comment concludes that there is no difference between absolute and specific opposition except determinacy.

Finally, the passage provides a refutation of the permitter's position: the example of blackness and whiteness does not support the permitter because their discourse is about the opposition pertaining to both, and conceiving blackness alone is not the same as conceiving blackness as opposed to whiteness. Thus, what is known with ignorance of the other is not one of the two terms of the opposition.

Key Terms
علم
معلوم
مضادة
تعلق
ذهول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if two things can be known separately, a single science cannot attach to both.
Correction: Razi shows that the science of opposition attaches to both even though each can be known separately.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi consider the permitter's division invalid?
A) Because the permitter's example was flawed.
B) Because the science of blackness-whiteness opposition pertains to both while each can be known separately.
C) Because the permitter ignored the role of heedlessness.
D) Because blackness and whiteness are not two distinct things.

Key Takeaway
A single science can attach to two things even if each can be known separately, as shown by the science of opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been certain that you understood a concept only to realize later that your understanding was incomplete because you ignored its opposite? How does Razi's example of blackness and whiteness expose a hidden assumption in your own thinking? Will you now look for the hidden relations between things you think you know?
?
When you learn about a topic, do you tend to isolate fragments or seek the connecting science that binds them? What would change in your study of Islam if you started asking, 'What single science unites these truths?' Starting today, pick one pair of concepts you hold separately and search for the 'science of opposition' between them. What will you do with the answer?

Survey of Scholarly Positions
Razi surveys scholarly opinions on the question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be familiar with the various positions of early scholars on whether a single science can pertain to multiple known things, including the views of al-Ash'ari, al-Jubba'i, al-Qadi Abu Bakr, and others.

Core Idea
Scholars differed widely on whether one science can attach to many known things.

After presenting his own initial argument, Imam Razi (or a commentator) provides a survey of opinions on this issue. This passage lists the views of several major theologians, including Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq, al-Jubba'i, Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi, and al-Qadi Abu Bakr. Each scholar either permits or denies the possibility, with al-Qadi Abu Bakr offering a nuanced distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage from 'أقول' to 'لا يمكن أن يتعلق بهما علم واحد'
This passage lists the opinions of various scholars. Read it carefully and note the differences.
The passage begins with a remark about God's eternal science to set the context.
2
Identify the key scholars and their positions
List each scholar mentioned and whether they permit or deny a single science attaching to multiple things. Pay special attention to al-Qadi Abu Bakr's condition.
3
Reflect on the diversity of views
Consider why such a fundamental question would have so many different answers. This shows the complexity of the issue in Islamic theology.
This variety is typical in kalam discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس كلامنا في ذلك العلم بل فى العلم المتعلق بالمضادة المخصوصة، وإن كان متعلقا بهما فهو المطلوب. ثم المجوزون منهم من فصل فقال: كل معلومين يصح أن يعلم أحدهما مع الذهول عن الآخر امتنع تعلق العلم الواحد بهما، وكل معلومين لا يصح العلم بأحدهما مع الذهول عن الآخر يجب أن يعلما بعلم واحد. وهذا الفصل باطل عندي، لأن العلم بمضادة السواد والبياض لما ثبت أنه متعلق بالسواد والبياض مع أنه يصح أن يعلم السواد وحده مع الجهل بالبياض فقد تعلق ذلك العلم بأمرين يصح العلم بأحدهما مع الجهل لآخر.

(1) أقول: العلم القديم عند أهل السنة يتعلق بمعلومات الله تعالى التي لا نهاية لها مع أنه واحد، وهذا البحث يتعلق بالعلم المحدث. فقال أبو الحسن الباهلي أن العلم الواحد يجوز أن يتعلق بمعلومات كثيرة، وحكى عن أبي الحسن الأشعري ذلك وأنكره الأستاذ أبو إسحاق وقال إنه ذكره في الإلزام على من يقول العلم الواحد يتعلق بمعلومين. وذهب الجبائي إلى جواز تعلق العلم الواحد بمعلومين، وأوجب ذلك من أهل السنة أبو منصور البغدادي. قال القاضي أبو بكر: كل معلومين لا ينفك أحدهما عن الآخر في العقل يجوز أن يتعلق بهما علم واحد، وكل ما يصح أن يعلم مع الذهول عن شيء آخر فلا يمكن أن يتعلق بهما علم واحد. ويقال للمصنف: إذا فسرت العلم بالتعلق جاز تعلق العلم بالمجموع ويكون الأجزاء داخلا فيه، وحينئذ قد تعلق بأمرين وأنت حكمت بامتناع ذلك، وأنت استدللت على الامتناع بصحة تعلق العلم بأحد المعلومين مع الذهول عن كونه عالما بالآخر، وههنا لا يصح هذا الاستدلال. وأيضا كان يجب أن يقول مع الذهول عن الآخر وأنت قلت مع الذهول عن كونه عالما بالآخر، وذلك لأن المطلوب ههنا التعلق بمعلومين لا معلوم وبالعلم أيضا. وأيضا على تقدير تفسير العلم بما يوجب التعلق جعل العلم بمطلق المضادة غير متعلق بشيئين وذلك غير معقول، فإن المضادة لا تعقل إلا بين شيئين بل يكون الشيئان شاملين ككل ما يقع عليه اسم الشيئية. ولا فرق بين المضادة المطلقة والمضادة المخصوصة إلا بعدم التعيين ووجود التعيين فيما تتعلق المضادة بهما، ولا يختلفان من حيث تعلقهما معلومين.

وإبطال قول المجوزين بقوله: العلم بالسواد والبياض يتعلق بأمرين يصح العلم بأحدهما مع الجهل بالآخر غير صحيح، لأن كلامهم في المضادة المتعلقة بهما، وتصور السواد وحده غير تصور السواد المضاد للبياض. فليس ما يصح العلم مع الجهل بالآخر هو أحد الشيئين اللذين يتعلق بهما معا.
Our discourse is not about that science, but about the science pertaining to the specific opposition, and if it pertains to both, then that is the goal. Then among those who permit [a single science to pertain to two things], some distinguished and said: Every two known things such that it is possible to know one of them while heedless of the other, the attachment of a single science to both is impossible; and every two known things such that it is not possible to know one of them while heedless of the other, they must be known by a single science. This division is invalid in my view, because the science of the opposition of blackness and whiteness, since it is established that it pertains to blackness and whiteness while it is possible to know blackness alone with ignorance of whiteness, then that science pertains to two things such that it is possible to know one of them with ignorance of the other.

(1) I say: The eternal science according to Ahl al-Sunnah pertains to the infinite knowable things of God while it is one, and this discussion pertains to created science. Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili said that a single science can pertain to many knowable things, and he narrated that from Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, but al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq denied that and said he mentioned it in al-Ilzam against those who say a single science pertains to two knowable things. Al-Jubba'i held the permissibility of a single science pertaining to two knowable things, and Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi from Ahl al-Sunnah deemed it necessary. Al-Qadi Abu Bakr said: Every two knowable things that are not separable from each other in the intellect, it is permissible for a single science to pertain to both; and everything that can be known while heedless of another thing, it is impossible for a single science to pertain to both. And it is said to the author: If you interpret science as attachment, then it is permissible for the science to attach to the aggregate and the parts are included in it, and then it has attached to two things, but you ruled its impossibility. And you argued for the impossibility by the soundness of the attachment of science to one of the two knowable things while heedless of being a knower of the other, but here this argument is not sound. Also, he should have said 'while heedless of the other,' but you said 'while heedless of being a knower of the other,' and that is because the required here is the attachment to two knowable things, not to a knowable and to science. Also, assuming the interpretation of science as necessitating attachment, making the science of absolute opposition not attached to two things is unintelligible, for opposition is only understood between two things; rather, the two things are inclusive like everything that falls under the name of thing-ness. And there is no difference between absolute opposition and specific opposition except by non-determination and the presence of determination in that to which opposition pertains, and they do not differ in terms of their being two knowable things.

And the refutation of the permitter's statement: 'The science of blackness and whiteness pertains to two things such that it is possible to know one of them with ignorance of the other' is not correct, because their discourse is about the opposition pertaining to both, and the conception of blackness alone is not the conception of blackness opposed to whiteness. So what is possible to know with ignorance of the other is not one of the two things that both are attached to.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of whether a single science (ilm) can be attached to two distinct known things (ma'lumat). The author (Imam Razi) begins by clarifying that the debate is about science pertaining to a specific opposition (al-mudadda al-makhsusa) rather than absolute opposition. He then mentions a division made by some who permit it: if it is possible to know one of two things while heedless of the other, then one science cannot attach to both; if it is impossible to know one without the other, then they must be known by one science. Razi declares this division invalid, using the example of the science of the opposition between blackness and whiteness: since one can know blackness alone while ignorant of whiteness, but the science of their opposition pertains to both, a single science can attach to two things even though one can be known without the other.

Next, there is an extended comment (aqul) that surveys scholarly positions on this question. It notes that the eternal science of God is one but pertains to infinite things, and the discussion is about created science. Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari (narrated) allow one science to pertain to many things; al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq denies it. Al-Jubba'i permits one science for two things; Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi deems it necessary. Al-Qadi Abu Bakr distinguishes: if two things are inseparable in the intellect, one science can attach to both; otherwise, not. The comment then objects to the author's (Razi's) own argument: if science is defined as attachment, then the science of the aggregate includes the parts, so it attaches to two things, contradicting his claim of impossibility. Also, the argument that one can know one thing while heedless of being a knower of the other is misplaced; the issue is the object of knowledge, not the knower. Furthermore, the science of absolute opposition is not unintelligible—opposition requires two things. The comment concludes that there is no difference between absolute and specific opposition except determinacy.

Finally, the passage provides a refutation of the permitter's position: the example of blackness and whiteness does not support the permitter because their discourse is about the opposition pertaining to both, and conceiving blackness alone is not the same as conceiving blackness as opposed to whiteness. Thus, what is known with ignorance of the other is not one of the two terms of the opposition.

علم محدث
مجوز
مانع
افتراق في العقل

Checkpoint
According to al-Qadi Abu Bakr, when is it permissible for a single science to attach to two known things?
A) When they are both known by the same person.
B) When they are intellectually inseparable.
C) When one can be known while heedless of the other.
D) When they are both created.

Key Takeaway
Scholars held diverse views: some permitted a single science for many things, some for two only, and some conditioned it on intellectual inseparability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to 'know' something in your life, do you reflect on whether your knowledge is a unified truth or a collection of separate facts? How does Baqillani's condition of intellectual inseparability challenge you to pursue knowledge that is integrated rather than scattered?
?
The scholars of the past exerted great effort to precisely define the nature of knowledge. Are you willing to invest similar effort to understand the truths of your faith, even when the subtleties are difficult and you could simply accept a simpler view?
?
Think of a time you were certain of a fact but later realized you were heedless of another aspect. How does this lesson's distinction between inseparable and separable knowables call you to humility about the limits of your own understanding?

Objections and Final Refutation
Razi addresses objections and refutes the permitter's final argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised against Imam Razi's own argument and the final refutation of the permitter's position using the distinction between the concept of a thing alone and the concept of its relation.

Core Idea
Knowing a thing alone is not the same as knowing it in relation to another.

After presenting his own argument and surveying opinions, Imam Razi (or a commentator) addresses objections to his reasoning. These objections focus on the definition of science as attachment and the correct formulation of the heedlessness condition. Finally, a refutation of the permitter's example is given, arguing that the science of opposition is not the same as the science of either term alone.

Guided Path
1
Read from 'ويقال للمصنف' to the end
This passage contains objections and a final refutation. Try to follow the logical flow.
The objections are hypothetical criticisms of Razi's own position.
2
Understand the first objection
If science is defined as attachment, then the science of the aggregate includes its parts, so it attaches to two things, contradicting Razi's claim of impossibility.
This objection targets Razi's use of 'heedless of the other'.
3
Understand the second objection
Razi's argument used 'heedless of being a knower of the other' instead of 'heedless of the other'; the objection says the required is attachment to two objects, not to an object and the science of it.
4
Grasp the final refutation
The permitter's example of blackness and whiteness is refuted: the science of blackness alone is not the same as the science of blackness opposed to whiteness. Thus, what is known with ignorance of the other is not one of the two terms of the opposition.
This shows that the permitter's example actually supports Razi's position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the science of blackness opposed to whiteness is the same as the science of blackness alone.
Correction: Conceiving blackness alone is different from conceiving blackness as opposed to whiteness; the latter includes a relation.

Checkpoint
Why is the permitter's example of blackness and whiteness considered invalid in the final refutation?
A) Because blackness and whiteness are not two distinct things.
B) Because the science of opposition is not the same as the science of blackness alone.
C) Because the permitter used the wrong pair of colors.
D) Because the argument about heedlessness was applied incorrectly.

Key Takeaway
The relation of opposition makes the science of two things different from the science of each alone; thus the permitter's example does not undermine the possibility of a single science attaching to both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your knowledge of Allah's attributes, do you treat each attribute in isolation, or do you actively seek to understand how they relate to one another? This lesson reveals that the relationship itself is a distinct object of knowledge—what is one relationship between divine attributes you have never considered before? Commit to exploring it this week.
?
The permitter thought his example (knowing blackness without whiteness) settled the matter, but he missed a deeper truth. In your own spiritual life, have you ever settled for a superficial understanding, assuming you had the full picture, only to later realize you overlooked a crucial relation? Where might that be happening right now in your faith?
?
If knowledge of opposition is fundamentally different from knowledge of its terms, then how might your approach to understanding difficult theological topics—like predestination and free will—need to change? Are you willing to let go of comfortable separate answers and instead seek the relational knowledge that this passage demands?
