The Intellect Defined as Knowledge of Necessary and Impossible
Razi defines the intellect and argues it is self-evident universal knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the well-known definition of the intellect as the basis of religious obligation and the proof that it consists of self-evident universal knowledge.

Core Idea
The intellect is not sensory nor theoretical, but self-evident universal knowledge.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi begins by clarifying the intellect that is the basis of obligation. He first presents the well-known definition: the intellect is the knowledge of the necessity of obligations and the impossibility of impossibilities. He then provides a series of arguments to show that this knowledge must be self-evident and universal, not derived from the senses or from theoretical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the opening sentence where the intellect is defined as knowledge of necessary and impossible things. Note that it is presented as the 'well-known' view.
2
Follow the proof
Trace the argument: if intellect were not knowledge, they could separate; but a rational being always knows something; so intellect entails knowledge. Then, it cannot be sensory (animals have that) nor theoretical (circular).
3
Identify the conclusion
The author concludes that the intellect is a set of self-evident universal knowledges (علوم كلية بديهية).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : في بيان العقل الذي هو مناط التكليف ) المشهور أن العقل الذى هو مناط التكليف هو العلم بوجوب الواجبات واستحالة المستحيلات ، لأن العقل لو لم يكن من قبيل العلوم يصح انفكاك أحدهما عن الآخر لكن محال لاستحالة أن يوجد عاقل لا يعلم شيئا البتة أو عالم بجميع الأشياء ولا يكون اقلا ، وليس هو علما بالمحسوسات لحصوله في البهائم والمجانين فهو إذا علم بالأمور الكلية وليس ذلك من العلوم النظرية لأنها مشروطة بالعقل ، فلو كان العقل عبارة عنها لزم اشتراط الشيء بنفسه أوه و محال ، فهو إذاً عبارة عن علوم كلية بديهية وهو المطلوب . فقيل عليه : لم قالت إن المتغاير يقتضى جواز الا فكاك ، فإن الجوهر والعرض متلازمان وكذا العملة والمعلول سلناه لكن العقل قد ينفك من العلم كما في حق النائم أو اليقظان الذى لا يكون مستحضرا لشيء من وجوب الواجبات واستحالة المستحيلات، وعند هذا ظهر أن العقل غريزة يلزمها هذه العلوم البديهية عند سلامة صحة الحواس
أقول: قال أبو الحسن الأشعرى العقل علوم خاصة ، وزادت المعتزلة في العلوم التي يشتمل عليها العقل العلم بحسن الحسن وقبح القبيح، لأنهم يعدونه فى البديهيات ، وقال القاضى أبو بكر هو العلم بوجوب الواجبات واستحالة المستحيلات و مجارى العادات ، وقال المحاسى من أهل السنة هو غريزة يتوصل بها إلى المعرفة، وما ذهب إليه المصنف هو الصواب .
Question: On the explanation of the intellect that is the basis of obligation. The well-known view is that the intellect that is the basis of obligation is the knowledge of the necessity of obligations and the impossibility of impossibilities, because if the intellect were not of the class of knowledges, it would be possible for one to be separate from the other, but that is impossible because it is impossible for a rational being to exist who knows absolutely nothing, or for one who knows all things not to be rational. And it is not knowledge of sensory things because that occurs in beasts and the insane. So it is knowledge of universal matters, and that is not from theoretical sciences because they are conditioned by the intellect; so if the intellect were a phrase for them, it would necessitate conditioning a thing by itself, and that is impossible. So it is a phrase for self-evident universal knowledges, and that is what is sought. It was objected to him: Why did you say that distinct things necessitate the permissibility of separation? For substance and accident are inseparable, and likewise cause and effect. We grant that, but the intellect may be separate from knowledge, as in the case of the sleeper or the wakeful person who does not have present in mind any of the obligation of obligations or impossibility of impossibilities. And upon this, it became clear that the intellect is a faculty which entails these self-evident knowledges when the senses are sound. I say: Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari said: The intellect is specific sciences. The Mu'tazila added to the sciences that the intellect includes the knowledge of the goodness of good and the badness of bad, because they count it among the self-evident truths. Al-Qadi Abu Bakr said: It is the knowledge of the necessity of obligations, the impossibility of impossibilities, and the courses of habit. And al-Muhasibi from the Ahl al-Sunna said: It is a faculty by which one arrives at knowledge. And what the author (al-Razi) went to is the correct view.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the intellect (عقل) that is the basis of religious obligation (مناط التكليف). The author first states the well-known opinion: the intellect is the knowledge (علم) of the necessity of obligations (وجوب الواجبات) and the impossibility of impossibilities (استحالة المستحيلات). He provides a proof: if the intellect were not a type of knowledge, it could be separated from knowledge, but a rational being must know something (cannot be totally ignorant) and conversely one who knows all things cannot be non-rational — so intellect and knowledge are inseparable. Next, he argues that the intellect is not sensory knowledge (because animals and the insane have that). Therefore, it must be knowledge of universal matters (أمور كلية). But it cannot be theoretical sciences (علوم نظرية) because those are conditioned by the intellect itself, leading to circularity. Hence the intellect must be self-evident (بديهية) universal sciences. This is the thesis. An objection is then raised: distinct things are not always separable; substance and accident are inseparable, as are cause and effect. The author concedes the principle but points out that the intellect can be separate from actual knowledge, as in a sleeping person or someone who is not presently aware of those necessary truths. From this, it becomes clear that the intellect is a faculty (غريزة) that entails these self-evident knowledges when the senses are sound. The commentary (أقول) then reviews several scholarly opinions: al-Ash'ari said the intellect is specific sciences; the Mu'tazila added knowledge of good and evil among the sciences the intellect includes; al-Qadi Abu Bakr expanded it to include regularities (مجاري العادات); al-Muhasibi defined it as a faculty for attaining knowledge. The author sides with the view that the intellect is a faculty that produces self-evident knowledges.

Key Terms
عقل
مناط التكليف
علم
وجوب الواجبات
استحالة المستحيلات
علوم كلية بديهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The intellect is theoretical or sensory knowledge.
Correction: The author argues it is self-evident universal knowledge, not derived from senses or theory.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Razi reject the idea that the intellect is theoretical sciences?
A) Because animals have it.
B) Because it would cause circularity.
C) Because it is sensory.
D) Because it is a faculty.

Key Takeaway
The intellect that is the basis of obligation is a set of self-evident universal knowledges, not theoretical or sensory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your intellect is a set of self-evident universal truths that you already know, why do you still act as if you are not obligated to follow them?
?
Have you ever excused your disobedience by claiming you 'didn't know' something that your intellect already tells you is necessary or impossible? What does that reveal about your relationship with your own intellect?
?
The passage says the intellect is not theoretical knowledge acquired later. So what excuses have you been making for not acting on what you already know to be true? What will you change today to honor the intellect Allah gave you?

Objection, Refinement, and Scholarly Views on the Intellect
Razi addresses an objection and refines the definition, then reviews other scholars' definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that distinct things need not be separable, al-Razi's answer using the example of sleep, and how this leads to defining the intellect as a faculty, alongside a survey of other scholars' definitions.

Core Idea
The intellect is a faculty that produces self-evident knowledge, not the knowledge itself.

After establishing that the intellect is self-evident universal knowledge, a potential objection is raised: distinct things are not always separable. Al-Razi responds by pointing out that the intellect can be separate from actual knowledge in cases like sleep. This leads him to refine the definition: the intellect is a faculty (غريزة) that entails these knowledges when the senses are sound. He then surveys the views of al-Ash'ari, the Mu'tazila, al-Qadi Abu Bakr, and al-Muhasibi, affirming his own conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection: just because two things are distinct does not mean they can always be separated; substance and accident are inseparable.
2
Follow the answer
Note al-Razi's concession and his counterexample: a sleeper has intellect but no current awareness of necessary truths, so intellect and actual knowledge can separate.
3
See the refined definition
This leads to the conclusion that the intellect is a faculty (غريزة) that produces self-evident knowledge when senses are sound.
4
Survey the scholarly opinions
Read the review of al-Ash'ari, Mu'tazila, al-Qadi Abu Bakr, and al-Muhasibi. Understand how each defines the intellect differently.
5
Identify the author's position
Al-Razi sides with the view that the intellect is a faculty that entails self-evident knowledges.

Resolve as Decisive Will After Hesitation
Razi defines 'azm and explains its psychological mechanism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how resolve ('azm) is defined in kalam as a decisive will that emerges after a process of hesitation caused by conflicting motives.

Core Idea
Resolve is not raw will—it requires preponderance after hesitation.

In this passage, Razi defines 'azm (resolve) as a specific type of will that arises only after hesitation. He explains the mechanism of conflicting motives that lead either to bewilderment or to decisive resolve. This sets the stage for understanding human agency and divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of 'azm
Carefully read the Arabic sentence defining resolve as decisive will after hesitation.
Notice the phrase 'ba'da al-taraddudi fihi' — after hesitation about it.
2
Understand the process of hesitation
Razi explains that hesitation arises from conflicting motives (rational opinions, desires, aversions). Reflect on this cause.
3
Note the outcome: preponderance leads to resolve
If no side preponderates, bewilderment; if preponderance occurs, resolve. This logical flow is key.
This shows the rational basis of resolve.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : العزم عبارة عن إرادة جازمة بعد التردد فيه ، والمحبة عبارة عن الإرادة لكنها من الله تعالى فى حق العبد إرادة الثواب ، ومن العبد فى حق الله تعالى إرادة الطاعة والرضا ، قيل إنه الإرادة وقيل إنه ترك الاعتراض . أقول : التردد المذكور يحصل من الدواعي المختلفة المنبعثة عن الآراء العقلية وعن الشهوات والنفرات المتخالفة ، فإن لم يوجد ترجيح لطرف حصل التحير وإن وجد حصل العزم، والمحبة تقع باشتراك الاسم على إرادة هو مبدأ فعل وهو الذى نسبه إلى إرادة الثواب أو الطاعة وعلى تصور كمال من لذة أو منفعة أو مشاكلة كحبة العاشق لمعشوقه والمنعم عليه المنعمه والوالد لولده و أما محبة الله سبحانه وتعالى عند العارفين فهو لتصور الكمال المطلق فيه والرضاء . قال أبو الحسن الأشعرى : إنه إرادة إكرام المؤمنين ومثوبتهم على التأييد وهذا من الله تعالى ، وأما من العبد فهو ترك الاعتراض، والرحمة قيل هى النعمة وقال أبو الحسن هي إرادة الإنعام، والولاية إرادة الإكرام والتوفيق ، والبغض والعداوة إرادة الإهانة ، والطرد والتعذيب والسخط إرادة التعذيب، والاختيار عند أبى الحسن هو الإرادة واختار له أى فعل به خيراً ، والمشيئة هى الإرادة ، والكرامية يفرقون بينهما .
Issue: Resolve is an expression of decisive will after hesitation about it. And love is an expression of will, but from God Almighty in relation to the servant it is will of reward, and from the servant in relation to God Almighty it is will of obedience and satisfaction. Some said it is will, and some said it is leaving objection. I say: The mentioned hesitation occurs from different motives arising from rational opinions and from conflicting desires and aversions. If there is no preponderance for a side, bewilderment occurs; if there is, resolve occurs. Love occurs by homonymy: (1) a will that is the principle of action, which is attributed to will of reward or obedience; (2) a conception of perfection from pleasure, benefit, or similarity, like the love of a lover for his beloved, the benefactor for the one he has favored, and the parent for his child. As for love of God Almighty according to the gnostics, it is for the conception of absolute perfection in Him and satisfaction. Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari said: It is will of honoring believers and rewarding them perpetually—this is from God Almighty; as for from the servant, it is leaving objection. Mercy: some said it is blessing, and Abu al-Hasan said it is will of bestowing favor. Loyalty (al-wilaya) is will of honoring and granting success. Hatred and enmity are will of humiliating. Expulsion, punishment, and wrath are will of punishing. Choice according to Abu al-Hasan is will, and he chose for it that he acts by it good. The mashiyya is will, and the Karramiyya differentiate between them.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage defines two key terms: 'azm (resolve) and mahabbah (love). Resolve is a decisive will after hesitation. The hesitation comes from conflicting motives, and if no side is preponderant, one remains bewildered; if preponderance occurs, resolve results. Love is homonymous: it can be a will that initiates action (like will of reward or obedience) or a conception of perfection (like love of a beloved). Love of God is the conception of absolute perfection. Razi then reports Ash'ari's views: love from God is will to honor and reward believers; from the servant it is leaving objection (tark al-i'tirad). Other related terms are defined: mercy, loyalty, hatred, etc., all as specific types of will. The Karramiyya distinguish between mashiyya and irada, while Ash'ari equates them.

Key Terms
العزم
التردد
الدواعي
التحير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Resolve is simply any strong will without prior doubt.
Correction: The passage specifies that resolve occurs after hesitation and requires preponderance; otherwise it is just bewilderment.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if no preponderance (tarjih) occurs among conflicting motives?
A) Resolve is formed
B) Bewilderment (tahayyur) occurs
C) Will is automatically decisive
D) The hesitation disappears

Key Takeaway
Resolve is a decisive will that results from preponderance after hesitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hesitate between right and wrong, what motives pull you? Which one eventually outweighs? Have you let appetite dominate reason today? Will you seek tarjih for good?
?
Consider a recent decision you made. Did it involve genuine hesitation or was it just impulsive? Are you forming resolve based on preponderance or whims? How can you train yourself to hesitate rightly?
?
Razi says resolve only comes after hesitation. Do you avoid hesitation because it's uncomfortable? Could your hasty 'resolves' actually lack true resolve? Commit to one area where you will allow proper deliberation.

Love as Will and Conception of Perfection
Razi defines mahabbah (love) with two homonymous meanings and reports Ash'ari's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of love (mahabbah) in kalam: as a type of will (irada) and as a conception of perfection, and how these apply to God and humans.

Core Idea
Love is not a single emotion but a term with multiple rational meanings.

Razi explains that love (mahabbah) is used homonymously. One meaning is as a will (irada) that is the principle of action, attributed differently to God and humans. Another meaning is as a conception of perfection, like the love of a lover for his beloved. This distinction is crucial for understanding divine love and human love.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of love as will
Razi begins by stating that love is an expression of will, but specifies different applications: from God toward servant (will of reward) and from servant toward God (will of obedience and satisfaction).
Also note that some say love is simply will, others say it is 'leaving objection'.
2
Identify the two homonymous meanings
Razi then clarifies that love occurs by homonymy (ishtirak al-ism): (1) a will that is the principle of action, (2) a conception of perfection (pleasure, benefit, similarity).
Think of the second meaning as an intellectual apprehension, not a volition.
3
Understand divine love according to gnostics
For the gnostics, love of God is the conception of absolute perfection in Him and satisfaction.
4
Note the Ash'ari view on love
Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari defines love from God as will to honor and reward believers perpetually, and from the servant as leaving objection (tark al-i'tirad).
This ties love to the concept of submission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : العزم عبارة عن إرادة جازمة بعد التردد فيه ، والمحبة عبارة عن الإرادة لكنها من الله تعالى فى حق العبد إرادة الثواب ، ومن العبد فى حق الله تعالى إرادة الطاعة والرضا ، قيل إنه الإرادة وقيل إنه ترك الاعتراض . أقول : التردد المذكور يحصل من الدواعي المختلفة المنبعثة عن الآراء العقلية وعن الشهوات والنفرات المتخالفة ، فإن لم يوجد ترجيح لطرف حصل التحير وإن وجد حصل العزم، والمحبة تقع باشتراك الاسم على إرادة هو مبدأ فعل وهو الذى نسبه إلى إرادة الثواب أو الطاعة وعلى تصور كمال من لذة أو منفعة أو مشاكلة كحبة العاشق لمعشوقه والمنعم عليه المنعمه والوالد لولده و أما محبة الله سبحانه وتعالى عند العارفين فهو لتصور الكمال المطلق فيه والرضاء . قال أبو الحسن الأشعرى : إنه إرادة إكرام المؤمنين ومثوبتهم على التأييد وهذا من الله تعالى ، وأما من العبد فهو ترك الاعتراض، والرحمة قيل هى النعمة وقال أبو الحسن هي إرادة الإنعام، والولاية إرادة الإكرام والتوفيق ، والبغض والعداوة إرادة الإهانة ، والطرد والتعذيب والسخط إرادة التعذيب، والاختيار عند أبى الحسن هو الإرادة واختار له أى فعل به خيراً ، والمشيئة هى الإرادة ، والكرامية يفرقون بينهما .
Issue: Resolve is an expression of decisive will after hesitation about it. And love is an expression of will, but from God Almighty in relation to the servant it is will of reward, and from the servant in relation to God Almighty it is will of obedience and satisfaction. Some said it is will, and some said it is leaving objection. I say: The mentioned hesitation occurs from different motives arising from rational opinions and from conflicting desires and aversions. If there is no preponderance for a side, bewilderment occurs; if there is, resolve occurs. Love occurs by homonymy: (1) a will that is the principle of action, which is attributed to will of reward or obedience; (2) a conception of perfection from pleasure, benefit, or similarity, like the love of a lover for his beloved, the benefactor for the one he has favored, and the parent for his child. As for love of God Almighty according to the gnostics, it is for the conception of absolute perfection in Him and satisfaction. Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari said: It is will of honoring believers and rewarding them perpetually—this is from God Almighty; as for from the servant, it is leaving objection. Mercy: some said it is blessing, and Abu al-Hasan said it is will of bestowing favor. Loyalty (al-wilaya) is will of honoring and granting success. Hatred and enmity are will of humiliating. Expulsion, punishment, and wrath are will of punishing. Choice according to Abu al-Hasan is will, and he chose for it that he acts by it good. The mashiyya is will, and the Karramiyya differentiate between them.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage defines two key terms: 'azm (resolve) and mahabbah (love). Resolve is a decisive will after hesitation. The hesitation comes from conflicting motives, and if no side is preponderant, one remains bewildered; if preponderance occurs, resolve results. Love is homonymous: it can be a will that initiates action (like will of reward or obedience) or a conception of perfection (like love of a beloved). Love of God is the conception of absolute perfection. Razi then reports Ash'ari's views: love from God is will to honor and reward believers; from the servant it is leaving objection (tark al-i'tirad). Other related terms are defined: mercy, loyalty, hatred, etc., all as specific types of will. The Karramiyya distinguish between mashiyya and irada, while Ash'ari equates them.

Key Terms
المحبة
الإرادة
الرضا
ترك الاعتراض
تصور الكمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Love is purely an emotion, not a rational concept.
Correction: The passage defines love homonymously as either a type of will (irada) or a conception (tasawwur) of perfection, both rational categories.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two homonymous meanings of love (mahabbah)?
A) Will and conception of perfection
B) Will and emotion
C) Obedience and satisfaction
D) Reward and punishment

Key Takeaway
Love in kalam has two homonymous meanings: as a will (irada) and as a conception of perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say you love Allah, is your love primarily an emotion or a will that drives you to obey? Which meaning of love does your life reflect right now? Commit to one action today that transforms your love from mere feeling into willing obedience.
?
The gnostics conceive of God as absolute perfection. When you think of Allah, do you focus on His attributes or on your own desires? What would change if you truly saw Him as perfect? Resolve to ponder one of His beautiful names daily until it shapes your actions.
?
Ash'ari defines human love as leaving objection to God's decrees. Is there a decree of Allah in your life that you are still objecting to in your heart? Pray for the strength to truly submit and abandon that objection today.

Other Kalam Terms: Mercy, Loyalty, Hatred, and Choice
Razi defines mercy, loyalty, hatred, punishment, choice, and mashiyya as types of will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how several key kalam terms (mercy, loyalty, hatred, choice, mashiyya) are defined as specific types of will (irada) according to Imam Razi and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari.

Core Idea
In classical kalam, many attributes are reducible to will.

In the remainder of the passage, Razi defines a series of terms—mercy, loyalty, hatred, etc.—by relating them to will. He also notes a disagreement between Ash'ari and the Karramiyya over the relationship between mashiyya and irada. This shows the systematic nature of kalam in defining all divine attributes in terms of a few core concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions from mercy to mashiyya
Read the Arabic carefully: each term is defined either as a specific 'irada' (will of something) or with a different opinion.
Notice the pattern 'iradat' + object.
2
Observe the pattern: most are 'iradat'
Mercy, loyalty, hatred, punishment are all defined as 'iradat' (will of) something. This illustrates the centrality of will.
3
Note the Ash'ari-Karrami difference on mashiyya
Ash'ari equates mashiyya and irada; the Karramiyya distinguish between them. This is a point of kalam disagreement.
Think about why they might distinguish.

Definition and Proof of Speech of the Soul (Kalam al-Nafs)
Al-Razi defines kalam al-nafs and distinguishes it from other attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari concept of kalam al-nafs as a distinct divine attribute, and how al-Razi defines and proves its independence from will, imagination, and other attributes.

Core Idea
Speech of the soul is a real, eternal attribute of Allah, distinct from will and imagination.

In this passage, al-Razi introduces the concept of kalam al-nafs (speech of the soul), a key Ash'ari doctrine that affirms an eternal, non-sound, non-letter speech of Allah. He argues that command, prohibition, and information are independent meanings that are not reducible to will, imagination, or other attributes. This lays the foundation for understanding how Allah speaks eternally without created letters or sounds.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that al-Razi begins by stating that only the Ash'aris affirm this concept.
Pay attention to the terms: أمر, نهي, خبر.
2
Identify the arguments against reducing kalam to other attributes
The passage rejects identification with imagination of letters, with will, and with knowledge/power/life. Find each rejection and the reason given.
The rejection of will is the most detailed.
3
Understand the conclusion
After eliminating alternatives, al-Razi concludes that there must be a single unique entity, kalam al-nafs.
This is an example of positive argument by elimination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها كلام النفس ولم يقل به أحد إلا أصحابنا قالوا الأمر والنهي والخير أمور معقولة يعبر عن كل واحد منها في كل لغة بلفظة أخرى فهى معان متغايرة وليس عبارة عن تخيل الحروف لأن تخيلها تابع لها ومختلف باختلافها وهذه الماهيات لا تختلف البتة وليس الأمر عبارة عن الإرادة لأن الله تعالى قد يأمر بما لا يريد ويريد ما لا يأمر به وظاهر أنه ليس عبارة عن العلم والقدرة والحياة فلا بد من نوع واحد
And among them is speech of the soul. No one held it except our companions. They said: command, prohibition, and good [inform] are intelligible things, each of which is expressed in every language by another word, so they are distinct meanings. It is not an expression of imagining letters, because their imagining is dependent on them and varies with their variation, while these essences do not vary at all. Command is not an expression of will, because Allah may command what He does not will and will what He does not command. And it is apparent that it is not an expression of knowledge, power, or life, so there must be a single type.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage defines kalam al-nafs (speech of the soul) as a distinct category within divine attributes. Al-Razi begins by stating that only the Ash'aris (our companions) affirm this concept. He explains that command, prohibition, and information are intelligible meanings that transcend language; they are not the same as the mental images of letters, which vary by language. He rejects equivalence with will because Allah can command what He does not will (e.g., testing the disobedient) and will what He does not command (e.g., natural events). He also excludes identification with knowledge, power, or life, concluding that there must be a unique entity called kalam al-nafs.

Key Terms
كلام النفس
أمر
نهي
خبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think kalam al-nafs is the same as the mental image of letters or words.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that the meanings (command, prohibition, information) are invariant across languages, while mental images of letters vary, so they cannot be the same.

Checkpoint
What proof does al-Razi give that speech of the soul is not identical to will?
A) Allah may command what He does not will.
B) Command and will are opposites.
C) Will is eternal whereas command is temporal.
D) Speech is composed of letters.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi proves the existence of kalam al-nafs as a divine attribute by eliminating the alternatives and showing it is a distinct, eternal, non-created speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a command from Allah, do you assume He must also will it in your life? How does this lesson challenge your assumption, and what does that mean for how you receive both commands and trials you did not want?
?
Al-Razi argues that command, prohibition, and information are unchanging essences, not dependent on language. If Allah's speech to you is not bound by any human language, how does that affect the way you approach the Qur'an in translation or in Arabic? What would change if you truly believed the meaning is eternal regardless of the words?
?
You have likely at times used your tongue to command, prohibit, or inform. In what ways have you confused your own speech (which is tied to your will and imagination) with the eternal, non-varying speech of Allah? How can you begin to align your speech more with His, not just in content but in the underlying intent?

Perceptions Distinct from Knowledge: The Case of Sight
Razi defines perceptions as distinct from knowledge, argues for sight as non-knowledge, and introduces the debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that perceptions are distinct from knowledge, how the debate on sight illustrates this, and why theologians must refute the philosophers' view to affirm God's sight.

Core Idea
Perception and knowledge are not the same, as shown by the example of sight.

Razi begins by distinguishing perceptions from knowledge, arguing that sight is a clear example of a perception that is not identical to knowledge. He then presents the philosophers' claim that sight reduces to a physical impression in the eye, and explains why theologians must refute this to prove that God sees. This lesson unpacks these arguments and the underlying classification of perceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms and the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the example of seeing and then being absent from the object.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage opens with 'perceptions are other than knowledge'. Find the evidence Razi provides for this claim.
3
Understand the proof from sight
Razi uses the example of sight: we see an object, then we are absent from it, yet knowledge of it remains. The distinction between the two states shows that sight is a perception distinct from knowledge.
This refutes the idea that perception and knowledge are identical.
4
Recognize the opposing view
The philosophers, al-Kummi, and Abu al-Husayn claimed that sight is merely the impression of the image on the pupil. Understand why this view is problematic for the theologians.
If sight is a physical effect, then God cannot have sight without a body.
5
Note the additional classifications
Razi mentions that some scholars add perception of pain/pleasure, and others say every perception is a type of knowledge. Consider how these relate to the main argument.
These are supplementary points that enrich the discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها الإدراكات وهي غير العلم، لأنا نبصر الشيء ثم نغيب عنه فنجد التفرقة بين الحالتين مع حصول العلم فيهما، فالإبصار غير العلم. لكن الفلاسفة والكمي وأبو الحسين زعموا أنه عائد إلى تأثر الحدقة بصورة المرئي. والمتكلمون محتاجون إلى القدح في هذا الاحتمال ليمكنهم بيان أنه تعالى بصير. (مسألة: اختلفوا في الإبصار) بصير.
أقول: قالوا الإدراكات خمسة هي الحواس، وزاد القاضي أبو بكر فيها إدراك الألم واللذة، وقوم جعلوها علوما خاصة، فقالوا كل إدراك علم وليس كل علم إدراكا. والقول بأن الإبصار مؤثر في الحدقة خاص بالآلة، وليس يبعد أن يكون في غيره على وجه آخر كما في الإرادة، فإنها في العبد بخلاف ما نثبته من يبصر به الله تعالى.
Among them are perceptions, and they are other than knowledge, because we see a thing and then we are absent from it, and we find a distinction between the two states while knowledge is present in both, so sight is other than knowledge. But the philosophers, al-Kummi, and Abu al-Husayn claimed that it [sight] reverts to the impression of the pupil by the image of the seen object. The theologians need to invalidate this possibility in order to be able to prove that He, exalted, is seeing. (Issue: They differed concerning sight.) Seeing.
I say: They said that perceptions are five, which are the senses, and al-Qadi Abu Bakr added to them the perception of pain and pleasure, and some people made them specific sciences, saying that every perception is knowledge but not every knowledge is perception. And the statement that sight is an effect in the pupil is specific to the instrument, and it is not improbable that it be in other than it in another way, as in the case of will, for it is in the servant contrary to what we affirm of the one who sees by God, exalted.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage defines perceptions (al-idrākāt) and distinguishes them from knowledge (al-ʿilm). The author argues that sight (al-ibṣār) is not identical to knowledge, using the example of seeing an object and then being absent from it: we can distinguish between the two states even though knowledge (of the object) remains. The philosophers, al-Kummi, and Abu al-Husayn claimed that sight reduces to the eye being affected by the image of the seen object. The theologians must refute this to establish that God sees. The commentator adds that perceptions are the five senses, with some scholars adding pain and pleasure. Some hold that every perception is a type of knowledge but not every knowledge is a perception. The argument that sight is an effect in the pupil applies only to bodily instruments; it is not improbable that God's seeing is different, just as His will differs from human will.

Key Terms
الإدراكات
العلم
الإبصار
الحواس
الحدقة
الانطباع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Perception and knowledge are the same thing.
Correction: The passage shows they are distinct because we can distinguish the state of seeing from the state of being absent, even though knowledge remains.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between perception (idrāk) and knowledge (ʿilm)?
A) They are identical.
B) Perception is a type of knowledge.
C) Knowledge is a type of perception.
D) They are distinct.

Key Takeaway
Perceptions are not identical to knowledge; the example of sight shows they are distinct, and this distinction is crucial for affirming God's sight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that perception and knowledge are distinct. Think of a time when you saw something but did not truly know it — perhaps you misjudged a person based on appearance. How does this lesson challenge you to go beyond surface-level perceptions in your relationships with others, especially those you are quick to judge?
?
The passage argues that sight is not merely a physical process, but a perception that can exist even in God. Does your daily life reflect a belief that God sees you in a way that is beyond human sight? When you sin in private, do you truly feel seen by the All-Seeing? If not, what would it take for you to internalize that reality and change your behavior?

The Three Modes of Smell Perception
Razi enumerates three possibilities for smell perception and identifies the weakest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's classification of the three proposed mechanisms of smell perception and his judgment on their plausibility.

Core Idea
Smell perception has three possible explanations, but only two are sound.

In this passage, Imam Razi examines the philosophical question of how we perceive smells. He presents three competing theories and evaluates them, showing his critical approach to natural philosophy. This lesson will walk through each theory and Razi's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the overall structure.
Notice the use of technical terms like 'الخيشرم' (nose) and 'القوة المدركة' (perceiving power).
2
Identify the three modes
Find the three possibilities Razi lists for smell perception. They are introduced by 'قد يكون' (it may be).
3
Understand Razi's evaluation
Note which mode Razi calls 'أضعف الاحتمالات' (the weakest). Read the parenthetical comment to see his reasoning.
The third mode is rejected because a power cannot attach to something outside its locus.
4
Reflect on the broader context
Notice that Razi ties this discussion to the general topic of 'أقسام الأعراض وأحكام الأعراض' (divisions and rulings of accidents).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : إدراك الشم) قد يكون بتكيف الهواء المتصل بالخيشرم بكيفية ذي الرائحة وقد يكون لانفصال أجزاء لطيفة منها ووصولها إلى خيشو منا كما فى التبخرات ، وقد يكون تعلق القوة المدركة بالرائحة وهي هناك وهذا أضعف الاحتمالات ، وأما إدراك الذوق فقد تقدم الكلام فيه ، فهذا إشارة مختصرة إلى أقسام الأعراض (۲) وأحكام الأعراض ( أقول : الوجهان الأولان موجودان في أشياء لا تنقض باحتباس رائحة وفي التبخيرات . والوجه الثالث بعيد فإن القوة لا تتعلق بغير محلها ولا تنتقل من محلها .
Issue: Perception of smell. It may be by the modification of the air connected to the nose with a quality of the odorous thing, and it may be by the separation of subtle parts of it and their arrival to our nose as in vapors, and it may be by the attachment of the perceiving power to the odor while it is there, and this is the weakest of the probabilities. As for the perception of taste, the discussion of it has preceded. So this is a brief indication of the divisions of accidents and the rulings of accidents. (I say: The first two modes are found in things that are not invalidated by the retention of smell, and in vapors. The third mode is remote, because the power does not attach to other than its locus and does not transfer from its locus.)
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a brief treatment of the perception of smell. Razi presents three possible explanations: first, the air adjacent to the nose takes on the quality of the odorous object; second, fine particles separate from the odoriferous body and travel to the nose (as in evaporation); third, the perceiving power (the soul) becomes attached to the odor while the odor remains in its place. Razi judges the third possibility to be the weakest. He then notes that the discussion of taste has already been covered. The note by Razi clarifies that the first two modes are actually found in real objects, such as when a smell lingers, and that they occur in vapors. He emphasizes that the third view is unlikely because a power cannot attach to something outside its locus nor can it move from its locus.

Key Terms
إدراك الشم
القوة المدركة
الأعراض
الخيشرم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think all three proposed modes are equally plausible for explaining smell perception.
Correction: Razi explicitly states that the third mode (direct attachment of the soul to the odor) is the weakest because a power cannot operate outside its locus.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, which mode of smell perception is the weakest?
A) Modification of the air
B) Separation of subtle parts
C) Attachment of the perceiving power to the odor
D) Direct contact with the odor

Key Takeaway
Razi's analysis shows that not all proposed explanations for sensory perception are equally valid; sound reasoning requires evaluating each against physical principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that your soul can directly perceive spiritual realities without any mediation, forgetting that your perceiving power is bound to its bodily locus? How does this lesson recalibrate your understanding of your own limitations?
?
Think of a belief you hold about your own nature—does it respect the principle that a power cannot attach to what is outside its locus? If not, will you abandon that belief today?
?
When you encounter a compelling explanation, do you test it against basic principles as Razi does, or do you accept it because it feels spiritual or intuitive? What explanation in your life right now needs that kind of scrutiny?

The First Meaning of One Accident in Two Loci: Refutation
The commentator introduces two meanings of 'one accident in two loci' and refutes the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of 'one accident in two loci' and the rational refutation of the first meaning.

Core Idea
An accident cannot be in two separate loci without contradiction.

The commentator begins by clarifying that the phrase 'one accident in two loci' can be understood in two distinct ways. The first meaning, which philosophical theologians often discuss, is refuted by a clear rational argument. Let's examine this argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the two meanings
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the two meanings defined at the beginning.
The first meaning is the one that will be refuted.
2
Identify the refutation of the first meaning
After defining the first meaning, the commentator provides a proof of its falsity. Find the proof.
The proof uses analogy and a direct argument about dependency.
3
Understand the direct argument
The direct argument shows that an accident would be both dependent and independent if it were in two loci. Grasp this contradiction.
This is the key logical move.
4
Reflect on why the second meaning is not refuted
Notice that the commentator only refutes the first meaning, leaving the second open. This sets up the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول: يفهم من كون العرض الواحد حالا في محلين معنيان: أحدهما أن العرض الواحد الحال في محل هو بعينه حال في الآخر، والثاني أن العرض الواحد حال في مجموع شيئين صارا باجتماعهما محلا واحدا له. والأول باطل بما قاله، فإنه قاس العرض على الجسم الممتنع كونه في مكانين، ولو ذلك الفيل يمتنع اجتماع عرضين في محل واحد قياسا على امتناع الجسمين في مكان واحد، فإن اجتماع الأعراض الكثيرة في محل واحد كالسواد والحركة والتأليف والحياة مما لا يدفعه أحد، والدليل على بطلانه أن العرض محتاج في وجوده إلى المحل الذي هو فيه، ولو أمكن حلوله في محلين ثبت استغناء كل واحد منهما عن الآخر فيكون محتاجا إلى كل واحد منهما ومستغنيا عنه معا وهو باطل. والثاني لم تقم حجة على امتناعه والفلاسفة يقولون بقيام العرض الواحد بمحل ينقسم إلى أجزاء كثيرة كالوحدة بالعشرة الواحدة، والتثليث مجموع الأضلاع المثلثة المحيطة سطح، والحياة بنية متجزئة إلى أعضاء. وأبو هاشم إنما قال بقيام تأليف واحد بجوهرين لأن عدم انفكاك المؤلف عنهما دون المتجاورين محتاج إلى علة، ولو قام بكل واحد منهما تلك العلة يتعذر انفكاكهما، ولم يقل بقيامه بما فوق الاثنين، لأن التأليف لو قام مثلا بثلاثة جواهر ثم أزيل واحد منها من الاجتماع بالباقيين وجب انعدام التأليف لانعدام محله فلا يبقى الباقيان، وذلك بخلاف ما عليه الوجود. وقال بعد إبطال قيام العرض الواحد بمحلين في جواب أبي هاشم: إن إحالة صعوبة التفكيك على الفاعل المختار بأن يلتصق أحدهما بالآخر أولى من التزام جواز حلول العرض في محلين.
I say: Two meanings are understood from 'one accident being present in two loci': one is that the one accident present in a locus is the very same as that present in the other; the second is that the one accident is present in the sum of two things that have become, by their conjunction, one locus for it. The first is false by what he said, for he analogized the accident to the body whose existence in two places is impossible; likewise, it is impossible for two accidents to coexist in one locus analogously to the impossibility of two bodies in one place. For the coexistence of many accidents in one locus, like blackness, motion, composition, and life, is something no one denies. The proof of its falsity: the accident is dependent in its existence on the locus in which it is; if it were possible for it to exist in two loci, then each locus would be independent of the other, so it would be dependent on each and independent of them simultaneously, which is false. As for the second, no proof has been established for its impossibility, and the philosophers affirm the subsistence of one accident in a locus that divides into many parts, like unity in ten individual things, triangle as the sum of three sides enclosing a surface, and life in a structure divisible into organs. Abu Hashim only claimed that one composition subsists in two substances because the non-separation of the composite from them, unlike adjacent things, requires a cause; if that cause were in each one separately, their separation would be impossible. And he did not claim its subsistence in more than two, because if composition, for example, subsisted in three substances and then one was removed from association with the other two, the composition would necessarily cease to exist due to the annihilation of its locus, so the other two would not remain, contrary to what exists. And he said after disproving the subsistence of one accident in two loci in response to Abu Hashim: 'Attributing the difficulty of separation to the free agent by making one adhere to the other is more appropriate than accepting the permissibility of an accident existing in two loci.'
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a commentator's explanation of the previous issue, distinguishing two meanings of 'one accident existing in two loci'. The first meaning is that the very same accident present in one locus is present in the other; this is refuted by analogy to a body being in two places, and more directly by showing that an accident depends on its locus, so if it were in two loci it would be both dependent on each and independent—a contradiction. The second meaning is that the accident is present in a combined locus made of two things; the commentator notes that no proof has been given against this, and the philosophers actually accept it, giving examples like unity in ten things, triangle as a single relationship among three sides, and life as a single accident in a divisible body. Abu Hashim's specific claim about composition in two substances is then explained: composition requires a cause for its inseparability, and if that cause were in each substance separately, they could not separate; but he restricts it to two because if it were in three and one is removed, the composition ceases. The passage concludes with a statement that it is better to refer the difficulty of separating two composite bodies to the free agent rather than to accept the possibility of one accident in two loci.

Key Terms
العرض الواحد
المعنيان
الاستغناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that one accident can be literally present in two separate locations at once.
Correction: The passage shows this leads to contradiction because the accident would need to be both dependent on and independent of each locus.

Checkpoint
What is the direct rational proof against the first meaning?
A) An accident cannot be in two loci because it would be like a body in two places
B) An accident would be both dependent and independent if in two loci
C) Two accidents cannot coexist in one locus
D) The Quran prohibits it

Key Takeaway
The first meaning of 'one accident in two loci' is logically impossible due to the contradiction of simultaneous dependence and independence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson exposes a logical contradiction in trying to place one accident in two separate loci. Consider your own life: are there areas where you try to derive your identity or worth from two separate, independent sources? Does this lead to internal contradiction? What must you do to center your identity on the One who alone is sufficient?
?
The commentator uses a rational argument to refute a seemingly plausible idea. Have you ever held a belief about your faith that seemed reasonable but, when examined, led to a contradiction? Are you willing to subject your own cherished assumptions to the same rigorous logic?
?
The lesson shows that an accident's dependence on one locus is necessary. In your spiritual life, do you treat your connection to God as the sole locus of your existence, or do you rely on multiple, independent sources of meaning? How can you ensure your dependence is singular and consistent?

The Second Meaning of One Accident in Two Loci: Possibility and Application
The commentator discusses the second meaning, citing examples and explaining Abu Hashim's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the second meaning of 'one accident in two loci' is not refuted, and how it applies to examples like unity, triangle, and life, as well as Abu Hashim's view on composition.

Core Idea
The second meaning of 'one accident in two loci' is possible and the philosophers affirm it.

Having refuted the first meaning, the commentator now turns to the second meaning: an accident existing in a combined locus made of two things. He notes that no proof has been given against it, and that philosophers accept it. He then discusses Abu Hashim's specific claim about composition and explains the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the second meaning
Read the Arabic and translation from 'Wal-thani...' to the end. Notice the examples given.
These examples are meant to show that the second meaning is actually accepted.
2
Identify the examples from the philosophers
The commentator lists unity, triangle, and life as instances of one accident in a divisible locus. Understand each example.
These are not controversial; they illustrate the concept.
3
Understand Abu Hashim's position on composition
Abu Hashim claimed that composition (ta'lif) subsists in two substances. The commentator explains why he limited it to two and not more.
This is a more specific theological argument.
4
Reflect on the final statement
The passage ends with an alternative explanation: attributing the inseparability to the free agent. Consider how this relates to the issue.

Ibn Sina's Hylomorphism: Body as Matter and Form
Razi presents Ibn Sina's hylomorphism and its proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's claim that every body is composed of matter and form, and the argument he gives from divisibility.

Core Idea
Every body is a composite of a bearer (matter) and its attribute (form).

This passage presents the Avicennan doctrine of hylomorphism, which holds that the physical body is not a simple substance but a composite of two principles: matter (الهيولى) and form (الصورة). In this view, the property of occupying space (التحيز) is the form, and its substrate is matter. Ibn Sina offers a philosophical argument, based on the denial of atomism, to establish this composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Notice the technical terms: هيولى, صورة, تحيز, اتصال, انفصال.
The passage is dense; don't worry about fully understanding it yet.
2
Identify the two components
The passage states that spatial occupation (التحيز) is the form, and its locus is matter (الهيولى). Understand that form is an attribute, matter is the substrate.
Matter is passive; form gives actuality.
3
Understand the argument
Ibn Sina argues: a body is one yet divisible. The capacity for division must have a substrate (matter), separate from the attribute of continuity (form), because continuity is lost upon division.
This argument assumes the denial of atomism (الجوهر الفرد).
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters for kalam: if body is composite, then it is contingent and requires a cause. Also contrast with atomist views.
This is a foundational argument in natural philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( مسألة: زعم ابن سينا أن الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة ) ومعناه أن التحيز صفة حالة في شيء. فالتحيز هو الصورة ومحله الهيولى، واحتج عليه بناء على نفي الجوهر الفرد بأن الجسم في نفسه واحد وهو قابل للانفصال، والقابل للشيء موجود مع الهيولى لا محالة والاتصال لا يبقى الانفصال فالقابل للانفصال شيء مغاير للاتصال.
(Issue: Ibn Sina claimed that the body is composed of matter and form). Its meaning is that spatial occupation is an attribute subsisting in something. So spatial occupation is the form, and its locus is matter. He argued for it based on the denial of the indivisible atom: because the body is in itself one and is capable of separation, and that which is capable of something exists with matter necessarily, and continuity does not remain upon separation, so that which is capable of separation is something other than continuity.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's doctrine of hylomorphism: every body is composed of matter (الهيولى) and form (الصورة). Razi explains that spatial occupation (التحيز) is the form, and its locus is matter. Ibn Sina's proof is based on rejecting atomism: a body is one yet divisible; that which is capable of division must have a substrate (matter) distinct from the property of continuity (the form). Since continuity is lost upon division, the capacity for division inheres in something else—matter. Thus, body is composite.

Key Terms
الهيولى
الصورة
التحيز
الاتصال
الانفصال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ibn Sina denies composition altogether; he actually affirms a deeper composition of matter and form.
Correction: Ibn Sina's hylomorphism holds that every body is a composite of matter and form, not a simple substance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the form (صورة) in Ibn Sina's view?
A) The material substrate.
B) Spatial occupation.
C) Divisibility.
D) The indivisible atom.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina argues that the body's divisibility proves that it is composed of matter (substrate) and form (spatial occupation).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Sina’s proof shows that your body is not simply what it appears: it is a union of a bearer (matter) and its attribute (spatial occupation). If your physical existence is an attribute of something deeper, then what is your true self? How does this affect your daily preoccupation with your bodily image and identity? Will you today treat your body as the core of who you are, or as a temporary form carried by a deeper reality?
?
The capacity for separation (الانفصال) is shown to be other than continuity. Your own self feels continuous, yet you know it will be separated by death. What substratum (هيولى) do you possess that will carry your identity through that separation? Are you nurturing that substratum—your soul and its connection to Allah—more than you nurture your body’s form? If not, what is one concrete change you can make today to prioritize the bearer over the attribute?
?
Ibn Sina’s argument relies on denying the atom (الجوهر الفرد). He refuses to reduce the body to indivisible particles. Do you reduce your own worth to indivisible moments of success or failure, or to your physical appearance? If not, what is the substrate that holds your worth independent of your achievements? How does this lesson challenge you to see yourself as more than a collection of accomplishments, and to ground your identity in something unchanging—your relationship with your Creator?

Essence of Body: Spatial Occupancy vs. Qualities
Razi refutes the claim that body essence is composed of accidental qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that the essence of body (spatial occupancy) is distinct from its accidental qualities, and why the view of Dirar and al-Najjar is false.

Core Idea
Spatial occupancy is the shared essence of bodies, distinct from their differentiating qualities.

Dirar and al-Najjar claimed that a body's essence is simply a bundle of qualities like color and taste. Razi counters with a logical argument based on the principle that what is shared cannot be what distinguishes. This lesson walks through that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
Read the passage and identify what Dirar and al-Najjar claimed about body essence.
They claimed the essence is composed of seven accidental qualities.
2
Grasp the argument
Notice Razi's key move: all bodies share spatial occupancy but differ in qualities. That shows spatial occupancy is a separate essence.
The principle 'what is shared is not what distinguishes' is fundamental.
3
Apply the principle
See how Razi uses the principle to refute the view. Reflect on why this argument is compelling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : زعم ضرار والنجار أن ماهية الجسم مركبة من لون وطعم ورائحة وحرارة وبرودة ورطوبة ويبوسة) وهو باطل لأن المتحيزات متساوية فى ماهية التحيز ومتباينة بألوانها وروائحها وطعومها وما به الاشتراك غير ما به الامتياز ، فالتحيز ماهية مغايرة لهذه الصفات
Proposition: Dirar and al-Najjar claimed that the essence (ماهية) of the body is composed of color, taste, smell, heat, cold, moisture, and dryness. And it is false, because things occupying space (المتحيزات) are equal in the essence of spatial occupancy (التحيز) and differ in their colors, smells, and tastes. That by which they participate is not that by which they are distinguished. Therefore, spatial occupancy is an essence different from these attributes.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents a proposition (مسألة) attributed to Dirar ibn Amr and al-Najjar (early Mu'tazilite or speculative theologians). They claim that the essence of a body is composed of accidental qualities: color, taste, smell, heat, cold, moisture, and dryness. Razi immediately declares this false. He provides a logical argument: all bodies that occupy space (المتحيزات) share in the essence of spatial occupancy (التحيز) but differ in their colors, smells, and tastes. Therefore, the shared element (spatial occupancy) is different from the differentiating elements (the qualities). Hence, spatial occupancy is a distinct essence from these qualities, so the body's essence cannot be composed of qualities alone. This argument uses the principle that the common factor cannot be the same as the distinguishing factor.

Key Terms
ماهية
تحيز
متحيزات
اشتراك
امتياز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The essence of a body is just the collection of its perceptible qualities.
Correction: Razi shows that spatial occupancy is a distinct essence that all bodies share, separate from differentiating qualities.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi consider the view of Dirar and al-Najjar false?
A) Because bodies are not composed of qualities
B) Because spatial occupancy is the same as qualities
C) Because the common factor (spatial occupancy) is different from the differentiating qualities
D) Because the qualities are not real

Key Takeaway
Spatial occupancy is the essence that all bodies share, and it is distinct from the accidental qualities that differentiate them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You see others through their qualities—their wealth, intelligence, or personality—but do you ever consider their essence as a soul created by Allah? How does this affect your judgment of them? What would it mean for you to look beyond qualities to the shared essence of servitude to Allah? Commit to treating one person today based on their essence rather than their outward qualities.
?
Think of something you deeply desire—a promotion, a relationship, a possession. Is your attachment to its accidental qualities (prestige, comfort, beauty) or to its essence (the purpose it serves in your deen)? What would happen if you stripped away the qualities? Would you still want it? This lesson demands you examine your priorities. What is one thing you can let go of or reorient today?
?
You often describe yourself by your qualities: 'I am smart,' 'I am kind,' 'I am successful.' But what is your essence beyond these labels? In the sight of Allah, it is your iman and taqwa. Are you building your essence or just decorating your qualities? What one change can you make today to focus on your essence rather than your qualities?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
8 min
Spiritual Substances: Definition and Critique
Definition and classification of spiritual substances with critical note.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of spiritual substances in Islamic theology, their classification according to philosophers, and the theological critique that not only philosophers but also Mu'tazilites affirmed non-extended substances.

Core Idea
Not just philosophers affirm spiritual substances; Mu'tazila did too.

This passage from Imam Razi's Muhassal defines spiritual substances as entities that are neither extended nor located in an extended thing. It then outlines the philosophers' classification into hyle, human souls, and celestial souls/intellects. A marginal note critically challenges the exclusivity of this classification, pointing out that Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi, a Mu'tazilite, also affirmed a non-extended human spirit.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic definition of spiritual substances and its translation. Note the key terms 'mutaḥayyizah' and 'ḥall'.
2
Identify the classification
List the three divisions mentioned: hyle (prime matter), human souls, and celestial souls/intellects. Notice how the author refers to earlier and later discussions.
The passage says hyle was discussed earlier and human souls will be discussed later.
3
Understand the critique
Read the marginal note (أقول) and identify the two criticisms: (1) the claim that only philosophers affirm non-extended substances is false because al-Balkhi also affirmed it, and (2) including hyle among spiritual substances is disputed, and (3) no proofs are given for celestial souls/intellects.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this critique shows that the definition of spiritual substances was not exclusive to philosophers but shared by some theologians.
This highlights the overlap between philosophical and theological categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجواهر الروحانية ) وهي التي لا تكون متحيزة ولا حالة في المتحيز وقد عرفت أن الفلاسفة هم القائلون بها وعرفت أقسامها فنقول أما الهيولى وقد سبق الكلام فيها وأما الأرواح البشرية فسيأتي القول فيها إن شاء الله تعالى وأما النفوس السماوية والعقول فهي الملائكة وقد تكلمنا على أدلتهم في إثباتها . أقول: بأن القائلين بالجواهر التي لا تكون متميزة ولا حالة فيه هم الفلاسفة فيه نظر لأن أبا القاسم البلخي من المعتزلة وأتباعه ذكروا أن الروح الإنساني جوهر ليس له صفة الميز وارد الهيولى ههنا على أنها من الجواهر الروحانية ليس بمرضى عند المائلين بها وأما النفوس السماوية والعقول فلم يتكلم عليها في هذا الكتاب مما يدل على إثباتها إنما أورد حكايات الجرمانية فيها فقط وذكرها عند قسمة الجوهر على رأى الفلاسفة بأسمائها فقط
Spiritual substances (al-jawāhir al-rūḥāniyyah): they are those which are not extended (mutaḥayyizah) nor located in an extended thing. You have known that the philosophers are the ones who affirm them, and you have known their divisions. So we say: As for hyle (hayūlā), discussion has preceded it; as for human souls, discussion will come later, God willing; and as for celestial souls and intellects, they are the angels, and we have discussed their proofs for affirming them. I say: The statement that those who affirm substances that are not extended nor located are the philosophers is questionable, because Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi from the Mu'tazila and his followers mentioned that the human spirit is a substance that does not have the attribute of extension. And the inclusion of hyle here as among the spiritual substances is not agreeable to those who incline to it. As for celestial souls and intellects, this book does not discuss proofs for their affirmation; it only mentions the stories of the Germans about them and mentioned them in the division of substance according to the philosophers' view only by name.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage defines spiritual substances (al-jawāhir al-rūḥāniyyah) as those that are neither extended (mutaḥayyizah) nor located in an extended thing. It then notes that philosophers affirm them and divides them into hyle (prime matter), human souls, and celestial souls/intellects (angels). The author states that hyle has been discussed earlier and human souls will be discussed later. A marginal note (أقول) criticizes this classification: it says that the claim that only philosophers affirm non-extended substances is questionable, because Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi, a Mu'tazilite, also affirmed that the human spirit is a non-extended substance. Moreover, including hyle among spiritual substances is not accepted by those who study them. Finally, the note observes that the book does not provide proofs for celestial souls and intellects; it only mentions them in the division of substance according to the philosophers.

Key Terms
الجواهر الروحانية
المتحيز
الهيولى
النفوس السماوية
العقول
الملائكة
أبو القاسم البلخي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only philosophers affirm the existence of spiritual substances (non-extended beings).
Correction: The Mu'tazilite theologian Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi also affirmed that the human spirit is a non-extended substance, showing that some theologians shared this view.

Checkpoint
According to the marginal note, who besides philosophers affirmed that the human spirit is a non-extended substance?
A) Al-Ash'ari
B) Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi
C) Ibn Sina
D) Al-Kabiri

Key Takeaway
Spiritual substances, defined as non-extended and non-located, were affirmed by philosophers and some theologians like al-Balkhi, and their classification into hyle, souls, and intellects was not without controversy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may have thought that philosophical terms like 'spiritual substances' belong only to philosophers. But this lesson shows that a Mu'tazilite theologian, al-Balkhi, also held this view. Have you ever dismissed a concept because you associated it with a group you disagreed with? What might God be calling you to reconsider with an open heart?
?
The note criticizes the inclusion of hyle among spiritual substances, showing that even scholars debate classifications. Think of a belief you hold firmly. Is it based on clear evidence from revelation or on the authority of a particular group? Are you willing to re-examine it if the truth demands it, even at the cost of pride?
?
This lesson defines spiritual substances as neither extended nor located. Your own soul is described as such a substance. How does this truth change how you live today? Are you treating your soul as something invisible and transcendent, or do you act as if only the material world matters?
